We Who Are About to Die...
The Red Page
According to Homer Lea, the author of
that remarkable book, The Valor of Ignor-
ance, the Dqy of Judgment has arrived for
the Saxon race. The Valor of Ignorance
showed the United States their inability to
prevent the Japanese from occupying their
western littoral, prophesied the defeat of
the American fleet by Japan, and assumed
Japanese impregnability on the Pacific.
Now, in his second bombshell, The Day of
the Saxon (Harper’s), he does for the
British race what he had done for the
United States. The day of the Saxon is
almost over. And in the coming night Aus-
tralia, with her white ideal, has no refuge
nor hope of succor. We are doomed; and
the hour has struck. So! Though Ilomer
Lea’s gigantic and terrible prophecy is open,
here and there, to criticism, the cumulative
effect of the work is so convincing that
his argument must be adequately stated.
But the book is one that every patriotic
Australian must himself read; a copy should
be among the treasured possessions of the
Minister for Defence.
The Scarlet Circle of the Saxon
The Saxon has marked around this earth,
as has no other befoi'e him, the scarlet circle
of his power. This thin, red Saxon line, so
thin with his numbers, so red with his blood,
was made possible only by his heroism and
racial fealty...-There has been no storm it
has not encountered, no pain it has not en-
dured ; no race it has not fought, and no
disease it has not contended with. The
Saxon line has been to, the earth a girdle
tragic and heroic, binding within itself all
the old and great places of the world.
It is not so much in the vastness of
British possessions that are found conditions
provocative of war as it is in its geographi-
cal distribution.
At the present time no great nation in
Europe 'or Asia can move on those radii
of expansion, as determined by natural law,
radii upon which they must move or fall
into decadence, without the prior destruction
of Saxon sovereignty. It is because of this
that we find in Europe more than 16,000,000
soldiers, and in Asia more than 3,000,000, to
break forth at some predetermined time,
singly or in coalition, and shatter the vast
yet fragile circle of British power.
The author draws a memorable parallel
between this great wall of Britain and the
Great Wall of China. The former is now
but “a monument to the dead that built it
and to a spirit that has all but departed.’’
In the Empire the militant spirit has be-
come of secondary consideration: “It is
now hardly more than that spirit of the
trade rat, redolent and satisfied in the accu-
mulation of that which is useless to national
and racial progression.”
There can be no retention of present
British sovereignty without the repression
of the territorial and political expansion of
other nations—a condition that must cul-
minate in war, one war if the Empire is
destroyed; a series if it is victorious....
The circle of Saxon Dominion must be
broken or the greatness of other nations
restricted. Their growth, their ideals and
aspirations must, on reaching it, stop; and
in that ■ stoppage, decline; in that going
down, perish. But this condition can only
result after long and terrible struggles, after
they have been hurled back from the Saxon
circle into their own small corner of the
world. Should, however, these nations not
be hurled back, then the circle is shattered,
and other nations assume supremacy, each
in its respective arc, over those dominions
of the Saxon race. In this epoch of war
upon which the Empire is about to enter,
hopes of peace are futile. . . . ; constitutions
and kings and gods are without avail, for
these are the old, old struggles that govern
the growth and dissolution of national life.
The Inevitability of War
The same causes and the identical means
that brought about the establishment of all
other nations produced in like manner the
British Empire. By wars and conquests, by
theft and intrigue, by the same brutal use
of physical power, was it put together piece
by piece... .The British Empire has been
made up from v the fragments of four great
maritime Powers; satrapies of petty
potentates and the wilderness of nameless
savages.... Now, after a century of rule,
of such* supremacy as mankind has never
heretofore known, the British Empire is con-
fronted, not by one, but by four Powers in
the eventual struggle for the suzerainty of
one-third of the world. Each of these
nations is proportionally better qualified to
wrest it from the Saxons than were they,
from the middle of the 16tli to the end of
the 18th century, capable of taking- it by
force of arms from Portugal, Spain, Holland
and France.
The foundation of Homer Lea’s arguments
are based in what he calls natural laws: the
book is full of them. He regards war as the
result of natural forces, which can be re-
duced like any other laws to definitions. The
actual causes of the outbreak of wars, he
rightly' says, are never the real causes. These
surface irritations that lead to the declara-
tion of war are the only matters that are
or ever could be submitted to arbitration.
But the causes of wars are to be found far
beneath these transient irritations; they lie
bound up in fhe causes of the growth and
decay of natiobs. Thus the war with Ger-
many may be declared on any pretext; but
it does not lie with any German statesman
to prevent the outbreak of war against Eng-
land the mofnent the economic-pressure upon
Germany reaches that point when it must
expand or decay.
Homer Lea states his two laws that gov-
ern in the future the outbreak of decisive
hostilities, as follows :
1. When the resources of one nation stand
in inverse ratio to its military power, and
the military capacity of its competitor is
proportionate to the needs of its race, then
war will ensue when the military decadence
of one and the economic necessities of the
other reach a known point.
2. When the exploitation of the natural
resources of a nation militarily weak is pro-
hibitive to nations militarily strong, war
will ensue when the economic needs of the
militant powers exceed their own natural
resources.
It is in these two principles that is found
the true source and terribleness of those wars
that must in due time fall upon the British
Empire.
When the convergence of two races reaches
the degree of acuteness now existing between
the Saxon and the Teuton, it invariably re-
sults in war. There is no instance in history
where the political relationships of two
races, approximating that which now
exists between the British and the German
Empires—where one exerts its utmost to
prepare for war, and the other, by evasion
and subterfuge, seeks its postponement—did
not result in one of those decisive wars
which mark with fatal invariability the end
of the uttmartial race.. . .
A battle is a,part of a war; war is a part
of peace. We cannot determine where one
ends and the other begins. A battle is but a
moment of violent human activity, and is the
result of innumerable other conditions and
activities that have preceded it in regular
sequence. So a w T ar is only a battle pro-
longed in time and space....To deny pre-
paration for war during peace is a greater
tolly than to refuse to prepare for battle
after war has begun.
A Mongrel Australia
One of the most pregnant predictions of
this remarkable book is specially applicable
to Australia and her white ideal. The prin-
ciple is thus stated :
In all countries rich in natural resources,
but deficient in inhabitants, the future popu-
lation consists, not of. the descendants of the
first inhabitants, but of those nationalities
whose source of supply is most abundant,
and whose mobility is greatest. With this
migration of mankind there goes on in the
new land a transmutation of racial ideals.
Taking the case of Canada, he finds that
the birth-rate of the present inhabitants can-
not have any deterrent effect upon those
floods that must inundate the Dominion.
“The population of unexploited territories
has nothing to do, now nor in the future,
with localised procreation of mankind. It
belongs to his treks, to those new Crusades
of races wherein the passion that drives
them onward is their hungers.” In the
present immigration to Canada he finds no
single element that would perpetuate the
Saxon race, or keep the future Canadians
imperial. We know that the United States
is no longer Anglo-Saxon. A million persons
entered the States last year, made up almost
entirely of races other than Anglo-Saxon;
and in a generation or two the States will
be in the control of a mongrel crowd in
which it will be difficult to find a Saxon.
Applying this reasoning to Australia, the
fact emerges, not mentioned by Homer Lea,
that we Australians will have no say in the
creation of the nation that will succeed us
and call itself Australian. At present we
can control the racial quality of our immi-
gration, but in the near future we will have
no power to prevent the influx of the over-
flowing nations. As the United States has
become mongrel, as Canada is becoming mon-
grel, so must Australia become mongrel. If
we manage to keep out the Asiatic, a possi-
bility that the author utterly disallows, we
shall have to see our predominantly Saxon
Australia swamped by a motley soup of all
nationalities, out of which there seems small
possibility of the Saxon, or the present Aus-
tralian, ideal emerging. So—the grim end of
White Australia ! But worse !
At some more or less definite time, which
may or may not be the present, the natural
movement toward Australasia by Asia will
begin. There are two tentative preventions,
one peaceful, the other military. The first
by populating these regions with Saxons and
white races. Second, by military power en-
forcing the prohibition of Asian immigra-
tion. The impossibility of the first prohibi-
tory measure is apparent, since the increase
of the Saxon population must go on in the
future, as in the past, by propagation and
voluntary immigration. Yet the relative dis-
proportion in numerical strength now exist-
ing between the white races .of Australasia
and the colored races of Asia will not de-
crease, but/will be augmented in a constantly
increasing ratio To have confidence,
therefore, in the ability of the Saxons to pre-
serve for themselves, through immigration or
propagation, Australasia and its environs is
only expressive of futility. In this belief as
found the apocalypse of the white man's
ignorance It is in the realisation that the
armaments and military knowledge of Asian
Empires are now equal to those of Austral-
asia that we become cognisant of the omin-
ous gulf that separates the 6,000,000 of the
Saxons in the South Pacific from the thou-
sand millions that surround them.... Home
defence for Australasia and Maoriland is a
military anomaly.
The Futility of Our Land Defence
While the Pacific colonies and dominions
can never possess alone that defensive capa-
city to prevent the seizure of their territories
by any Power which has gained control of
their seas, yet they constitute a source of
war, and become more constantly provocative
ot it as the Pacific becomes more and more
the foci of human struggle.... Australasia
is not fixed, in the sense of racial or national
security, upon the bed of its ocean. It is
fixed only so long as it is anchored to the
British Empire. .. .The efforts, now being
made for Australasian defence are so de-
limited that they form an immaterial factor
in the ultimate struggle for national sur-
vival. They belong to world conditions that
no longer exist.... Considering Australasia
as a sovereign nation, with its inherent mili-
tary responsibilities, what constitutes the
error of its defence? It is in the adoption
of a continental system to the protection of
insular territory.... The permanent defence
of Australasia is naval.
The author lays down the three cofiditions
that show our land defence futile :
1. The relative disproportion between the
attacking forces and the defenders: (a) Dis-
parity in population and wealth between
Australasia and the power capable of attack.
(6) Australasia is divided into seven dis-
tinct military spheres that are neither correl-
ative nor co-operative, (c) The defence of
the whole is determined by the struggle in
the strongest sphere, the population of this
region determining the defensive potentiality
of the whole.
2. Nine-tenths of the population live adja-
cent to the sea.
3. Retreating inland, instead of moving
toward the base of their resources, they move
away from them with the first retrograde
movement.
Suppose Australia attacked, we can
either concentrate all our forces “into one
sphere, say that of Sydney, and the enemy
can occupy the rest of the" continent. Or if
we don’t know where the enemy’s attack
will fall, and each sphere is confined for de-
fence to its own forces, each can be isolated
and destroyed in detail. Had we, of course,
the two straight lines making the sign of the
cross on the map—a possibility of which the
author takes do notice—this would not be
so easy. The seizure of Australia, he states,
is restricted to two strategic spheres, those
of which Sydney and Melbourne are the
bases. It is the geographical fatality that
has kept the population on the edge of the
continent that is the fatal weakness in our
defence.
Australia, while a continent, is also an
atoll; without the ocean, within the desert.
Both the sea and the desert are, as regards
military calculations, identical. To retreat
toward the ocean in control of the enemy is
disaster; to flee to the desert is death....
Once the defence of eastern Australia is
thrown westward of the Blue Mountains,
and the defence ; of southern Australia is
pushed north of the Australian Alps, organ-
ised defence in the form of armies will pass
into that final stage of inutile warfare,
guerilla and predatory, until, self-exhausted,
it reaches its end. What Nature gave the
Boers it has denied Australia.
So our defence, the temporary land de-
fence, requires numbers that, owing to the
segregation of our defensive spheres, must
be equal in each sphere to the total forces of
the enemy. Our forces cannot be considered
in the aggregate; and “the character, arma-
ment and training of Australian forces are
determinable, not by Australian legislation,
but by the character, armament and training
of the strongest probable enemy.” The per-
manent defence is naval. We require a fleet
equal to the fleet of the strongest maritime
Power in the Pacific.
This degree of naval power can never be
attained by Australasia. It is possible only
to a united British Empire.
Australia to Defend India
The Day of the Saxon lays stress upon the
paramount importance of India to the Em-
pire. The author holds that the loss of India
would be. the most vital blow given to the
Empire. Indeed, he suggests that an inva-
sion of England would be of less importance
to the Empire' than the conquest of the
brown country.
Its loss means primarily that there has
been made in the circle of British dominion
a gap so vast that all the blood and fire and
iron of the Saxon race cannot again bring
together its broken ends.
And with the loss of India the author
sees the immediate destruction of Australasia.
The whole question of the control of the
Pacific is, in his argument, bound up with the
retention of the brown triangle. The first
principle of Australasian defence is the
defence of India!
The Fatal Alliance
The author has not words strong enough
to condemn the Jap. Alliance. He terms it
one of the most potent factors in those forces
which tend to bring about the dissolution of
the Empire. The defeat of Russia by Japan,
only rendered possible by the Alliance, re-
sulted disastrously to the Saxon.
It was in the renewal of this alliance that
the Empire finally failed in its duty toward
its Pacific dominions to even a greater degree
than has Australasia failed in its subordina-
tion to Imperial interests and unity....A
second insular Power has been born to live
as it (the British Empire) has lived, and to
loot as it has looted the highways of the
sea.... Janan is stronger in the Pacific than
the British Empire is in the Atlantic....
The Alliance has made possible, in the full-
est sense of the term, the Japanese Empire.
It may result in giving over to her sover-
eignty one-third of the w0r1d.... By the time
this alliance comes to an end we will find
the Indian frontiers more vulnerable than
ever, the western marches of China occupied,
and Japan impregnable in the Pacific. . . .
Should the British Empire, following this
war (that forecasted between Japan and, the
United States) become involved i- Europe
or on the Indian frontier, her defeat would
result in the eventual inclusion of Austral-
asia under Japanese sovereignty.
The right alliance that should have been
entered into, he contends, was one between
the Empire and China, in order to pre-
serve the vital balance of power in the
Pacific. The natural allies are Japan and
Russia. And in the cables last week we
heard of the first step toward that consum-
mation. Here, at least, Homer Lea has
shown himself a true prophet. At all costs
England must rehabilitate China —a deca-
dent China means the expulsion of Saxon
power from the Pacific. But there is a
greater alliance looming—one which means
the dominance of the world. That is Ger-
many, Russia and Japan. The author shows
why this potent alliance will inevitably take
shape.
The Handicap of Naval Power
Spdce does not permit the examination of
the successive stages by which the Germans
have steadily progressed toward war with
the British Empire; nor is there space to in-
dicate the necessity for Britain to secure
the Low Countries from German dominance.
But mention must be made of the arguments
by which he shows that a victory of the
British fleet over that of Germany, owing
to Britain’s military inability to take the
offensive, would in the end be actually as
disastrous as a defeat. Victory can only be
gained by the use of armies. The wiping
out of the German fleet would merely post-
pone the inevitable, since it could have no
effect on the German dominance of Europe,
nor prevent the German march upoh India.
British “control of the sea” grows less and
less important as land intercommunications
progress. A British naval victory only-
transfers the theatre of war from the sea to
the land. Nor can British naval power
prevent Russian advance upon Persia and
India.
Nothing except the x-uin of the British
navy increases German sovereignty in
Europe more surely than the defeat of her
own fleets. Unless the British Empire is
able to take those military measures that
will i-esult in the destruction of German
capacity to wage war, then its naval vic-
tories are useless.
And Britain has deliberately chosen as the
theatre of war the North Sea, instead of
Germany. The importance of this error it
is impossible to elucidate here.
The Saxon’s One Chance
Ilis advice is for England to jump in now
and initiate the inevitable war by occupy-
ing the buffer nations against the Germans.
England seized Denmark in the beginning of
last century merely to prevent the employ-
ment of the Danish fleet by the French. Her
occupation of the Low Countries would be,
in his opinion, merely a territorial and not a
moral violation, and in this act he sees the
Empire’s one hope of postponing the inevit-
able.
The only chance for the Saxon race to
survive is through : (1) Military and naval
unification of the Empire; (2) Complete
separation of defence forces from the civil
government of the dominions; (3) Universal
and compulsory military service throughout
the Empire; (4) All armies organised as ex-
peditionary forces; (5) Size and distribu-
tion of Imperial armies determined by size
and distribution of its probable adversaries;
(6) Militancy and military power to be in-
creased automatically with each military
increment of the expanding nations; (7)
Centralisation —-not a confederacy, but mili-
tary unification.
And not one of these suggestions is likely
to be acted upon before the arrival of the
great war, or the simultaneous wars, or the
series of wars, that w'T burst the thin red
Saxon .ring into fragments. And the one
white race will disappear, and over its
world-scattered grave the conquerors will
paint some hieroglyphics which, being inter-
preted, will read: ‘'This was the race who
said that the time was -not yet ripe." And
at last a very tired sun will set over the
Empire that used to boast that it couldn’t.

Business, Robbery, etc.
Geelong keeps challenging Melbourne in the matter
of big industries. Adding another to its long list,
it has just opened cement works at Fyansford, two
or three miles out. These works, replacing some that
were small and out of date, can turn out 3000 casks
a week, or altogether 5000 tons more than Victoria
found it necessary to import last year. The cost of
the outfit was, it is said, something like £75,000,
and there is already talk of going still further.
“Nulla Bona”: Amongst the latest immigrants to
land in Australia is the Greater American Beef
Trust. For five years its agents have been trying to
get a foothold here through existing meat firms and
companies. In Argentina they were able to buy out
all the big freezing works, but here they failed. The
men in the Australian trade were chiefly the pioneers,
who had just finished a long, hard struggle to estab-
lish the business here and in Britain, and they were
loth to accept the tempting offers made by the
Trust’s ambassadors. So the Trust made a start on
its own account by buying 130 acres with a frontage
of nearly a quarter of a mile to the Brisbane River,
and on this area it is erecting a frozen meat export
depot bigger than anything outside the huge La Plata
Freezing works, which J " Trust bought from the
Australian and Argentine shareholders of the La
Plata Cold Storage Co. The grip of the American
Beef Trust on the meat business, from the grazier to
the consumer, is complete, not only in the protected
Yewnited States but in Freetrade Britain. The
rancher in the States is forced to take whatever the
Trust bids for his cattle, because there is no open
market and no competition. The consumer in Liver-
pool and London is forced to pay whatever price the
Trust demands, because the Trust controls the meat
import trade. If the colossus is not vigorously re-
pulsed at the very beginning, it will regulate both
the production and the export of Australian meat in
a few years, to the permanent injury of our farmers
and graziers, and to the serious hurt of the Aus-
tralian. The Beef Trust’s plan is to give high prices
for cattle at first, and so wipe out other buyers. At
the same time it sells meat to the public cheaper
than the local butchers, whom it eventually bears
into bankruptcy or alliance. Directly it has throttled
both buying and selling competition, and col-
lared the trade completely, it drops the price
of cattle and pulls up the price of meat. It
means ruin for tens of thousands of Australians if
this business gets a sound footing here, and no powers
are too despotic for the Government to use to strangle
it at the very outset.

Kalgoorlie Miner (W.A.) howls for itself:—

The dreadful vehicles trundle about their business
in broad daylight, so it will not be necessary to have
the ads. done in luminous paint. I wait with in-
terest to see whether the chocolate folk, or the ladies’
hat people, or the clericals, make the first snatch at
this rare chance of publicity.
When the gentleman who robs the sheep of his
winter overcoat asks for more pay than he used to
get, he will be glad to consider this remark of the hig
Dalgety firm, in its just-issued annual sheep report:
“Though there were many more sheep to shear (say)
20 years ago, the clip shorn during the past season
eclipses all previous records.” Dalgety’s, who ought
to know, say that the weight cut per sheep is now
greater in Australia than in any other country of
the world, “though the fleece may not be so fine in
quality as formerly”—which last remark probably
has some connection with the disastrous export of
so many of Australia’s finest stud sheep to S’Africa.
“F.J.1.”: It is a harder matter than it looks to
find out exactly how much money Australia owes
abroad, collectively and individually. Here is the
result of my delving, submitted with the warning that
it cannot possibly be correct. These figures represent
“capital subscribed,” which often consists of two
parts paper, two parts water, and one part cash,
especially when the company has been floated in
London. Many companies on the lists were floated
only to sink, so this table does not include, for in-
stance, the Loddon Deep Lead cos., which were
simply sinks, and many unmistakable untamed
“cats.” To compile a “cat” list meant digging
into the past of a heap of mining and miscellaneous
cos., and stirring up large quantities of dust and
bad smell and dead body. The figures for mining cos.
represent book values, and £16,000,000 about fairly
represents the “live” value of Bull s mining scrip.
The market value at one time or another of the
£16,000,000 must have been more nearly £100,000,-
000 on the London Stock Exchange. As illustration, in
1889, Mount Morgans sold in London for £l6—and
there were a million shares on the registers. The
Bulletin usually states the vearly tribute paid by
Australia to London at £14,500.000, and this is
probably as near the mark as it Is possible to get,
because there are still large sums of mortgage money
and share capital invested here, of which there can be
no public record. No figures are published regarding
British insurance cos.’s investments in this country.
Speaking generally, the main deduction from the
compilation is that Australia is becoming more self-
reliant in the matter of capital, and every year less
inclined to borrow .money from its poor' old Uncle
John. _ The statistics concerning Government and
municipal loans have been taken from newspaper
announcements as well as official statements, so as
to bring them up to 30th June, 1912.
Public Debt, funded and unfunded . . £286,000,000
Corporation and municipal loans . . 23,000,000
British Banks —Capital . . . . . . 7,755,000
British Banks —Deposits in London.. 2,245’000
Pastoral companies . . . . . . 3,755.000
Miscellaneous companies .. . . 2,670,000
Mining companies 16,000,000

ROCKEFELLER’S FINISH
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Statisticians have computed that John D. Rocke-
feller’s income is £2B a minute.
While some of us must pull and haul,
Or delve, or scribble rhyme, or scrub,
Or guide a plough, or swing a maul
All day to earn our meagre grub,
This fellow's income never ends,
And, though he sleep, or stand, or sit,
In ever-dripping dividends
His shower of minted gold descends.
If Heaven in its wrath think fit
Some day he may be caught in it!
Through eyes of faith the stirring scene
Is photographed upon* my brain,
A living picture. All serene
Rockefeller feels the precious rain
That bounces on his hairless crown.
He grins and coughs a joyous wheeze
To hear it gaily clinking down,
The while he capers like a clown.
But dollars on his ankles seize,
And soon he’s buried to the knees.
He chortles while the tide of cash
is rising well above his thighs.
He loves its sweet, metallic splash,
And greets the flow with rapturous cries.
It climbs about his neck, it slops
Into his gaping mouth. Anon,
Above him rain the golden drops,
And John D.’s breath for ever stops.
So up to judgment passes John
With all his sins his head upon.
VICTORIA.
EDDYSON.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A Song of Bunker Chow.
The 19 Chinese stowaways, discovered on the steamer
Empire, received a terrible knocking about during
the recent gale. —News item.
The wind pipes shrill ’neath a low’ring sky ;
The wet cliffs tremble, the sprays blow high ;
Hove-to in the gale the steamers lie.
See the “stand-off” signals flying!
The flags are stiff in the savage squalls ;
Around the station the fine rain falls,
Arid a buzzing siren whisp'ring calls »
Like a sea-thing slowly dying.
The smoking chargers the mad gusts ride,
Rear up and paw at a captain’s pride;
Each iron thing staggers from side to side
And out of the combat -lunges ;
A wave sweeps up like a trembling hill,
A burst of white and the well-decks fill;
A sickly roll I—but she’s floating still !
Then down in the trough she plunges.
Out there in cabins and cold saloons
Are voyagers begging of all God’s boons,
In accents sad as the deep bassoon’s,
The gift of a death-bed stable;
And further down, 'neath the cabin floor,
Amongst the coal are a ghostly score,
Who jabber madly, and skid, and roar
In a Chow-made Pit of Babel.
Would pleasure brighten the Captain’s brow,
When shrill winds whistle and great ships bow,
If he knew Two Thousand Pounds of Chow
Were down in the bunkers batching?
Would he like to listen at each stray hole
To the sliding roar and crash of coal,
And the whines and squeaks of each lost soul
At Australia’s front door scratching?
When wind is shaking the window-pane,
And pelting it with the icy rain,
When the good house timbers creak and strain,
And a fearful sea is running,
I wake and wonder how fares the Chow,
Who sits and shrieks in the bunkers now!
Oh! the China boats they dive and bow,
And the coal is simply stunning!
N - s - w -
KODAK.
Three Cities.
BRISBANE.
Within her lotus bower,
Heavy with many an over-scented flower,
Beneath a feathery Cocos palm she lies,
Watching the lazy painted butterflies
Flutter about her, settle, pause and whirl
In mail of green, vermilion, gold and pearl.
Her dusky rounded breasts, delicious pair,
Half-drowned beneath the black wealth of her hair,
Peep out their rosy apples, playing spy
On all the garden’s scented panoply.
Hibiscus flowers are throning her around,
And strew with crimson petals all the ground
For her delighting. One by one she takes
And weaves them into garlands, leans and breaks
Some leafage to relieve their hue’s excess,
Till all is to her liking; halts to press
Some ruffled petal with her slumbrous lips,
Then flings her hair back, rises up and slips
The crimson crown upon her.
So was she
When all my heart she took in dreamy feel
SYDNEY.
Her lusty lover and lord, the Sun,
Well has he wooed her to-day and won„
Who, sprawling there in delicious ease,
Listens the witchery of the breeze,
Murmurous through the slumbering trees.
The waters, lapping in bay and cove,
Croon her a lullaby of love,
As, smiling and sighing in turn, she seems
The sweeter a wanton, dreaming her dreams.
To-morrow again her lord will press
Those amorous limbs in hot caress,
Gladly compliant to his offence;
Till, roused from her dreamy indolence,
She mingles with him in a flood of sense,
Spent, but happy to hear him tell
The morrow again he will love her well.
Then tenderly smiles at the touch of his lips
As off" to''his western couch he slips.
But should he his coming long delay
And all is sullen on cove and bay,
She’ll wrap herself in her scented hair,
With naught to console her until she hear
The wheels of his chariot rumbling near.
Then up will she leap to meet her lord,
Yielding to him her glories stored
Against his coming, until she be
Steeped in delicious ecstasy.
ADELAIDE.
A prim little damsel, neat and sweet,
Trips down the glaring lazy street,
Dressed so demurely, all in white,
Looking to neither left nor right:
“You know how people talk!” she thinks.
A moment later the little minx,
Sipping her cup of afternoon tea,
With mirrors round and none to sec,
Smooths her collar, adjusts her hat,
And home she hies her, pitter-pat-pat!
O maid demure! I marvelled oft
If ever you your staidness doffed ;
But when I came to know you well,
Oh, Holy Smoke —but I mustn’t tell! ! !
N.s.w.
BERNARD INGLEBY.
Dawn Song.
MAORILAND.
The night is deep
And the moon swings low,
The world’s asleep,
And the grave clouds go
Past the quiet stars above. Below
My heart is full of music, though
The world’s asleep.
The flush of day
Tips the hills with gold.
You bid me stay,
Though my tale is told
And my lips are bold,
And the dawn strikes c01d,..
You bid. me stay !

THE INADEQUATE FENCE.
Mr. Hughes, Attorney-General of Australia, says he has learned that the United States Meat Trust has purchased land and made satisfactory arrangements with some stockowners
to carry on business. “The Government,” he adds, “cannot do anything in the matter in the present state of the law.”
THE INADEQUATELY-ARMED PERSON: “Wh-s-s-s-sh!”

TERMS TO SUBSCRIBERS.
(In Advance, Only.)

By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty-
Six Shillings sterling you can have The Bulletin
posted regularly to you every week for a year.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., which should be crossed, and
all Business Communications to be addressed to The
Manager.
How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and, to an extent, themselves) by taking
some care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no ccse be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send tlierh to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin,” is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
f used.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and, where prac-
ticable, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” col-
umn all contributions submitted, whether in the
form, of Political, Social or other Articles, Verse,
Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially accept-
able), Paragraphs, Letters or Newspaper Clippings.
All communications, will be regarded as strictly confi-
dential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS.
when the address is specified and stamps are en-
closed. But no MS. will he returned, or vre* n "ved.
unless these conditions are observed. Whr - MS. is
forwarded without stamps for return it will be un-
aerstood that the copy is to be destroyed if not ac-
ceptable. Personal applications for return of MS.
cannot receive attention. The Bulletin desires,
fiirther, those sending MS. to distinctly understand
that it is not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of MS.
To Amateurs in Black anti White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original
humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash,
of sufficient merit and interest and adapted for
direct reproduction, or any interesting photograph
illustrative of a current event, will be paid, for same
on acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea,
but unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any pic-
ture which has to be re-drawn—will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, AUGUST 1, 1912.
TEMPORARY FINANCE.
The N.S.W. State Government secured
tnree months’ supply in a spare moment last
week, at a rate which suggests that it in-
tends the year’s expenditure to be well over
£20,000,000 —possibly up to £25,000,000. The
loan expenditure for the 12 months just
ended was a record even for the thriftless Ma
State, and there are signs and wonders and
indications that the loan outgoings for the
present year are intended to be on a still
larger scale. As to where the money will
come from there is little evidence, but there
are many misgivings, The Ma State has
put up the rate of interest in what used to
be the Post Office Savings Bank by i per
cent., in order to attract more funds, and is
thus running a notable sprint with the new
Commonwealth Bank. It was lately driven
to admit that loose cash was becoming so
tight in the local market that it could no
longer do its borrowings in Sydney as of old,
so it raised a couple of millions in Condon
on a one-year I O U, and on terms which sug-
gested that it was close up against the dead
wall of necessity. Now the Australian Gov-
ernment has lent N.S.W. a further £300,000
to go on with, out of the Australian notes
if serve, which about ends all visible possi-
bilities from that source for a long while to
come. This is a short-dated loan, like others
obtained in the same Quarter. And when one
considers the short-dated loans that will fall
due almost as soon as raised; the old, long-
dated loans, which are falling due in fairly
large quantities, and the fact that N.S.W.
and Queensland both want to float new loans
on a bigger scale than they ever did before, if
the present boom is to be kept going, it is
evident that complications may arise. The
extreme difficulty with which Queensland and
Maoriland have lately raised the wind in
John Bull’s money market constitutes fair
warning that there is very little to be done
there nowadays, except on rather costly
terms. The unprecedented upheaval of im-
ports, under a tariff which was fairly good
some years ago, but which the recent general
rise in wages has made quite inefficient, was
the beginning of the trouble. Then the tem-
porary drought and the fall in prices re-
duced the value of exports. The quantity of
exports was also reduced, because a very
large number of men who would, in the
ordinary course of events, have been supply-
ing material for sale in foreign markets,
were attracted away to the job of building
public works—often rather unpromising pub-
lic works—paid for out of loan money. So the
gold export increased enormously f b fill the
gap, while the production of gold was dimin-
ishing very heavily, and the local market
grew more and more bare of the only circu-
lating medium which speaks all languages.
Just as the loan boom was going its strongest
it practically fell down at a moment’s notice,
as one might say, and broke its neck, so to
speak. Wherefore, it would be no great
exaggeration to say that it may possibly be
a corpse, or thereabouts.
* * . * *
Theoretically, when the various Australian
Governments can’t get money locally, they
have merely to borrow in England. This is
theoretical, but it isn’t practical. Though
money has been pouring out of Australia of
late, i£ an almost unprecedented fashion,
there is no evidence that J. Bull has been
getting the money. In fact, he seems to
have more trouble on his hands just now
than cash. His consols have fallen to a
point that they hadn’t reached for SI) years.
The decline is so great that the British Sav-
ings Banks have lost about £40,000,000
through the depreciation of their stock of
Government securities, and, but for the fact
that they have the State guarantee behind
them, they would have had, ere this, to file
their schedule. Consols are reckoned about
the most respectable old security on earth.
They have never been discredited by sus-
picion, repudiation, or the payment of 55., or
even 17s. 6d., in the £. Yet at the present
figure they stand for £3 7s. 9d. per cent,
interest. Every State in Australia has bor-
rowed, at one time or another, on much bet-
ter terms than these, and deeply-indebted
Westralia, paying £3 Bs. 4d. per cent, as an
average on its entire debt, is actually almost
on a level with the British Government. The
simple explanation seems to be that Bull
has been on a financial jag, just as we have,
and when Bull is expected to support, not
only his own public drunk, but ours as well,
a great deal too much is asked of him. If he
finds himself unable to finance the double
burden, he will probably look after his own
financial orgy, and let ours go. And that
will be very bad for Australia’s borrowing
Governments, unless they contrive to stop
the outflow of gold in exchange for imported
goods, and get back to a position in which
they will be able to finance their own orgy.
Britain doesn’t make additions these days
to its recognised and official public debt—its
consolidated annuities or consols —for the
sufficient reason that, if it did so, it would
have to issue the new securities at about £7O,
and the discredit would be too great. Rather,
it devotes its surpluses to buying up and ex-
tinguishing the depreciated securities. Mean-
while, the country has “saved its face” by
issuing a multitude of small miscellaneous
securities at higher rates of interest. And
its public works expenditure (apart from
railways and canals, which are in the hands
of Private Enterprise) are mostly attended
to by )ocal bodies, which often borrow nowa-
days at high rates —the Port of London
Authority has raised money at 4 per cent.—
and often spend none too wisely. The total
British debt —national and local—is esti-
mated to have increased by about £600,000,000
since the beginning of the present century.
The wholly unproductive local debt of Eng-
land and Wales—the writer has no figures
at hand for Ireland and Scotland—has grown
by about £100,000,000 since the vear 1000.
So J. Bull has his own anxieties to deal
with. The misfortune is that he is
pushed for money at the same time as
ourselves. The gold we have lost mav have
gone to France, or Germany, or the United
States, or partly to one and partly to an-
other, but apparently our father Bull hasn’t
got it. •
* ! * * *
The Tory Preetrade press, as might be
expected, seizes the opportunity to throw a
brick at Australian democratic legislation.
This, for instance, was the view which the
S. M. Herald professed to take of the mat-
ter in an article headed “Temporary Sup-
ply” (25/7/T2) :
Already indications of trouble have given the State
Treasurers much anxiety. Recent loans have not
been successful in London, and the underwriters are
wondering how far they are likely to be involved with
Australian stock. This is due very largely to a
feeling of resentment in the old country at dis-
criminating taxation. The absentee tax. jor instance,
has roused much indignation among those who are
helping substantially in the development of this con-
tinent, though domiciled in Great Britain; and the
question of Empire is being discussed from a stand-
point not at all favorable to us as a Dominion sup-
posed to be nroud of the Imperial tie. It is not now
a question of dealing with men who have made great
sums of money in Australia, but who decline to
spend it hero. What has to be considered is the diffi-
culty into which New South Wales is being cast at a
critical period in her history by the action of those
who symnathise with absentees, and who are button-
ing up their pockets just when it is essential that
they should be emptying them.
It is. of course, the view which a Freetrade
and Tory paper would put forward. But it
isn’t the fury which has been aroused by
the Australian absentee tax which has made
the bottom fall out of the British Govern-
ment’s own special gilt-edged security. Our
troubles and those of the old man in London
have arrived at the same moment, and as a
mere matter of figures his are quite as bad
as ours. His advantage lies in the fact that
he has no great mass of debts falling due at
a fixed date; and that his liabilities are
local, whereas a great part of ours are for-
eign. Australia has to go holding out its
hat to Bull. Bull, at the worst, has only
to hold out his hat to himself.
It is consoling to recall the fact that,
however bad things may appear, Australia
has always pulled through its troubles and
avoided public insolvency. On the other
hand, the luck doesn’t last for ever, and
everybody dies one time or another. The
main elements of the situation are these:
Australia has never owed so much money
before, so it may be inferred that it was
neyer before so close to the tail-end of its
credit. (Presumably, the more that has
been borrowed, the less there remains to bor-
row.) It has never before had so many old
loans falling due in a short space of time.
It has never before wanted so many new
loans in a short space of time, for it has
neyer, at any previous date, tried to run
quite so vast a boom. It has never before
imported such a mass of foreign goods. It
has never before rushed up its export of
gold to pay for foreign goods in such a
sudden and heroic fashion. And it has not
once, since 1854, seen its output of gold de-
creasing so rapidly, which means that the
candle has, within the last few months, been
burned at both ends, a record addition to
the demand being accompanied by a record
islump in the supply. That is the debtor’s
side of the case. As regards the creditor,
our valued parent Bull has not, for 89
years, seen his own gilt-edged securities so
low. He has never before owed so much
money, or been so heavily loaded with taxes,
or had so gigantic a shortage in his Savings
Bank funds, or had so big an aggregate
debt—national and municipal. He has never
paid so much for his bloated armaments,
and not since the days of Napoleon I. has
he had so bad a scare re possible invasion.
He has never before seen so many people
leaving his country, or been so dependent
on foreign nations for his food, or found
himself so definitely relegated to third place
among the world’s manufacturing communi-
ties, or had such extensive strikes, or had so
many windows broken by Suffragettes.
! urthermore, he has never had a humble
Australian or ojther relative, with a look of
dog-like fidelity in his eye, clamoring on his
doormat for so much new loan money, or
for the renewal of so many old loans. So it
may be alleged with some reason that the
situation is unusual, or is going very shortly
to be unusual.
* * * *
Yet it is difficult to see that there is much
to be done in the way of reform or repair
unless a lot of the old wooden gods are
thrown overboard, and there is great re-
luctance to part with these old gods: Con-
sider the case of N.S.W.: It has the largest
debt of all the States. It is struggling
hardest after new borrowings. It supplies,
both to the Australian Liberal party and
the Australian Labor party, the largest con-
tingent of those politicians who believe in
low tariff, huge imports, and a great out-
flow of money to pay for the imports—that
outflow which is making it impossible to
raise loans in Australia. It possesses the
most strenuous high wage Government in
Australia, and when high wages and a low
tariff come together things are bound to
happen. It adheres hard and fast to the
policy of dragging all possible trade and
population to Sydney, in consequence of
which that private-landlord-owned city has
overgrown its means of communication,
-this capital of a State with about five per-
sons to the square mile has acquired a
traffic problem worthy of the Capital of a
State with 100 people per square mile.
Therefore N.S.W. is faced with a very large
bill (to be liquidated out of the loans which
are so difficult to get) for underground rail-
ways, bridges and tunnels. It can’t realise
the great profits that would accrue through
the establishment of State-owned cities at
such neglected deep-water outlets as Two-
fold Bay and. Port Stephens, because the
congestion policy and the assumed interests
of Sydney and of the private landlords of
Sydney forbid. It can’t make much out of
the lands in the Far North anu Far South,
because to do that it would have to open up
new ports and break away from the Sydney
fetich. It has to build absurdly long rail-
ways at great cost, and to work absurdly
long railways at an absurd loss, in ordm- to
drag everything to Sydney. For a State
which.has got the congestion disease and
the Foreign-trade disease and the State
I rights disease badly, there is really no re-'
source save to keep the loan boom going as
long as possible, and on almost any pos-
sible conditions. And Queensland, which
represents the other important section of
the loan boom, has to build its so-called
transcontinental line in order to express its
anger and cussedness against the proposed
lederal railway from Port Augusta to Port
Darwin. Therefore, Queensland also, it
would seem, must continue its wild career
in the interests ’of State Frights.
These are strenuous times, but the main
features of the case—the stupendous loan
revel, the importing revel, the outflow of
gold,, the falling off in the local gold pro-
Auction, the practical stoppage in local loan
supplies, and the comparative failure of the
British loan market, combined with the drop-
ping of the bottom out of Britain’s his-
torical public security—have all arrived
very suddenly. It will be some little time
before the effect is wholly manifest. Still,
if events keep moving for a few more
months along the present lines the results
should be _ interesting. It should be worth
while to sit up a't night and watch them.
THE BOY WHO MUST NOT WORK.
A GOOD many years ago it happened, in a
remote country, where there were no indus-
trial laws or compulsory education laws,
that child labor in factories became, for a
little while, a serious problem. In fact, it
became a temporary horror. Children started
as industrial workers at the age of live years
or thereabouts, and toiled for enormously
long hours at low wages, while their parents
were unemployed. It is all a matter of
ancient history—as dead and buried as the
Pope’s scheme for seizing Londonderry, or
the material Hell, or the horned and tailed
devil who goes about like a roaring lion,
chewing those he can devour. Nowadays, in
a country like Australia, where comnulsory
schooling lasts till the patient reaches the
age of 13 or 14, and there are practically no
child workers except on cow farms,
the supply of apprentices is insufficient
to meet the demand, and not enough
to - replace the old man when he goes
to his great, reward. There is no need for
restriction, and the idea that* the hoy of 15
or so is an enemy to industry, a curse and a
public danger, is as out of date as the armor
which the old crusaders wore when they
clubbed the enemy in Palestine. Neverthe-
less, the dread of the rising generation sur-
vives, and one of the quaintest developments
of trades unionism is the desire to restrict the
number of apprentices—just as if the world
was full of possible apprentices. It is
really just as. necessary to restrict the
domestic servant or the bunyip, who are both
scarce at present. With which exordium
The Bulletin prints one clause of a pro-
posed agreement, as regards compositors,
which has been submitted to it:—
Apprentices. —That the office shall be entitled to
one apprentice for every seven journeymen com-
positors permanently employed, such apprentices shall
serve for a period of not less than six years, and shall
receive 12s. 6d. per week for the first year, 17s. Gd.
second year, 225. 6d. tnird year, 30s. fourth year, 40s
fifth year, and 50s. sixth year. Apprentices shall
not operate the linotype until the last two years of
their apprenticeship, and may permanently operate
the linotype during the last year only of their ap-
prenticeship. A week’s work to consist of not more
than 44 hours.
That proposal, put forward apparently
in all good faith, shows how the old fear
of being swamped by child labor lingers, just
as if there was no compulsory schooling,
and the miserable infant still started work
at the age of five years. It is suggested that
there shall be one apprentice for seven per-
manently-employed journeymen. Thus, an
office with six regular compositors would
have no apprentice. One in which the work
was spasmodic, so that the average number
of employees was 10 or 11, of whom only six
were regular hands, would also have no
apprentice. One with 13 regular employees
would have a single apprentice, it being a
man short of the Staff which qualified it to
have two. Probably it would be a lavish
estimate to assume that, under such an
arrangement, there would be one apprentice
all round to every 10 journeymen. But
even assuming that every printing office had
an exact seven or 14 or 21 or 28 grown-up
experts, and that there was an apprentice to
each seven, and that the apprentice never
came to a premature death, the position
would be interesting.
* '•* * *
In the first place, there must be only
one son of working age lo every seven com-
positors. If there were two sons one would
ha,Ve to but I into some other occupation,
and the other occupation would have just
as good a right to preserve the proportion
of one learner to seven qualified men as the
printing trade. Or else : the other son would
have to be a drunken loafer, and a curse to
the community. In fairness, every seven
men, in every skilled. or unskilled occupa-
tion, should, only have onq son of working
age among them. And the old man would
have to stick at his job for 42 yehrs after
he finished bis apprenticeship. He must do
that, even if Australia never expanded at
all —even if it acquired no more
population, And raised no more litera-
ture, and produced no more papers or
books of pamphlets, and grew no more job
printers. Assuming that a boy was appren-
ticed at the age of 15, and became a full-
blown expert at the age of 21. he would re-
quire to toil capably and persistently till he
reached the age of 63; merely in order to
keep up the existing supply of workers. Any
deaths, disablements or retirements that
happened before that age would reduce the
supply, yet deaths, disablements and
retirements must needs be numerous.
It is a lamentable fact that, even in this
splendidly healthy country, the average
length of life is not 63 years, or anything
like it. And even if it were, no healthy-
minded person wants to wprk till the last
day of his life, and take Ills tombstone to
the office with him when lie- sees* Death
staring him in the face, so as to be pre-
pared for eventualities. Out of the
2,313,035 male citizens of Australia enum-
erated at last census, not many more than
110,000 had reached their 63rd year. In
fact, the slump began between the ages of
30 and 34, and between the ages of 55 and
50 the falling away in the number of sur-
vivors was terrible.
On the basis of one learner to seven
grown-up experts all industries must gradu-
ally die out. And either the birthrate must
be squashed, in which case the nation
will also die out, or else the great majority
of the boys must lean up against posts in
the streets while the old man is battling for
the family crust. To put the matter into
figures, there were in Australia, at the cen-
sus of 1911, about 310,000 males aged be-
tween. 15 and 21, which is about the appren-
ticeship age. And there were about three
times that number who were over 21 yet not
quite due for the old-age ■ pension, though
many of them were doubtless more or less
invalided, or had more or less given up active
work. When lliere are only three pos-
sible adult workers to one possible learner
there isn’t much hope of keeping industries
alive, to say nothing of providing for their
expansion, if only one learner is allowed to
seven adult workers.
* * * *
Something might also be said of the con-
dition of the wretched youth who is to be
apprenticed for “not less than six years,”
but is to spend the whole of the first four
years in sweeping the floor and running
errands, and isn’t to begin learning his
trade till the fifth year. That proviso re-
presents in full the idea that a boy is a
danger, and a curse, and an enemy to the
old man. The right of the unemployed to
demand that the State shall find them work
is often heard of, yet if the people who put
forward that plea also demand that most of
the rising generation shall not be allowed
to learn how to work the situation becomes
complicated. But, as already mentioned, the
trouble is merely a tradition, and a survival
from other days and other circumstances.
It dates from the period of child labor in
English factories. The Australian compul-
sory education system has abolished all pos-
sibility of the son becoming a. dangerous
competitor of the father, except in the
accursed cow industry, and even there he is
generally the father’s slave rather than his
competitor. The cow industry is the only
one nowadays where youthful labor requires
much regulation, and there regulation is re-
quired very badly.


PLAIN ENGLISH.
Enter the Meat Trust.
It was definitely announced last week that
‘ the American Beef Trust had secured 130
acres of superb country on the Brisbane
River, near Queensport,” and that it was
erecting there “the largest and most up-to-
date meat-exporting depot that Australia
has yet known.” Whereupon the Melbourne
correspondent of one of the Sydney dailies
wrote: —
“Abject terror” describes the feeling of the Aus-
tralian meat exporters since the American Beef
Trust has commenced its operations in Australia.
According to a very high authority in the city,
Federal Ministers have been reviewing Federal legis-
lation in order to ascertain if any existing law will
enable them to prevent the Trust gaining control of
the meat supplies of Australia.
So far they have failed to discover any Act which
will give them the power.
It was a sloppy way of putting it, but. it
gave a fair general idea of a position which
contains all the elements of a tragedy. The
American Beef Trust, so-called, is in reality
a Meat Trust. It deals in every conceivable
variety of flesh, including mutton. It repre-
sents an amalgamation of the vast busi-
nesses of Hammonds, Armours, Libbys,
Swift, Morris, Sulzbergers and others,
and has a capital of £40,000,000 sterling.
It takes no heed of tariffs or boundaries, and
spreads over half a dozen countries. It
realised early that the world’s population is
increasing much faster than the world’s food
supply, and that a huge corporation which
got there first might corner the meat of the
globe. It resolved to be huge and to get
there first. It fixes the price of meat, day
by day, in Chicago and elsewhere at its own
unsavory will, and the consumer has to pay
up and look as cheerful as he can. There are
practically no auction sales of Cattle any-
where in the States, and the erstwhile
dealers have become mere agents. The
Trust is supreme in Argentina. In fact the
only great meat-producing country on earth
in which it is not supreme at the present
time is Australia. As this paper pointed
out, over a year ago, when the Beef Trust
first started sparring for an opening in the
Commonwealth, there is considerably more
in its operations than meets the casual or
superficial eye. What it is aiming at is the
control of the meat-supply of the United
Kingdom. That country is the world’s
great meat importer. It depends more en-
tirely upon imported meat than any other
place of account. It is a place worth
capturing, and the simple way to do that is
to capture the sources of supply. Most of
them are already in the Trust’s hands, but
that body is a tidy and symmetrical octopus
and wants the lot. Besides, though its pre-
sent profits are immense, it hates to think
that there may be an odd 2d. that it has
missed. When the institution carries its
point the unhappy Briton will be faced with
the terrific problem or job of paying a Yan-
kee meat-bill on a Freetrade wage-basis.
The result may be that the million Bri-
tishers who are chronically on the verge of
starvation; and the further _five millions
who don’t earn more than 25s. per week,
year in year out; and the further five mil-
lions who do a little better, financially, and
yet walk ever hand in hand with indigence,
will take the thing in a philosophic spirit
and eat grass or some other vegetarian fare.
On the other hand, these people may be
maddened at the irretrievable loss of the
sustenance which their national song and
story have assured them for centuries is
their inalienable right, and in their fury it
is conceivable that they may rise up and set
fire to Buckingham Palace, execute the
blameless George V. and his consort, refuse
to pay the taxes requisite to build 10 battle-
ships to Germany’s six, and in the final up-
shot tear down the Empire upon their heads
in a condition of chaos and ruin. Such
tragedies have happened, for less serious
causes, all through history. Australia is
fairly creeping with people who call them-
selves Imperialists; and the strong suit of
every one of them is Devotion to the Mother-
land. An opportunity now offers to prove
that devotion in a substantial fashion. As
the newspaper paragraph above quoted re-
marks : “Federal Ministers.... have failed
to discover any Act which will enable them
to prevent the Trust gaining control of the
meat-supplies of Australia.” There is only
one way in which such a concern can be
effectively scotched, and that is by the
people of Australia so voting at the coming
Referendum as to give the Australian Par-
liament the right to say what is and what
is not an injurious Trust, and in the event
of its identifying one of t-he latter sort, to
order its dissolution. The allegedly dis-
loyal Laborites are willing to do their share
in this genuinely Imperialistic work. What
is. the matter with the frothy Liberal “Loy-
alists” lining up beside them?

Sydney’s Most Desperate Problem.
“Henri Singemoine’s” View of the Case:
The N.S.W. Government, like so many other pre-
ceding Governments, has announced in a firm tone
of voice its intention of embarking, presently, on
the construction of a North Shore bridge, and an
underground railway system that will bring Sydney
abreast of the times as regards transport facilities.
If a general election weren’t pending; if so many
other Governments hadn’t talked in a similar strain,
and wound up by doing nothing; if the McGowen
Administration wasn’t heavily committed already in
the matter of finance; and if there were no such
matters to consider as the exigencies of party gov-
ernment, I might delude myself with fond dreams to
the extent of believing that, before I go completely
bald, and enter on the doddering period of life’s fitful
struggle, I may know the joy of riding home in a
sitting posture after the day’s toff. instead of having
to accomplish the journev on a crowded tram-step, in
imminent peril of my life. As things are, I make
bold to risk a prophecy or two.
The bridge and underground proposals having been
ventilated sufficiently to stir the Sydney press to a
pass’on of enthusiasm will be gently but very reso-
lutely dropped. When the elections transpire in
December, or earlier, these matters will not be men-
tioned anywhere save in a few North Sydney centres,
and in the city constituencies which are immediately
concerned to bring about their accomplshment. Can-
didates for the political favors of Outback seats
will, on being challenged, hotly deny that under-
ground railways and a bridge are intended to be
built by their crowd, and they will denounce these
schemes as samples of wild extravagance, and as
flamine- iniustices to the long-s-’ffering person upon
the soil —just as C. G. Wade did in the early part
of this year, in an endeavor to enthuse an audience
which he was addressing in the Back of Beyond. As
p-h alternative to city railways and a North Shore
Bridge, up-country orators will advocate, as one
gas-bag, that the two or three millions requisite for
siich works shall be spent on more railways for the
nrimarv producer. Wade did this the other dav,
according to the sworn statements of a reoutable
country reporter and half a dozen morally stainless
bucolics or backblockers, and the smaller State poli-
ticians —if there are any smaller than Wade —may
be relied on to follow suit.
Whichever party wins, whether Labor or Liberal,
it will inevitably find itself composed of seven or
eight city members who are fairly lusting to see two
or five or 10 or 20 millions sterling borrowed with-
out a moment s delay, for the purpose of building
the bridge and tunnelling the tubes; and it will also
comprise 40 or 50 country members who are irre-
vocably pledged to raise Gehenna before they agree
to any such ventures. A couple of years will pass
more or less peacefully, and then, another general
election being imminent, the Government of the day,
hounded to action bv the city press, will send to
England, or Rome (U.S.A.), or Mecca, or Bagdad, or
any oth?r old spot, and order out a “traffic expert.
He will regard Sydney narrowly, and remark, in the
tone of voice employed by one to whom a bright new
thought has suddenly occurred, that what the city
needs is a system of underground railways and a
North Shore bridge. Whereupon the farce or tragedy
(the reader may regard it either way, according to
whether he habitually frequents the metropolis or
no + > will be resumed again de v»vo. _ _
The factor which has been the death of a civilised
city transit scheme in the past, and is likely to go
on being the death of it for all time, is,, of course,
that most imbecile of fetiches, Party Government.
The country constituencies can’t see why they should
incur liabilities with the obiect of advantaging Syd-
ney, and so long as they look at things that way the
politicians may be relied on to encourage them, with
the result that nothing will be done.
The real and only answer to a problem which has
been acute for the past five years, and which is
shortly going to become so ramnantly urgent that,
unless it is solved, something will surely burst, is
the speedy creation of a Greater Sydney, and the
undertaking of these essentially civic jobs by a muni-
cipal Parliament. Logically, Sydney has no more
right to expect the State to build and run its tubes
and bridges than it has to expect the same authority
to administer its electric light plant. New York,
Paris and London have all made successes, financial
and otherwise, of their underground systems. Till
recently there was some doubt as to whether one of
the London underground cos. was in a sound position.
However, a couple of months ago, the financial writer
of London Truth was able to say
“The public of Greater London is served, above
and below ground, in a manner that can only be
described as lavish, and the public in return has been
lavish in its patronage. This observation brings me
to the real task I have in hand —namely, to attempt
to show in figures what the ’Bus-Tube Combine is
accomplishing at the present time, with a view to
emphasising the desirability of the Underground
Electric Company’s fixed charge stocks as invest-
ments.”
And, after quoting the figures of the concern, he
wound up:—
“The underground electric stock yields fully 5 per
cent., and it would be difficult to find a stock better
secured so: far as income is concerned to give the
yield.”
In brief, there doesn’t appear even to be the slender
justification of monetary risk to excuse the neglect
of this frantically-needed Sydney service. But Sydney
will have to undertake it, for as long as politicians
are driven to manoeuvre for office, no State adminis-
tration will.
An Absurd Offence.
The legal offence known as “collusion,” the
thing which prevents the granting of so many
divorces, and invalidates so many others just
when they look good and promising, was given
a bad showing-up in England last month. In
fact, it was publicly reduced to the absuidity
which it is. The encouraging affair occurred
in the undefended suit of Pears v. Pears.
The evidence showed that the petitioner, Mrs.
Pears, had married Joseph Pears in 1886.
A year or so later she was consigned to a
home for inebriates. The husband fell in
love with one Ellen Nash, and took
her to live with him. The petitioner, Mrs.
Pears, does not seem to have worried unduly
about the matter. She identified herself
with the missionary business in Leeds, on
emergence from the Drink Home, and for 20
years left Joseph and Ellen to their own
devices. Then a clergyman put it to her
that it would be a gracious and kindly act
on her part if she divorced Pears, and so
gave him a chance to make “an honest
woman” of Nash, and regulate the anom-
alous position of his family. She agreed,
and was able to place before the President
of the Divorce Division, a collection of evi-
dence sufficient, under normal circumstances,
to ensure the dissolution of any marriage.
There was one haw in her case, and one only.
It consisted in the fact that she had no ill-
will towards her husband (due, probably, to
her realisation that his misconduct had
been largely brought about by her jam-
borees), and to the further fact that
she wanted him to re-mam'. If she had
been furious with her injured husband, and
anxious to trail his name in the mud. and
compass his financial and social ruin—if she
had desired to blast the character of his
paramour and proclaim to the world that the
offspring of the pair were bastards —the
Court would have beamed on her annrov-
ingly, and presently handed her what would
amount to an official certificate that all these
things were true. As it was, the President
said that: —
Though the allegations in the petition had been
proved, vet he did not think that the petitioner was
entitled 'to relief, since she did not want it for her-
self. He thought it was obviously a case directly
opposite to the principle the Court acted on, as it
was a case brought to relieve the guilty person.
Though he sympathised with the woman, he must
act according to judicial principles. He concluded
that the woman had not brought the petition to pro-
tect herself, and, therefore, he must refuse to exer-
cise his discretion on her behalf.
Marriage is the only legal contract or bond
which is rendered irrefragable by the mere
fact that both the parties wish to be freed
from it. The judicial principle which lays
it down that one or more parties to the suit
must at least appear to be unwilling that it
should be granted does nothing for the cause
of morality. One result—as witness the
Pears case —is that peonle are driven to
live in what is known as “open shame,” who
would otherwise get comfortably into line
with the rest of Society. Another result is
that those who are determined, to separate
are driven frequently to deception and per-
jury. Those who are too conscientious or
nervous to attempt to hoodwink Justice,
battle on in a relationship. which means
misery to themselves and serious difficulties
for their children. In this naper’s opinion
one of the changes for which the time is more
than ripe has to do with the wiping-out of
“collusion” as a bar to divorce. When a
citizen commits a series of thefts the Law
doesn’t enter into the psychology of the busi-
ness and inquire why he took the goods :it
simply passes him or her a term of gaol.
Similarly, when a husband or a wife is
guilty of marital transgressions, the Law has
no real call to consider the spirit in which
they were perpetrated, or to question the
objects or inner sentiments of the person
seeking redress. If it simply found on the
facts and acted accordingly, in the Divorce
as in other jurisdictions, a tremendous lot
of unhappiness, lying and crime would be
obviated; and these, after all, are the things
the Law is there to prevent.
