The Old Hop.
“0.K.” discourses :
The Vie. police force is seething with annoyance
at anew reading by the Crown Haw Department
of the Act which deals with the employment of the
John Dunn. It now appears that the Government
may retain the services of a Dunn for an indefinite
time after he reaches 55, he having no right to re-
tire and automatically start drawing his pension at
that age. To the layman the new interpretation ap-
pears sufficiently equitable and sensible. In fact, it
is something of a mystery that it isn’t applied freely
in other branches of the Civil Service. There are
men who are “tired and old and worn,” as the late
Harry Morant had it, at 45. The persistent beer-
fancier ; the neurotic ; persons who are born languid,
and folks with serious congenital physical defects,
are in this class. On the other hand, you get types
like James Bryce, Chief Justice Griffith, and
others who never seem to age. Obviously, it is rotten
economy to hoof out the fit because, at a certain
age, a certain crowd are liable to become unfit.
To get back to the Hop of Victoria, a policeman
who has a sound mind in a healthy body should be
worth keeping—in a billet where stoushing or chas-
ing ability is not a first requirement—up to the
age of 60 or a bit older. A red-nosed puffy sergeant,
with gouty feet and muddled brains, is a good rid-
dance as soon as his retiring hour strikes, if not
earlier. Providence, which is full; of compensations
for the public, usually decrees that the useless police-
man of 55 shall not live to enjoy many annual pen-
sions from the taxpayer in consideration of his pre-
vious unvaluable services, and, anyhow, his pension
is a smaller loss to the dountry than his full salary.
But the only sort of dignified policeman who was
worth his keep in active service up to the “retiring
age,” is usually worth paying for his experience a
few years longer. The Government should have the
option of refusing to convert a valuable public ser-
vant into a costly pensioner, although he personally
would rather draw sufficient means of support for
doing nothing, or running a pub, than a larger pay-
ment for continuing his old work. It may be argued
that a man who wants to retire on his fixed pension
is no longer worth the difference between his pension
and his salary ; but the argument applies only to those
constitutional loafers who never were worth the
salary. Also, it may be, and is, urged by young
constables that the sergeant of over 55 blocks the
way to the promotion of his juniors. Which is a very
young and one-sided view of the case. The useful
veteran who remains in harness to draw his salary,
whilst his worn-out contemporary is dropped by the
roadside, is practically showing the way for young
constables to enjoy a longer innings when they are
55.
The question of grievance in this matter turns on
the value of an old policeman’s experience. If no-
body’s experience is more valuable than that of the
grog-blossomed or dunder-headed incubus, whom the
Government gladly gets rid of in his 55th year, then
there should be no older man in the service. But if
even a policeman is not impervious to educational
influences, there is something to be said for the
Vic. Government’s claim to make the Force’s age of
pension a movable feast. If Burglar Bill had a say
in the regulation, he would superannuate every trap
at 40, or earlier.
The Claims of Geelong.
A roar of parish-minded bigotry has gone
up among N.S.W. politicians because the
Federal woollen factory is to be at Geelong
(Vic.), instead of being located, as every
good Federal institution should be, close to
Sydney. Already the meanness and petty
spite which caused the Federal capital terri-
tory at Yassberra (N.S.W.) to be cut down
so small are beginning to bear fruit. The
Australian Parliament believes in Public
Enterprise, and it has a large number of
activities either on hand or in view. These
require space, but there isn’t room for half a
dozen little industrial towns as well as the
capital itself —the City Beautiful —in the
Federal area. Thus a tendency arises, with
some show of excuse, to dot Federal indus-
tries here and there over the face of Aus-
tralia, so that when war comes the soldier’s
rifle will be manufactured at one place, and
his ammunition and clothes in another, and
his boots will grow in this direction, and his
hat a long distance away in that. Asa
matter of fact, Geelong is about as bad a
place as Sydney for Federal factories, and
Melbourne is by no means good. Yassberra
is better, but not nearly so much better as
it should be. It is Australia’s misfortune
that the great bulk of its population, most
of its wealth and its resources, and the mass
of its railway connections are on or close
to the coast. And the only so-called trans-
continental railway over the building of
which the Government shows any energy is
also, to a great extent, a coastal line. If war
comes —and it comes one day or another to
every country—and the attempt to defend
the coast-line —especially a scrap of coast-
line in the south-east —is a failure, then the
Australian armies must fall back inland, as
the Koers did when they put up their magni-
ficent scrap against Britain, and as Barclay
de Tolly and his Bussians did when they
led Napoleon I. to destruction in 1812. But
there will be this difference, and it is a differ-
ence which destroys the parallel and reduces
it to naught. The Boers and the Russians
were falling back on their resources, their
mass of population, their reinforcements,
their armories and factories, and, in the Boer
case, on their big railway centres. The Aus-
tralians will be leaving all these, and falling
back on hunger and comparative nothingness.
One of the things they will leave in their
retreat will naturally be the nice new fac-
tory at Geelong-beside-the-sea. A Govern-
ment with a wide Australian outlook would
make haste to build the Port Darwin-Port
Augusta railway through the middle of Aus-
tralia, and connect it with the railways from
the east, and thus open up the best-watered
far-inland country that Australia possesses.
Then it would try to establish population,
and agriculture, and some at least of its mili-
tary resources in these far inlands, out of
gunshot of the coast and the enemy’s fleet.
But, unfortunately, there are always votes
to be considered. The coastal places that
exist already have votes; the places that
ought to exist in the undeveloped interior
have none as yet. Geelong, once Sleepy Hol-
low, is rather a rising and ambitious spot
these days. It has about 31,000 electors,
and at last election it returned A. T. Ozanne
by a rather small majority. Geelong reckons
that something ought to be done for it, and
is capable of becoming nasty if something
isn’t done. The same sort of trouble arises
in a ( hundred other places. It doesn’t im-
prove the outlook for Australia, but then
party comes first, and Australia hobbles a
long way behind.
A Dirty City.
“Bloggs” scrapes some of the mud and wet
off his garments, and howls as follows: —
Sydney’s new Mayor weighed in with a superb
sample of municipal or unconscious humor last week.
He informed the dailies that he had made arrange-
ments to have the city hoardings, the tram cars, and
the windows of the bigger shops placarded with the
instruction: —
“Keep your City Clean. Do not throw paper, or even
tram tickets, on the streets, if you uo' you will be
prosecuted.”
The weather, in Sydney, during most of last week,
was rotten. It poured and cleared up, then drizzled
and cleared' up, and then repeated tne entire cere-
mony over again. Incidentally, it demonstrated to
every Sydney citizen with so much as an apology for
an eye for suen matters that the street-cleansing
methods of his native town are away back in the
dark ages of municipal effort. In many of, the
most frequented streets a rich black mud lay like a
pall over the landscape, and no one appeared to
dream of disturbing it. In the wood-blocked thor-
oughfares the ram did a certain amount in the way
of washing away clay, sand, offal, manure and the
like, but no human being took on the job so far as
the writer could observe. The result was that, for
several days, Sydneyites lived side by side with
liquid filth of all descriptions, and heated language
was general. In other big towns bands of civic
employees are constantly at work sweeping up and
carting away mud and manure as fast as they ac-
cumulate. The citizens find it pays, in the saying
of wear and tear to their garments, goods, vehicles
and health, and in a dozen other directions. Syd-
ney persistently economises in this regard, even as
it does in connection with the dust calamity and
peril. An occasional small boy, with a hand-broom
and a tin, is reckoned sufficient to cope with a
drenched city’s mud nroblem, just as one lonely and
prehistoric old watering cart, dragged by a single
horse, and worked by some contraption of string or
wire, is held to be ample to banish the dust of half
a dozen important and' wealthy streets. It was
characteristic of the immemorial Municipal Council
that, at the very moment when Sydney was at its
filthiest— la.rgelv owing to neglect of road-cleansing
and road-repairing—the head or chieftain of the
band which is responsible for the situation, should
come out with a bland suggestion that paper and
tram tickets ought not to be thrown round care-
lessly. Hardly anyone is ever prosecuted foy the
loathsome and insanitary offence of spitting on the
pavements, but there now appears to be a noss’bihtv
of someone being fined or gaoled or something for
dropping tram tickets. Still, it is only a possibility
after all. In the Vast resort the City Fathers are
going to depend, seemingly, on the citizens to keep
their city clean —in much the same spirit as the
crew of' a sttamer might helplessly cast on the pas-
sengers the job of working and navigating the vessel.

The intensest Tories of the Liberal Party
in Victoria continue to reveal their fiscal
feelings in their persistent attacks on Sam
Mauger’s candidature for the Senate. Dr.
Carty Salmon and McColl, his brother-
Senator, have the unqualified approval of
the Fusion, but Mauger is the pet aversion
of the Jokook end of the firm. Melbourne
Argus professes to despise Samuel because
“His • sympathies are with Labor, and his
votes will go as far as possible to Labor
proposals,” whereas the great objection to
the busy hatter, from the Jokook party
point of view, is his sympathy with the one
Labor cause which the Fisher Ministry has
consistently betrayed. Mauger, the Pro-
tectionist, is the man the Argus is wildly
endeavoring to crush. To barrack for him
would be to advocate a whole-hearted tariff
reform, and the discordant organ of Conser-
vatism can’t do that. So Mauger almost
promises to be the rock whereon the cranky
Fusion craft will split in Victoria. He
must stand for High Tariff if he is to stand
at all. Asa matter of fact, Mauger repre-
sents the only sort of Liberal party that has
a hope of ousting the Revenue Tariff Labor-
ites at next election, but the Argus, in. its
blindness, still thinks of re-establishing
“Liberalism” in power on terms of “fiscal
truce.” Meanwhile, Deakin. seemingly,
has ceased to think at all. The Mauger
problem has got him down and stifled him.

The Return of the Golden Age.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mr. Payne, member of Maoriland Parliament, ljas
prepared a Workers’ Right of Wages and Work Bill,
which provides that male and female workers, be-
tween the ages of 24 and 60 years, who may be un-
able to obtain employment, shall present themselves
at the nearest labor bureau, and receive as an abso-
lute right £3 weekly in respect of males, and £1 10s.
in respect of females.— -News item.
Before Jove sat upon his throne
The world was very sweet and fair,
And no one talked of cash or loan,
Or wanted very much to wear;
Such golden days shall now return,
And everything be quite as good
As when old incense used to burn
To Saturn in a Grecian wood.
Take hence the shovel and the pick,
Accursed be the heavy pen.
We shall not build of board or brick,
Nor plough the fiat, nor drain the fen;
We shall not lump upon the wharf,
Nor pull the teats of horned brutes,
Nor grind the wheat, nor weave the scarf,
Nor put a shine on muddy boots.
We shall not teach the wheel to roll,
Nor swing an axe, nor wield a saw,
Nor work to save an erring soul
The muscles of a preaching jaw;
We shall not slay the fatted ox,
Nor trim the whiskers of the sheep,
Nor pull a rope, nor quarry rocks,
Nor sell a choice allotment cheap.
But we shall lie in sun or shade,
And let the pleasant days go by,
Or dally with a winsome maid,
And look the bailiff in the eye;
Nor dun nor debt shall us affright,
But idle as the lilies blow,
We shall observe the sparrow’s flight,
And watch the cauliblossom grow.
The large, kind hand of Providence
Shall ope within the bureau door,
Without obeisance or pretence
Each morning we shall ask for more ;
Our own inalienable right
That none may filch away or rob
Is written fair in all men’s sight,
“Each week tliou shalt have sixty lok.”
All labor troubles here have end,
The curse of poverty is dead
We rise to hail the people’s friend,
And hang green things upon his head ;
All noisy agitations cease,
The Arbitration Court is vain,
Strikes are no more, the world is peace,
And pleasure follows after Payne.
N.S.W.

A Battleship Spun into a Ribbon —
Rolled around a reel, set away on a shelf: then,
thrown out through a tiny lens, it sails away before
your very eyes. Magic? No—Motography!
All Public Events
of interest, especially sports, are taken by our staff
photographers; screened weekly at the Crystal
Palace, Sydney; The Britannia, Melbourne; and
our other theatres throughout Australasia.
It’s “Williams Weekly,” real views of the news.
Managing: Director,
The Greater J. D. Williams Amusement Co. Ltd.
trade to England and Europe is a feature of great
concern in connection with any footing being ob-
tained here by the American concern.
Quite true —“so long as it conforms to the
law” the U.S. Trust is at perfect liberty to
butcher the Australian meat export industry.
And the Herald is quite resigned about it.
The idea of getting anew law doesn’t occur
to that paper. Seemingly, it never heard of
certain .Referenda intended to provide the
very measure which the hard case of the
Australian meat industry is calling for.
Minister Tudor and His Friend, the
Importer.
The leaders of the Labor Fusion —the thing
that is part Protectionist and part Foreign-
trade, part Real Labor and part Cheap
Labor —has one stereotyped reply nowadays
to any and every demand that White Aus-
tralia shall be adequately defended against
colored and low-wage competition. It is
the answer of lazy men who hate to tackle
a big job—pf half-and-half or shandygaff
men, who hate to commit themselves —of
scared men, who can’t bear to risk their
billets by awaking the comatose dog. And
Minister Tudor threw is off his boundless
chest last week, as usual. This is the daily
paper report:—
Mr. Tudor was waited upon in Melbourne yester-
day by representatives of the iron trades, who urged
an immediate upward revision of the tariff.
The Minister replied that he was against re-opening
the tariff until the workers and consumers’ interests
were safeguarded. If he gave 1000 per cent. Pro-
tection, he said, the workers would still have to fight
for a penny rise.
Yet Tudor and his friends are re-opening
the tariff every day—always in the interests
of that Tory middleman, the importer, who
deals so profitably in sweated and non-union
and colored labor goods. The tariff is
simply a weight put in the scale to balance
the difference between fair wages on one
side and poor wages on the other. If the
wages are raised at this end, without the
tariff being raised at the other, or if the
tariff is lowered, while the wages are kept
stationary, it is exactly the same thing.
Hither way the tariff is being tampered witn,
and the tariff question re-opened. Even the
dullest dog of a politician knows that arti-
sans’ wages in Australia have been steadily
on the up-grade for some time. It is ques-
tionable if Tudor could point to a single
instance of late where artisans’ pay has
been reduced, whatever may have happened
to the employees of his dear friend the
importer, but increases have been innumer-
able. And Tudor and his mates have cor-
dially approved. They have done everything
they could to interfere with the tariff in the
interests of foreign cheap labor, by increasing
the handicap on Australian industry, while
carefully refusing to interfere with the handi-
cap on foreign industry. Every rise of local
wages, while the tariff remains stationary,
represents so much Protection to Foreign
Manufacturers. And while doing this sort
of thing, Tudor and his crowd haven’t even
had the decency to insist that the importer
shall pay his clerks and warehousemen and
sailor folk better remuneration in considera-
tion of the extra advantages he receives. His
chances have nearly doubled in a
very few years, and the Federal Labor party
looks on him with a friendly eye all the time,
and sees nothing wrong about the business.
Of course, it is true that the local manufac-
turer’s employee Ims to light for his increase
ot wages every time, as Minister Tudor
was careful to mention. The local manu-
facturer has no idea of being a professional
philanthropist* Also, the importer’s em-
ployee has to fight just as hard, as Minister
ludor was so careful not to mention. Also,
though the manufacturer’s chances keep
growing worse, while the importer’s grow
better, the importer’s employee fights for his
little increase with much less hope, and
much less success, and with much less
assistance from Tudor and Cos., alleged
Laborites, alleged unionists, alleged advo-
cates of fair wages, and professed supporters
ot White Australia. Why that unstable
crowd always leans, on tariff matters, towards
the foreigner, the Tory, the middleman, the
sweater s agent and the nigger’s representa-
tive, the Lcird only knows. Possibly the
baleful eye of Bruce Smith has hypnotised
it and got it down.
North Sydney —the municipality which
turned down the City Council’s offer to sup-
ply it with cheap electricity lest acceptance
thereof should help the Greater Sydney
movement along—yearns to hand its electri-
cal business over to Private Enterprise. That
is to say, its aldermen yearn to do this
foolish thing. The other day they got
“counsel’s opinion” as to their powers in
the matter. Counsel opined that Bumble
had power to make a contract with Private
Enterprise. But— (1) Bumble had no
power to permit P.E. to erect poles or inter-
fere with roadways to supply electricity to
private consumers. (2) If Bumble granted
such permission, any property owner suffer-
ing damage could sue Bumble in his cor-
porate capacity. (3) Each individual
Bumble would be personally liable. (4)
Bumble had no power to regulate prices of
electricity to private consumers. Bumble,
in fact, according to counsel, stands in the
position of Siiylock —he may annex his
morsel of flesh, but blood-letting is nro-
hibited. In this case, however, Shylock is
a reckless financier, and is going for his
meat all the same. In other words, the
Council has decided to call for tenders from
Private Enterprise to supply North Sydney
with electricity. Unless Bumble thinks
better of it, or some patriotic ratepayer kicks

At Her Majesty’s, Sydney, the Quinlan
Opera Co. lately put on the brake by slowing
down to an average of about three first
nights a week. As most of the nights have
been filled with all the rain that missed the
ancient brolly of Noah, first-nighters paddled
home by emergency boat or water-tyred
motor. The management now threatens to
return to its original gallop. Every even-
ing at seven or eight o’clock ( accord-
ing to the length and breadth of
the remarks the tenor and soprano
will make to the stage moon or the
twinkling limelight star) there will be a
fresh trial stakes. This paper bets heavily
on the chances of that good-looking Wag-
nerian filly, Agnes Nicholls, winning by a
breadth or two, a shoulder ahead of Edna
Thornton.
Verdi’s digression into barbaric splendor,
which he called “Aida,” was intended for
Cairo, and first produced there in 1871.
This prolific composer keeps his best opera-
tic grist for the singer’s mill. But the over-
ture to “Aida” is sufficiently important to
come nearer to the German ideal (which
A; ns the vocalist and makes him sing for
his life against a hurricane blast of orches-
tration) than any of the popular Italian
operas of the Verdi period. In the Quinlan
co.’s production on Monday night Edna
Thornton, the jealous daughter of the Phar-
aoh of the moment, was an Amneris who was
strong and consistent in her vocal attack on
the heart of John Bull Coates—the Badames
of the cast. Amneris forced her voice too
much, once or twice, but the orchestra,
when Voghera steers the baton, leans ever
to the noisy side. Jeanne Brola, as the
Ethiopian bond-maid Aida, was shrill and
insignificant. This Yankeee singer’s vocal
organ is not up to grand opera standard.
This paper gave Brola the benefit of the
doubt Avhen the brawl of Puccini’s “Girl of
the Golden West” upset her musical apple-
cart. It would like to give her the ofition,
or the rising of the court, for her top notes
on the shores of the Nile. The sharp edge

lays. Allen Hinckley, without the Father
Christmas beard he has been doomed to
wear in other operas, was large, beautiful
and clean-shaved as the High Priest of Isis.
** .*■*■
“Faust,” produced on Saturday night by
the Quinlan aviary, proved one of its best
all-round performances. Agnes Nicholls,
admittedly too large in the size of her belt
to look the girlish Marguerite of “Faust’s”
vision, was enchanting on the vocal side.
Melba is always ahead of competition in
the florid trimmings of the older school of
Italian and French opera. Agnes Nicholls
is a safe second in the elaborate and showy
“Jewel Song.’ Rosina Beynon’s “Siebel”
was an improvement on most of her pre-
vious efforts, as heard in this city. Robert
Parker reduced Mephistopheles to an attrac-
tive vaudeville corner man with a streak of
the Devil in his worst moments. The
Faust of John Harrison was weak in spots,
but the spots only the size of three-
penny bits. As this issue smites the linotype,
“Valkyrie” is, or ought to be, swirling round
Her Majesty’s stage.

All melodramas are the same melodrama
with a different setting. For the latest
Adelphi production, the scene painters have
supplied a succession of sunlit Australian
backgrounds which are decidedly more cheer-
ful than the usual gloomy London surround-
ings. There are few rags in it, practically
no grime, and altogether a general atmo-
sphere of brightness and buoyancy. It is
well handled, by the company, headed by the
very decorative Violet Paget, in a series of
frock inspirations, and the capable and good-
looking Hugh Buckler. Comedian-author
Wilton Welch has provided himself with a
good deal to do; but he does it so well that

no one could resent his lack of absence. In-
cidentally, his Boy Scout is a deadly blow
at the Barnum-Powell circus organisation.
Miss Elwyn Harvey, as a Girl Scout, acts
crisply, and Frank Reis supplies a credit-
able burlesque of a Jew. Jennie Pollock is
gracefully insipid as a sort of secret service
female, and Godfrey Cass makes a thoroughly
vindictive villain, who takes his frequent
dashings to earth in a sportsmanlike spirit.

The frivolity of “Nightbirds” is to give
way to stern finance on Saturday at the
Criterion. “Get-Rich-Quick Wallingford,”
Cohan’s humorous comedy of the fake com-
pany-promoter, American species, will be
given by the Firm’s comedy company, with
specially imported leads. The analysis of
Wallingford’s balance-sheet will appear next
week.
The four cyclonic Amaranths hurled them-
selves back to the Tivoli, last Saturday.
They use up an enormous amount of energy
in their whirling display—probably as much
as a whole suburb of sluggish Mary Annes
supply in a month. It is harrowing to re-
flect on the amount of work that could be
done if the Amaranths would only turn a
dynamo for a bit instead of turning somer-
saults. The rest of the second part is sup-
plied by the genial songster, Alec Hurley,
the clowning Webb Brothers, Niagara and
halls, Frank Sidney and co., and Fred
Bluett. The bill is also graced by the
demurely clever Fanny Powers.
Two thousand feet of blood and fire is the
draw at the Lyceum this week. The film, “A
Soldier’s Honor,” was taken around and in
an American fort. A young lieutenant, who is
getting his first taste of war, shows a yellow
streak, and remembers an appointment at
home. However, his wife won’t have any-
thing to do with him; and he goes back, and
is captured, but, like Hajj, he breaks the
ropes and escapes to his own fort. There he
tells his comrades of a plot he overheard
among the enemy, and is thus the means of
saving his side being beaten by five wickets.
Other films that share the bouquets are
Views of Katoomba” and an adaptation of
the Bible story concerning the threatened cut-
ting up and subdivision of the great estate
comprised by the baby. This is attributed
to our old friend Solomon—the bookmaker
of old Jerusalem.

The people of Sydney had already seen
Sweet County Kerry” at the Adelphi, and
the second application at the Palace on
Saturday night inclined to become weari-
some. At first sight, it would appear that
Allen Doone’s humor was spontaneous, but
when, after seven months, it is practically
unchanged, the first impression fades away,
and the humor seems to be all cut and dried,
Doone’s songs are still the leading feature,
and his job of making a horseshoe out of a
made-to-order bar of iron still looms large
in the list of attractions. Although Edna
Keeley has lost some of her Yankee accent
(her chief drawback in Irish drama), she
still shows an occasional desire to overstep
the dividing line between brogue and twang,
or between blarney and Broadway. All the
other characters appear to have "been chis-
elled out during George Marlow’s season.
Ronald Riley,_ as Valentine Hay, is still the
very mechanical villain who employs a
second scoundrel to find a third ruffian to do
his dirty work for a fourth of what the work
is worth. Tom Buckley and Frank Cul-

The Pechilli Troupe has arrived at Sydney
National Amphitheatre. From the fact that
its members are billed as “Royal Entertain-
ers to the Emperor of China” (Who, as every
student of the Yellow Agony knows, is but
six years old), the public, ever hasty in such
matters, might leap to the conclusion that
the Pechillis are wet-nurses, or something
equally dull. This is not so. They are ath-
letes of a high order, contortionists of
supreme merit, plate-spinners and many
other things that are marvellous and bizarre.
Bessie Slaughter is still there, and her
mighty voice shows no signs of giving out;
while Shipp and Gafney, Professor Karl
and his pony, and a whole herd of other
artists are doing their duty by the manage-
ment right lustily.

Bernard Shaw’s jesting sermon seemed to
catch on to the uninitiated in the third act
of its first night at Melbourne Royal. People
who know not Shaw can hardly be expected
to enjoy the atmosphere of his wildest com-
edy the moment they enter it. Situations
in the opening scene are more obviously un-
reasonable than any that come later. A dot-
ing mother and an inseparable brother allow
Dolly Clandon to go to an unknown dentist
about her tooth before they call at the tor-
ture chamber to collect the damsel. Also,
the mother from Madeira, who has been
drawing a large income from her discarded
husband for 18 years, doesn’t know that he
is a resident propputy-owner of the English
town she has come to stay in. Moreover,
the cute Dolly and her unknown father, in
his turn, submit their mouths to a dentist
who treats teeth as mere matters for extrac-
tion-—which is very unlike a gum architect,
anyhow. The first act is the weakest of the
four. Subsequently, the developments of love
between Gloria and the dentist seemed too
subtle for most of the audience, and it wasn’t
until Valentine delivered his discourse on
sex-warfare to Mrs. Clandon that Shaw held
the house by the ear. Probably “You Never
Can Tell” will do satisfactory business. Cer-
tainly it will smooth the way for another
and another humorous philosophical comedy
by the same optimistic cynic.
Generally speaking, the performance is
better than was expected of “The Blue Bird”
co. Miss Gladys Ellam, as the popular
Dolly, although she has too sharp an edge
on her style, is pleasing in everything but
her make-up. Ewan Brook is a capital den-
tist, and Miss Bayley is a mother that any
family might be proud of. As to whether
Mrs. Clandon is not an impossibly superior
woman for any old-fashioned man to live
with, the question remains open in the play.
E. A. Warburton’s nervous, explosive im-
personation of Mrs. Clandon’s suffering hus-
band is strong by comparison with the
cruder work of the mummer who acts
McComas. Bohun, K.C., passes muster. For
the rest, there are Norman Page (the cat
of “The Blue Bird”), who presents twin
brother Philip as a smileless young prig with
a meeowic voice, rather than a light-hearted
young firework; and Madge Mclntosh and
Edward Rigby. Miss Mclntosh, as Gloria,
is an example of how to be charming though
unbeautiful. Her way of falling, unwillingly,
in love in the first place, and then holding
on to her victim lest he should escape her,
is eloquent with the gospel according to
Shaw. Rigby (the Bread of “Blue Bird”)
is a delight in the Waiter William part. A
trifle more antique than William needs to
be, perhaps, but full of understanding of the
author’s wit, and thoroughly artistic in his
expression of it. The scenery is George
Upward’s, and the staging is satisfactory
in all respects, save the luncheon table,
which should be placed on the bias instead
of length ways.

“The Girl Who Knew a Bit” is the forth-
coming drama at the King’s. She sounds
like a musical comedy, or a serio-chronic
ditty, but the advt. credits her with 16 thrill-
ing _ scenes, ineluding “dangers ashore and
perils afloat.” Meanwhile, Cyril Mackay is
leaping from the bowels of an Indian god to
rescue Miss Duggan from the sacrificial fire
where she has so many times been laid to
frizzle.
* * * ■-;<
In “The Time is Not Yet Ripe,” which re-
ceived a fairly respectable amateur perform-
ance at Melbourne Repertoire Theatre last
week, Louis Esson reveals a healthy young
talent for full-sized playwriting. When the
final scene has been reformed, his farcical
comedy might be better worth production as
a theatrical spec, than some of the stodgy
bellowdramas which have only their con-
ventional action to recommend them. There
is dramatic action enough in Esson’s play,
and the roar of human interest in the elec-
tion crowd of the third act would appeal to
the public that wants to be entertained. The
story of “The Time,” etc., is a chaotic
travesty of things political, the hero being a
squattah-socialist who advocates a two-
liours’ working day and compulsory Greek
education for the people, while the heroine
is a purely feminine daughter of a Liberal
Prime Minister who is compelled to be a
Good Woman candidate on the Hearth and
Home ticket. When she has defeated
the revolutionary squattah by SOOO votes to
170, she arranges to take him to Britain
with her father’s blessing on their union.
Between the utterly foolish Tories on the
one hand and the hot-air reformers on the
other, the plot would fall to the ground if it
were not supported by some happy satirical
thoughts in the dialogue. But the election
act would still stand on its own as a matter
of the first importance in which the poor
little plot is not vitally concerned. Esson’s
amusing imitation of Bernard Shaw’s up-to-
date method in the old Gilbertian manner
(or vice versa ) will presumably be repeated
in the next Repertoire session.
* * * *
Melbourne’s gastronomical outbreak (or
is it astronomical) begins next Monday.
Miss Mary Proctor will talk of the sun,
moon, planets and such matters in a popu-
larly scientific manner at the Athenaeum
Hall, and for six nights the bright little
lady, whose earliest memories are of tele-
scopes and constellations, will vary her in-
teresting discourses. Baker and Dix are
directing the tour, and Joe Brown, who has
adopted a star-gazing expression as J. Wal-
lace Brown, is seeing that the directions are
duly carried out.
* * * *
Mozetto, “one of the world’s finest jug-
glers,” arrived at the Opera House on Satur-
day. The name is apparently Dago for
Moses, and the performer’s strongest suit
is his manipulation of monish. He throws
half a dozen coins into the air simultane-
ously and catches them separately. Mr.
Thomas Dawson, who takes his jokes seri-
ously, has returned, and is provoking the
loud larf which earns him his beer and bread.
The De Serris company has been subtracted
from the bill, but the excellent Paxton
troupe, Fred Russell and the Stavordale
Quintet, still remain.

“The Snake Dancer,” a phantom of languid
poses and indiscreet appeals for admiration,
had a few minutes at West’s, Melbourne, last
Saturday night. During her turn on the
screen she appears to subject a gent to the
glance of giddy persuasion, but her legal
owner intervenes and prevents developments.
“The Old Actor,” which also stirred the emo-
tions of the bob and tanner “rush,” on
Saturday evening, was an item so bereft of
sin and duplicity that it would make an
admirable tale for Christian young men and
sentimental old ladies. “Pathe’s Animated
Gazette” supplied the usual glimpses of
royal blokes lifting their tiles to the abject
herd.

This has been Australian Week in Adel-
aide all right. The city has honored the
memory of McDouall Stuart, and banqueted
the survivors of his 50-years-ago party. Won-
dergraph is showing the films of ’ Birtles’s
travels, with the iguana devouring the snake,
and the seabirds flocking on the Barrier
Reef, and the native in peace and (appar-
). war. And now the Royal (where
Nellie Fergusson and George Cross and
others had previously been wasting some
good acting on a poor bellowdfamaj has
blossomed out with “On Our Selection.”
The playwrights have unfortunately thought
it necessary to graft same conventional melo-
drama on to Steele Rudd’s story. At the
Tivoli is Max Laube, who calls himself a
Human Bird, and the small Rees child ties
herself into knots, while papa and mamma
discourse- sweet music. At the King’s, the
Wynford brothers have been flitting over
pianos and into casks, the birdlike effect
being slightly discounted each time by the
twin thuds of a pair of heavy dumbbells,
cast back on the boards from mid-jump.
Biberti is a clever illusionist, and funny
withal; and Carrie Moore is as artistic as
ever. Tiny Town’s return visit has been a
success, especially when one midget lady
announced her wedding, and exhibited her
trousseau : and Edward Reeves, at the Vic-
toria Hall, has wrestled pluckily and suc-
cessfully, with “Othello,” single-handed.
YOU NEVER CAN TELL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
You never can tell
What may chance to befall from a pain in
the tooth
Of a bright little maid,
When her visit is paid
To a five-shilling dentist quite new to the
trade,
If her brother and twin
(A remarkable youth)
Like a feather flies in
To the den of the dentist, and asks him to
lunch
With their sister and mater
(Who come along later),
In a casual way at a breezy hotel,
Where William the waiter
Is fated to dwell,
And the young people’s pater—
A morbid old hater
Of modern diversions in manners of life—-
Being brought as a stranger,
Not dreaming of danger,
Is thrust in the midst of the family bunch.
To feed once again with his children and
wife
In a casual way at that breezy hotel.
Oh, you can never tell!
You never can tell
How a singular girl, who is courted in haste
By a jaunty young dentist of critical taste,
Will be suddenly stirred
By his amorous word
And the shocking surprise .of an arm round
her waist,
When his kisses rain warm,
And her sensitive form
For the very first time is profanely embrac’t.
You never can tell how Humanity’s tricks,
Turning “natural law,”
In a story by Shaw,
To the happy results of a family mix
At that breezy hotel
By the sea,
Will alter the orthodox course of events
By fooling a couple of solemn old gents,
And changing the trend of a father’s intents,
As though by a magical spell,
Dissenters agree
When a learned K.C.
(A son of the wonderful William who waits)
Removes a false nose
From the feature he blows,
And claims their respect for the case that
he states.
Sing hey hilly-holly,
The surface is folly—
There’s wisdom beneath ;
And, as William remarks to the puller of
teeth—
“Ah ! you never can tell!”
VICTORIA.
O.K.
AT POVERTY POINT.
Mary Proctor’s forthcoming lecture tour is not
wholly for self-enrichment. Part of the proceeds are
to go to the building of a solar observatory in this
part of the world, which is sadly lacking iii the
necessary facility for studying sun-spots. People
who have met the popular science lecturess speak of
her as a quite sweetly human litfle woman. The
Salonites booked her as “star” guest for last Monday
afternoon, and to-day (Thursday) the Gaud Mayor
gives her an “informal” reception in his apartment at
the Town Hall.
“Stargazer” : Anton Tchekhov’s “the Seagull,” re-
cently introduced to a Melbourne audience by an
amateur cast, was something more' than a failure.
The reason is not far to seek. Riftsian civilisation
is not our civilisation. Russian character is not our
character. Russian emotionalism is the product of
conditions foreign to us —many famines, much suffer-
ing, long tyranny, and the ferment of the down-
trodden in juxtaposition with the luxury of a domin-
ant plutocracy. This makes a play of Tchekhov’s
look to Australians more like a day in an asylum for
the Possessed of Devils than a page from life, unless
the acting is so intense as to impress the vitality of
the suffering portrayed during every moment of the
play. And Melbourne does not hold the amateur
who is capable of supplying that class of acting.
Where habits, customs and emotions are familiar, less
is demanded of the actor. The popular imagination
supplies deficiencies. But when the reverse is the
case, the man on the boards must be a devout student
and a powerful interpreter. Which is why I hold
that the Repertory Theatre, with the best intentions
in the world, does more harm than good in turning
its crude bo.y players on a vast proposition like this
Russian drama.
Six feet six inches of drink advertisements in a
theatre programme seems about the limit. That is
the achievement of the Sydney Palace “Sweet County
Kerry” programme. Seventy-eight inches out of
132 inches of available advertising space are devoted
to an appeal to the folk who lead a tight life, and
another six inches are an appeal to women who wear
a tight and breathless corset.
“0.K.”: The “theatre-packer” is seemingly doomed
to extinction-—in Victoria, at any rate. The local
Board of Health, somewhat late in the day, has de-
nounced him as a fiend, or suffocater, who compresses
about 10 per cent, more people into a certain breath-
ing space than it is licensed to hold, and an Act of
Parliament is threatened which will insure a fixed
allotment of sit-down for everybody who pays for it
—-“One Person, one Seat.” This will happen, per-
haps—agitation for reform of that sort being subject
to the possibility of sudden subsidence and ultimate
oblivion. If the “packer” is actually ejected from
his unhealthy sphere of industry, his departure will
not be unregretted by all who knew him. He is a
humorous incident—especially on first nights—-and
he has his sentimental bearings. He produces ripples
and roars of merriment, along with growls of blas-
phemy, in the crowded gallery. When the world
below wonders what the goddesses are laughing at,
the joke is due to the packer, who has spotted a
chance to make room for a little one between two
artful citizens, who are inclining towards one an-
other at an angle of 25 degrees, or is inserting a
stout lady into a place that would be a tight fit for
a starved child or a lost cat. And in his capacity of
agent for Cupid the business-like packer often supplies
a long-felt want. Affable young men approach him
with silver offerings and whispered requests. “Put
me among the girls,” or “Wedge me in next to the
plump little piece in green.” Also, girls indicate
to the packer the particular young men they would
like to be placed in juxtaposition with, and his ser-
vices in such cases are rewarded with a heartfelt
smile. It seems more than probable that the packer
has tended to promote marriages and births, as well
as enhance the death rate.
Alice Crawford has been appearing in Tree’s Shaks-
pearian revival in London. An Australian judgment
on Alice’s personation of Emilia in “Othello” is not
very favorable. “She gave quite a new rendering
and trailed about more like Dante’s Beatrice as
Rossetti painted her, than Shalcspeare’s traditional
heroine.” Which indicates the wilful eccentricitv
that is commonly alleged against Alice Crawford
nowadays. She presents commonplace characters in a
poetic decorative style. Emilia is essentially an
ordinary married woman of her period, with only a
fair amount of soul, and a bit of a tartar when
roused.
Never have London papers been more unanimous
in slating a new opera than in the case of ‘(The Chil-
dren of Don,” written by Lord Howard de Walden,
and produced at the lord’s expense by the New York
Jew, Hammerstein. Lord Howard de Walden is a
young man, who draws an enormous revenue in rents
from an estate in the most fashionaible district of
London. He is under the hallucination that he can
write lyrical poetry, and his “Children of Don” is
an outrageous attempt to versify an ancient Welsh
legend. It deserves 12 months’ tough labor without
the option. Some of the selections from the rich
man’s muse, given in the London papers, are ludi-
crous in the extreme. Composer Holbrooke could do
little or nothing with such stuff. The scenery and
staging are all that is praised by the critics.

BELIEVE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Dr. Chapman preached to-day on the Bible as an
inspired book which could not be ignored. He had
no time tor sceptics, he said. Let them believe a
thing fully, and the facts would come along after-
wards. —Report of Chapman-Alexander Mission.
Only believe. The facts will follow after.
This is our joyous method, proved and tried,
No room is here for scoff or sceptiy laughter,
Just start believing—set the redt aside.
Believe in what? Nay, nay, the facts will follow.
Don’t worry. Just believe, and let them go.
We do not ask what statements you can swallow,
But urge you to believing all you know.
Believe quite fully. Do not seek to reason,
All unbelief is now quite out of date,
The plain and solid facts will come in season,
Chasing hard after the collection plate.
Believe. Here is no room for idle chatter,
Science and logic can have nought to say,
What you believe in does not really matter
As long as you will start in right away.
Believe. Your hesitation is appalling,
When plain belief is such a simple thing;
Get to the job, bog in, and help in bawling
The songs that Alexander bids you sing.
Believe, believe! The facts are close behind you,
And any moment now they may be seen ;
Believe them straight away, in case they find you,
When they arrive, still doubting what they mean.
Believe at once. We’re open to receive in
Our Yankee fold all those who yearn for grace;
But miss this chance, the facts you don’t believe in
May be exceeding difficult to trace.
Therefore believe. The facts will follow after.
Our joyous method has been proved and tried,
In spite of faithless scoff and sceptic laughter,
Believing pays us, and the rest can slide.
N.S.W.

THE HATPIN ACT.

Two recent instances of the power of
“self-suggestion” have caused remark in
Melbourne. In one, a man betook himself
to the city hospital suffering from severe
internal pains caused by swallowing a set
of shop teeth. He could only be persuaded
that the doctors and the Itontgen rays appa-
ratus were right when somebody hastened
to the institution with the eaters he had
mislaid at home. A few days later a girl
happened along declaring herself almost in
articiilo mortis from carbolic acid poison-
ing, which liquid she had mistakenly swal-
lowed. Her mouth had been slightly burned
by the few drops she had taken, but none had
been swallowed, and on the doctor assuring
her such was the case, she immediately
became convalescent. But this self-sugges-
tion business is fairly common in other
places than hospitals. The average farmer,
frinstans, fancies he is the backbone of the
country, and can seldom be persuaded other-
wise. Big landlords believe the whole popu-
lation exists for their sole benefit and advan-
tage Affable Alfred has an idea that fervid
eloquence accomplishes great deeds. Spruce
Myth and Jokook imagine they are genuine
statesmen, and so forth. In fact, there is
bardly any limit to the power of self-
suggestion.
In Victoria’s Supreme Court lately the
Law spent most of a day inquiring into the
state and habits of a lady named Green or
Gawne, in so far as concerned her alleged
marriage. It seems that recently Gawne
was acquitted of a charge of larceny, and in
the course of his trial Mrs. Gawne swore
that she had been married to him for two
years. The State considered this perjury,
and charged her with that offence. In
defence, she put up a quaint plea that she
had in reality been married to Gawne
two _ years ago, but only in “the Scotch
fashion.” Now there have been many
“Scotch fashions” in marriage, including the
ancient one of leaping over a broomstick, the
Gretna Green style, and the other one in
which the groom only married the lady if
she first bore him a son. But the particular
“Scotch fashion” referred to was one in
which the contracting parties joined hands
in the presence of witnesses, and agreed to
become man and wife for the balance and
remainder _of their days. The lady was
cross-examined at great length about this
ceremony, and adhered to her opinion that
it was a proper and complete wedding,
though she couldn’t explain why she subse-
quently got herself and Gawne married by
a Methodist parson, or why she then
described herself as a spinster. It was a
scrappy, unfinished sort of case, and Scotch
marriages are extinct now; and a Scotch
marriave perpetrated outside of Scotland
doesn’t seem to be Scotch or to have ever
been Scotch. Still, the jury acquitted the
lady of perjury. After that, his Honor
curtly intimated that the State was wasting
its time, and that a similar charge against
Gawne might as well be dropped.

“Harrison O.”: What is wrong with the
female hatpin being not merely shortened by
legal process, but placed in the same cate-
gory as sling-shots, knuckledusters, fire-
arms and life-preservers? At the Norwood
(Adelaide) Oval the other day, a male who
appears to have umpired a football debate
not wisely but too well, had one of these
accursed gauds jabbed into his arm when he
was returning to the dressing-room. Blood-
poisoning set in, and the Lord alone knows
what is going to follow. Footballers, John
Hops and theatre “packers” regard the
hat-spear or the tile-pike more appre-
hensively than blue metal. Let one be-
come unpopular with any crowd which in-
cludes a large number of females, and he
lias just as much hope as a small lion in a
den of hungry Danielesses. His only chance
then is to whirl a scythe rhythmically around
him to the tune of a snuffling rhyme, and
thus clear a patlr—but, then, he may not
have his scythe along with him at the
moment.
The man that hits a Sydney tram con-
ductor is a fool—unless he is something
extra in the boxing division. A young man
made this discovery last week. ' He made
rude remarks to the conductor when asked
to pay his fare, and threatened to strike
that inoffensive-looking young man. Later
on he carried out his threat. The next
thing he knew was that he was on his back
in the street, and a constable was taking
him in charge. As the Yankees say, “he felt
sore,” so sore that he got angry, and, rising,
he smote and kicked the constable so severely
that the officer had to go to bed for six days.
At the critical moment another constable
appeared, and made things lively. The
accused was willing, and dusted the street
with the second constable. He was going
strong when the mild-looking conductor
smote him scientifically on the point, and
when he came round he was securely hand-
cuffed, and the two damaged constables
landed him in the jug without any serious
difficulty. The majority of Sydney tram-
conductors belong to the Tramways Athletic
Club, and are trained in the use of their
fists. They are not taught to box but to
rough and tumble. Generally the conductor
roughs and the abusive passenger tumbles —
first into the street and second to his mis-
take.

A concoction, that Victoria's Board of
Health is taking a mild interest in, further
emphasises the desirability of the Common-
wealth insisting that the ingredients of
every blatant medicine shall be stated on
the bottle or package. The contraption
purports to be “the remedy for the world;
cures blood-poisoning, pleurisy, pneumonia,
erysipelas and all inflammations, typhoid
and other fevers, and cancer in all its
varied forms.” Analysis showed it consisted
of mostly water, a certain amount of starch
and some scraps of borax. The price of
this precious remedy for cancer, typhoid
and other ills was not stated, but the paten-
tee would probably be satisfied with a
modest half-crown per bottle.

guv’nor, and ’e can go round
with the ’at,” said he. Another
two who were looking for open-
ings in Melbourne pubs, re-
cently, had brought a ravishing
new trick from Europe, and by
its agency they hoped to simply tear money
oft the ingenuous Australian. It was played
with thimbles and a pea.
What the Japanese papers call “regret-
table incidents” have occurred in connection
with the late elections. Mr. Kusaha, con-
servative candidate for Wakamatsu, seems
to have left no worm unturned to get into
Parliament. He bribed the postal olßcials
to hold up all his opponent’s letters; he
bribed the police to arrest’’his rival’s can-
vassers on various charges; and he armed
his own canvassers with revolvers. On
polling day his minions attended at the
booths and threatened to shoot straight if
the electors _ didn’t vote straight. “Mr.
Kusaha,” it is reported, “was returned with
a considerable majority.” The Tokio news-
papers say that the scandals were not
nearly so bad as at former elections!
THE STRIKE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was announced in a telegram from Venice on
Wednesday, that the gondoliers of the Grand Canal
had gone out on strike. — Cable.
The bright Italian moon
Shines on the long lagoon,
And illuminates the Palace of St. Mark;
And the Campanile's bells,
And the old Venetian smells
Are blending and ascending through the dark !
(Oh, harl:!)
’Tis the joyous note we hear
Of the merry Gondolier—
In his gondola he twangs the light guitar;
Care to Blazes he has cast,
And his gondola is fast
To the doorstep of a two-centesimi bar!
(Oh, look!)
And he grins a happy grin
At his wine so red and thin,
And he grounds the bar, and yells a loud Hooray;
For he’s out on strike (you bet!)
And the minimum is set
For fourteen hours, at one-and-four a day!
( Oh, my ! )
You can hear the tourists yell
Up and down the Grand Canal —
They can stop there till the moon sets, if they like!
Venice streets are very wet,
So the minimum is set
And the gondoliers are bound to win the strike!
(Oh, yes !)

The North Sea fisherman who staggers
about on a frail, wooden craft, and hauls
the cheap salt food out of tlie depths, never
knows liow many hundred warships are
blundering round him in the mist and fog.
But he doggedly pursues liis humble, wet
calling in the sure knowledge that two
immense, watchful and heroically reckless
fleets are exercising and rehearsing for the
inevitable hour of combat. He doesn’t mind
so long as they miss his matchwork bulwarks
by the width of a herring, and refrain from
steaming through liis precious nets. But
they don’t always refrain. A week or so
back, a chorus of Dutch howls split the fog
when something like 100 British fighting
craft foamed along through the nets of the
fish-farmers of Holland. It must have been
a liarrowing cruisers,
destroyers and submarines smoking proudly
and swiftly . past on a sea thrashed white,
and wild, raw Dutchmen in ridiculous gar-
ments, -dancing and screaming on foolish
little craft which rolled and pitched heavily
in the- 'confused water. The North Sea
fisherman's lot is only comparable to that of
the man who tries to grow potatoes in a
prize A'ihg while the preparations for the
big congest -are ;being made.

Sydney’s old maritime friend, the steamer
Changsa, rblled into port last week,- and
landed a few people who saw Spruce Myth’s
gamboge friends in action during the recent
political boil-qver in Canton. The proceed-
ings are described as “indescribable” —

that the eye-witness fneutioned the murder
of womfen and children almost cheerfully.
Presumably those who were murdered
plainly p.nd promptly, with rifle barrels
thrust against their bodies, were the lucky
ones. Whilst gathering his baggage toge-
ther, the returned voyager mentioned that
the murderers khew all about Australia’s
Alien Restriction Act, and promised “all
sorts of vengeance”; also that they were
not granting any concessions, to foreigners—
not even to Spruce himself. When the white
passenger 1 staggered thankfully down the
gangway on to the upper crust of his un-
guarded continent, the reporter accosted one
of the 52 Chinese who came back on the
ship. And the poor exile of Cathay said
with tears in his voice: “Me glad come home
again—l belong Sydney now, my word!”
It was over the above-mentioned political
boil-over, by the way, that a British press-
man tried to sell The Bulletin some photos
of Chinese executions. They were mostly
crucifixions, and ■ the women had their
breasts sawn off, while the knee-pans of the

was done. Also,'/very little lunch was done
in the office that day/

“Iyles Innison” : Feeling a dumb, aching
spiritual hiatus, I wandered into Alexander
and Chapman’s show in the Sydney Town
Hall. According to the unreliable daily
papers, the magnetism of these Yankee soul-
savers was unprecedented, irresistible, un-
canny. But I was disappointed. Those
booming daily papers proved to be the leg-
pullers I’d always suspected them to be. I
heard some very ordinary singing, accom-
panied, or interspersed, or emphasised, or
something, by Alexander’s Vociferous right
foot. I looked around me to’see how the
leaven of grace was working. The man on
my right, his face ambushed behind a wil-
derness of whiskers, was asleep. Immedi-
ately behind me someone, who had evidently
tendered a large coin for a hymn book, was
accusing one of the salesmen of giving him
the wrong change. Then Chapman preached.
I had been told that a theological platitude
never passed between his lips. I could
understand why. He spoke through his nose
—mainly about “Noo Yark.” At the end of
the last lap he requested all those who, felt
the seed of salvation germinating to stand.
A few assumed the semi-perpendicular —hesi-
tatingly and uncomfortably, as though the
floor was laid with hot bricks. Then Chap-
man spoke an advertisement about a book
that could be bought at the door for a shil-
ling. The man at my right woke up from
behind his ambuscade of whiskers. “A won-
derful man,” he observed.. I concurred,
grabbed my gamp, and running the blockade
of what seemed like 17,000 salesmen offering
books at a bob a time, I passed out into the
sunlight.

“O.C.G.” writes: —
The decision of the U.S. courts that Harry Thaw,
the murderer of Stanford White, is insane, ought to
end . a criminal scandal by Thaw’s detention for life.
The spectacle of Thaw, the luxurious, non-moral
Pittsburg millionaire, walking about, a free man
among men, after his cold-blooded and atrocious
murder of Stanford White, man of genius and alleged-
debaucher—such a spectacle would have caused the
godly to scoff and the ungodly some cynical indigna-
tion.' Perhaps it is just as well for Thaw that he
can’t get out, for the dead man’s friends are many
arid long-memoried, and the odds would have been
; against Thaw living to claim the Octogenarian Club’s
, gold medal. Thaw (who escaped electrocution by
swearing, or getting others to swear, he was mad)
will be punished either way. If he is mad his pun-
ishment will be tedious; if he isn’t mad it-will be
hideous.
The Victorian Artists’ Society grows
crudely commercial in its old age. Its smoke-
nights of yore were free, gratis, and enlight-
ened gatherings which strove to pay the gas
bill by the profit on liquor consumed on the
premises, the society for the nonce being an
unlicensed victualler who sold drinks, or en-
gaged a law-breaker to supply the waht.
There is no such lawless Bohemianism now-
adays. Teetotal patrons of art brought no
grist to the mill, anyhow. If they drank not
nor purchased cigarettes, there was no wealth
in them. Under the up-to-date system, the
Wowsers provide the prof, whilst the thirsti-
est Souls presumably represent a dead loss.
Last Saturday night members were exhorted
to come at the Galleries and bring then-
friends. The invitation card said “No money
will be taken for refreshments, but an
entrance charge of one shilling will be made
for each guest. Lady members are asked to
pass their cards, on to Gentlemen Friends.”
The simplicity of the artist nature is sweetly
expressed in the suggestion that every young
sister of the brush should send her best boy
to a smoko on a Saturday night, that he
might be happy- “on ffiis own” for a “bob,”
instead of shouting her to the theatre.

“A.B.” leans over the office bar, and muses
grimly':—
And so the ketch Candidate, and her crew of half a
dozen, have gone where most of us said she would
when the first big blow smote her. The little
hooker was a rare survivor of the mosquito fleet that
staggers up to the N.S.W. Northern rivers behind a
southerly and back again with a north-easter astern,
carrying logs and sawn stuff for the Timber Com-
bine. Every year sees one oi* two of these frail craft
lost or “sold to the islands,” and fortunately they
are not being replaced. This is the second crew that
the Candidate has drowned. About 16 years ago it
turned over off the coast with fatal effects. The hull,
or coffin, was recovered.

On July 24, 1862, McDouall Stuart
struck the Indian Ocean or the Arafura Sea,
being the first known white man to cross
Australia, south to north, and next day he
hoisted the flag. So Adelaide, which had
equipped him and sent him off the previous
December, last Thursday banqueted the sur-
vivors of that expedition. For a jubilee,
they rolled up surprisingly well. Stuart him-
self lived only to 1866. His right hand was
crippled just at starting, he covered much
of the distance in a litter, and on his re-
turn he was a wreck. Kekewick, Water-
house, Thring and Frew are dead; but Auld,
Billiatt, King, Nash and McOorrery survive,
and all but Billiatt (who is in England)
were present at the banquet. They have
done much better for themselves in after life
than most explorers (Pat Auld, for instance,
founded the big wine business that is named
after him) ; but Premier Peake announced
that a sum would be put on this year’s esti-
mates to increase the comfort of any of the
veterans who need it; also that certain new
“hundreds” in land now being cut up would
be named after them. At the banquet were
shown a shoe of Stuart’s horse, made into an
inkstand, and (in a bottle) a scrap of the
dried meat carried by the party in Central
Australia. Adelaide already has a statue of
Stuart in Victoria Square, facing that of
Colonel Light, the city’s founder; and pro-
poses to finish off with one of Sturt, whose
pupil and great admirer Stuart was. In fact,
the hill which is nowadays always called
“Central Mount Stuart” was actually named
“Sturt” by the younger man on discovering
it; but, all things considered, the modern
name seems the more fitting.
“Iyles I.”: I took a stroll down Lil’lee
Bourke-street, Melbourne, on a recent Sab-
bath night, and beheld one of the most
grotesquely pathetic futilities of the age. A
group of missionaries, aided and abetted by
three or four elegantly-dressed Chows, was
noisily endeavoring to “save” the residents
of the almond-eyed thoroughfare. A dozen
or so white men stood around, talking
mainly about the previous day’s horseracing
and football. An endless procession of
Chinks went up and down the dimly-lighted
street. They walked straight through the
circle of grace, and seemed totally unaware
of the missionaries’ existence. Some of
them, I discovered later, were going to meet
tbeir lil’lee whi’ girls in the awful blind
lanes that intersect “Chinese Avenue.”
Others were off “to play the game.” Right
opposite the meeting an old Chow sat in the
recess of a hallway and smoked a long
opium reed. If the music and the testi-
monies outside reached his crinkled ear .he
made no sign. But it was plainly evident
that no white god could bring him the peace
of mind that he sucked through the hiatus
of the reed. Yes, if any inquisitive person
really wants to see the most stupendous
futility of Melbourne’s many and assorted
futilities let me recommend Lil’lee Bourke-
street any Sunday night. Why those mis-
sionaries persevere is one of the world’s
mysteries, for I don’t believe that they save
a Chinaman’s soul in 275 years.
* * * *
“John Dishragge”: Marianne left us
about a fortnight ago, and proceeded to the
altar. Now the wife and myself are grap-
pling with the housework and inserting
cries for help in the “Situations Vacant”
column. The cries have gone unanswered
so far, and we continue to broom, and dust,
and wash up, and dash broken victuals into
the sadly overladen dustbin. I make fires
gladly enough, and sweep the yard with
something approaching enthusiasm ; but the
washing-up maddens me —also the missus.
Night after night I am struck by the way
our conversation degenerates whilst juggling
with greasy water and slippery crockery.
No matter how cheerful we are when we
start we quickly begin to speak uncharit-
ably of people—mostly better-off people with
servants. We magnify our own troubles,
speak ill of departed Marianne, repeat all
current scandal concerning those in high
places, and hang up the washing-up tin with
a crash, convinced that the world is a rotten
place. Messing about with broken food and
soiled plates is ruinous alike to intellect and
morals. I don’t mind much so long as it
isn’t my intellect and my morals. Suppose
you don’t happen to know of h girl who is
willing to sacrifice these luxuries in return
for 14s. per week?
By the Oldest Inhabitant: —
A house historic will come under the auctioneer’s
hammer towards the end of August—Byron Lodge,
Randwick, originally built by Isaac Nathan, a dis-
tinguished musician, who was the composer of
Hebrew Melodies. He was a friend of the poet who
sang about wine and Greece, hence the name given
to the house. He was father of the late Dr. Nathan,
and grandfather of Alfred Nathan, solicitor and vocal-
ist, and of the wife of the University Chancellor, Nor-
mand MaeLaurin. John Tait, of much turf fame,
occupied Byron Lodge for many years, and on a
trade adjoining the house were "trained most of his
famous winners. When John Tait gave up racing he
sold Byron Lodge and went to live at Lewisham on
the Boulevard.
BACKING OUT.
“And do you ever think of marriage, Mr.
Loanlee? ”
“Oh, yes, often —with a shudder !”
That exhaustive person, Victoria’s police
chief O’Callaghan, has given up expatiating
on the diminution of crime and the effici-
ency of the force. The latest feat of the
gangs of thieves who pervade Melbourne has;
been to lift a large section of the cargo of a
coastal steamer lying peacefully at the
wharf, the said section being enough cases
of raisins and currants to fill several carts,
and valued at £250. Probably the buccaneers
would have lifted the steamer itself had they
known a reliable buyer of steamers, houses
and similar awkward property. In fact,
the blessed wharves, and even the Yarra,
would very likely have been prigged before
this had a trustworthy receiver been avail-
able.

“E.S.S.”: “The returning exiles!” said a
passenger, in allusion to himself and companions, as
the coach extended the dull, grey miles behind us,
and the veil of haze was drawn over the austere face
of Tongowoko and its neighboring counties. The
people of the eastern, southern and northern regions
were wont to style that part the Gates of Purgatory,
and term its inhabitants the exiles of Outback. But
the speaker was not as happy as a stranger to that
land of surprises would have expected; and one of
the returning exiles, a lady, was crying—crying be-
cause she was leaving for ever the pregnant, brooding
spaces where the sun goes down. They had gone to
that corner to make money, with the intention of
getting out of it again as speedily as possible; and
subtly, unconsciously, the place had gripped them.
Not with joy, but with a pang of regret, they saw
the glinting rock-caps of Mount Browne disappear
behind the obscuring curtain. A more striking con-
trast to the cold green isles beyond the Atlantic
could hardly be imagined; yet English, Irish and
Scots were scattered all over it, a great many of them
in a position to purchase an estate in their native
land, and to live well on the interest of their money
for the rest of their days. But you couldn’t drag
them out of it. Australians, too, who had tasted
the sweets of the coastal climes, turned back, in spite
of all the disadvantages, to that siren with the aus-
tere countenance and burning tresses. We sai as
much to the man who had called us “the returning
exiles,” and he closed the argument by saying they
were mad. Two years afterwards he was carrying
between Wilcannia and Warri Warri the extreme
corner ; and carrying, out there, was about the most
trying occupation he could have engaged in.
“Old Drover” deposes: “The worst kind
of stock to travel with is pigs. If you take the bad
travellin’ points of the turkey and the goose an
mix ’em together, the pig can still go one worse.
You can’t lead or drive a mob of pigs like any
other animals. They go along grunting an’ rooting
an’ foragin’ all the time; you’ve got to work every
yard of the way to keep them movin’ on the right
track. Stop a minute an’ they’ll stop; try to
bustle them an’ they’ll split in all directions an’
say ‘Whugh.’ An’ when they come to a bog or a
waterhole they’ll plough in an’ lie there. You can
shout yourself hoarse, an’ caper around an’ pelt ’em
till you’re knocked up, an’ all you’ll get from them
is a grunt. They don’t take much notice of dogs
either; they stand an’ smack their chops at a dog,
or run backwards and say ‘Whugh.’ You’ve got to
go in an’ root them out; if it’s a hot day, an’ they’re
a bit tired, you’ve got to pretty well drag them out.
An’ they’re not animals that keep any regular hours.
They don’t stampede at night time, and they get up
at any hour to go rootin’ about for something to
eat. A black pig is hard to see in the dark, an’ he
goes moochin’ off so quietly that it’s the simplest
thing out to lose him off camp. On long trips you’ve
got to take one or two waggons piled full of pump-
kins or corn. You camp at a good watering place,
an’, after a feed of pumpkin or corn, they’ll settle
down fairly well for the night. In the morning,
when they’ve got used to the track, they’ll be nosin’
an’ gruntin’ around the waggons for breakfast, an’
makin’ trouble with the cook. They’re always ready
for tucker-time; you can make ’em finish a hard
day well by going ahead an’ ringin’ the dinner bell.
I carried a big football on one trip. When they were
extra cantankerous I’d get away in front, call out
‘Pig, pig, pig!’ an’ start bouncin’ the ball hard.
At the second thump I’d hear ’em cheering, and then
they’d come along, with their ears flappin’, as if
there was nothing they enjoyed so much as brisk
trottin’ exercise on the overland. They thought I
was breakin’ up pumpkins.”

“Vagabond”: The “creeping sand” of
Raglan, on the West Coast of Maoriland, is a weird
sort of nuisance. It
has climbed the side
of a hill at the en-
trance of the . port,
flowed up over the
shoulder and down
again round a shal-
low valley. It is now
rapidly ascending the
next hill, which is
good pasture land. It
isn’t blown on to the
places it infests, so
far as one can see; it
just clambers hungrily
up out of the sea, and
flows gradually but
persistently over the
face of the earth. If
you plant a brick wall
or a bulldog in its
path, so much the
worse for them. They
are buried with expe-
dition and despatch.
The local theory is
that the sand is the
continuation of the
bed which rises up
and forms the bar
outside the entrance,
and that the breakers
beating on the bar
keep jostling the
grains of sand along
over the bed, till
they’re crowded out on
to the beach, and the
continued push-on
movement keeps shov-
ing the grains upward
and onward. How-
ever, the theory of
the tragedy doesn’t in-
terest the local farm-
ers. The sand being
no earthly use to bird,
beast, fruit or flower,
the problem which
concerns them is how
to check its onrush.
“Billy T.” : Lara is the Victorian national
amphitheatre where the local dipso, conducts gladi-
atorial ' combats with his personal devils, and van-
quishes them with great slaughter. I once met a
couple of salvaged alcohol-lovers, on their way from
Lara to a life of teetotalism. Their converse impres-
sed me. “Never again, Ned,” said the more earnest-
looking of the pair, leaning across the railway carri-
age to tap his friend resolutely upon the knee. “Not
on your life, Horace.” “The hell broth nearly did us in ;
but we’re done with it, old sport.” “For good ’n’
ever.” They shook hands on this much with an iron
grip. “See here, Horace, I wouldn’t give that for a
tubful.” “Ned, I could screw the neck of a bottle
of Scotch and pour it over a dog.” “I don’t want
it.” fit’s gone clean out o’ my life.” “Filthy
muck!” “Bilge!” “When I think of the good lads
it’s outed”—“When I see the cross between a rooting
hog ’n’ a sick cat it makes of a man”—“l could take
a bundle of bombs, ’n’ blow down every pub in crea-
tion.” “I feel I’d rather wear a sack in a monkery
for the rest o’ me days than get back to the old bar-
roosting game again.” They parted affectionately,
with further declarations of their determination to
lead the simple, salubrious life, having arranged to
meet for mutual congratulations a month later.
And meet they did in due course—but they were not
alone. Ten thousand yellow fantods, and gaudily
striped snakes, to the number of 757, were with
them; and the venue was Lara—the place they had
left in a cured state, a month previously.

Descendants of one of the imported pests
that misguided people turned loose on this country,
are harassing Melbourne’s new Benevolent Asylum
at Cheltenham. The asylum bosses went to a lot of
expense to construct an ornamental lake, and
planted grasses, palms and shrubs round it; also,
they placed wild and tame ducks on the

THE OLDEST INHABITANT: "Well, Mister , I

roof or table of the fluid. The old people
were to recline on the grassy banks and
admire the fowls; also, there was to be roast duck
on the menu when the natural resources permitted.
But the imported curse willed otherwise. lie dwelt
in the heathy country around, and wore a bushy
tail and an overpowering smell. Pervading the lake
at night he sampled a duck or two, and, finding
them palatable, ate the lot. The management grew
some more, and set a gardener on watch with a gun.
He had two shots. The first killed a dog, the
second nearly slew the manager as he went to in-
spect-the first victim. Ducks are now off at Chel-
tenham, and the directors are stocking the lake
with fish, eels and other edibles. But it doesn’t feel
sure that some imported abomination won’t pre-
sently happen along and eat them, too.
“Pakiri” : Three years ago a partner and
I ran a flax-mill out at Mangakahia (Maoriland).
We cut out the green flax for a distance of 50
miles up and down the river, cleared a swamp or
two, and then looked round for more plunder to feed
the stripper. There was a lake, away up on Maori
ground, with something like 2000 tons of splendid
flax grdwing on the banks. We’d had
our eye on it all along, so we went up to
interview the chief. Old Titikowarau
was discovered in a slap-up bungalow,
and we did, or tried to do, our business
in a drawing-room furnished in very-
good taste and supplied with good pic-
tures and a first-class piano. Young
Titi was reading for his 8.A., and the
old man’s daughter was a graduate of
Te Aute College. We offered him £2OO
cash for the right to the flax, and pro-
mised to give as many of the tribe as
liked the chance of cutting at 7s. a day.
It was no go. “T’at lake tapu,” the old
chap said. “Suppose te Maori go cut,
Taipo come out, ketch ’im in te scruff
an' hoi’ his head under till he dead. No
fear. Plenty taipo t’at lake. Full of
taiposl” (ghosts). We sprung the price
another £5O, and offered to get whites
to do the cutting. The old man’s face
went chalky-white under the temptation,
and his fingers twitched to handle the
notes. But he was staunch. Old Titi
departed suddenly for Te Reinga (or
elsewhere) six months afterwards, and
his son reigns in his stead. Others have
been after that flax since, bettering our
price; but the Maoris won’t part. The
young chief’s B.A. degree is modern, and
his clothes are modern, but his religion
is the old article. He won’t admit it,
and he gives no reason for refusing to
make money out of the haunted lake,
but he is thinking of the old hereditary ghosts just
as his father did.
“Tamumu” tells how ITenare wpnt duck-
shooting:—“You know te lake at Te Auk? All ri’ ;
one time me and Wi go there to shoot te tuck. We
paddle te canoe all over te lake, but te tuck he clear
out too quick every time, before we can get te shot.
By-and-bye we see te big log floating o-n te lake, and
I say to Wi, ‘No plurry good this way. I go on te
log, and te tuck he no frighten then ; he think me
all te same te log, and I get plenty shot.’ Wi he
say, ‘All ri’ ; so I get on te log, and Wi he go away.
Long time I stop on te log, but I never get te shot.
I think this te por game, better I have te smoke.
So I get te knife and te tobac, and I fill te pipe. I
think now I get te good smoke, but I never get him.
I stick te knife in te log, and, by cripes, te log he
dive, and I dive too —he not log at all; he te plurry
big tuna (eel). By korry! I swim ashore quick. I
never like to be all te same that fellow Jonah.”
THE MODERN MAGDALENE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Considerable discussion arose at a committee meet-
ing of the Agricultural Society yesterday concerning
a motion dealing with the definition of heifer in the
show schedule. There is a difference of opinion
amongst exhibitors at local shows as to whether a
cow under three years having a calf is a heifer or a
cow. It was eventually resolved that all female
cattle under the age of three years, with or without
calf, should be termed heifer.
—A N.S.W. country news item.
O sainted Cow, we did not mean to hurt
Your dignity, or tarnish your good name;
We did not mean to say you are a flirt,
Whose gay flirtations culminate in shame.
We beg your pardon. If we gave offence,
It was because we were a little dense.
But lest your sister—poor unfortunate!
Should pluck the blossoms from the primrose
ways—
So narrow, and ordained inviolate,
We’ve made this written law for when she strays:
Although her feet forbidden-ward shall go,
She shall be counted pure as Alpine snow.
Should Fate and Nature secretly decree
That prematurely she her prince will find,
To all things, save her stainless pedigree,
We’ll ever be con-ven-i-ent-ly blind.
No Mother Grundy shall covertly spy
Upon her loves, to shame her by-and-bye
We shall not see the token of her fall;
But rather shall her tumble be divine.
She shall not taste abandon’s dregs at all;
But laurel tendrils shall her horns entwine.
’Tis true mere mortals wear no diadem
For sin—but you’re superior to them.
Henceforth you’ll wear a halo all the time;
We’ve glued it on your spike-surmounted head.
You’re Simply Perfect; no clandestine crime
Shall weigh upon your virgin heart like lead.
Forgive us if we dared imply a taint
Was on your character; for you’re a saint.
N.S.W.
R. J. CASSIDY.
