“Kodak”: It was in that curious region
called Back-o’-Sunset that I encountered them again.
The blatant ruin of a gentleman bellowing outside
the booth I at once recognised as A. William-
Cann “The Australian Hercules ” was none
other than Jimmie Pannikin. A. W.-Cann,
after bragging in the most reckless fashion of his
humble confederate’s marvellous strength and en-
durance, called upon Hercules to come forth. Where-
upon there stepped into view the most bashful and
bespangled, and (if I may say so) the most betighted
human being I have ever seen. He looked about
him with a ghastly grin, and then, at the bidding of
his brutal master, started to go through certain un-
easy contortions with A. W.-Cann alleged were muscle-
making inventions of his very own. Meanwhile he
read out in a pompous voice the alleged best per-
formances of the unhappy Pannikin: “Hercules has
thrown the hammer 100yds. A world’s record!
Hercules has supported nine horses on his chest for
10 minutes. A world’s record! Hercules has run
100yds. in Bsec. A world’s record!” He only de-
sisted when a dirty youth asked him “if ’Ercules
had ever pulled his leg, and if it was another
world’s record?” However, he covered his confu-
sion by announcing “Hercules, the real ‘White Hope,’
will now give a demonstration of punching. So
terribly strong are his arms that no living fighter
can hope to survive a round with him. If Johnson,
whom we are now after, can be induced to enter the
ring, rest assured, gentlemen, that the Championship
of the World will come back to the race that pro-
duced Wellington and Nelson.” This magnificent
speech won over the crowd again, and the tall and
bulky-looking Pannikin, who had been pulling on a
pair of punching gloves, bowed almost cheerfully and
turned to the leather-faced machine. A respectful
hush fell on the company as he raised his meaty fist
to strike. A. William-Cann leant excitedly forward
and peered at the dial. Crash! The machine stood
unmoved and Pannikin stepped back with a jarred
expression. But Cann leaped into the air and
shouted “Eight-een hundred and forty pounds! Gen-
tlemen, a world’s record!” The announcement was
received with laughter and applause. Cann was
about to enthuse some more when a small, insignifi-
cant navvy climbed clumsily into the ring without
invitation, and asked if he could have a punch.
Cann looked at him and laughed in a condescending
way. “Cer-tainly, my man! Cer-tainly! ” The
crowd sniggered and A. Cann flushed and said some-
thing under his breath. The navvy slowly peeled off
his coat and revealed a sort of awful baboon’s back
fitted with branch-like arms. Carefully folding and
placing his filt;:y garment in his corner, he limped
up to the punching machine and carefully read all
the records printed on its face. Then, with a
patient, ape-like look, he considered his knotted
right hand and spat into it more in sorrow than in
anger. Followed another suspicious look at the
waiting machine, a quick, indrawn breath and a
crashing HIT! The face of the murdered old
machine fell forward; the back tottered backward;
and its steel entrails rattled and clattered all over
the stage. “Another world’s record!” howled the
amazed navvy, his nobby fist upraised. “No !” thund-
ered A. William-Cann, darting forward. “NO record!
The machine is destroyed! What proofs have you
that you have made a record ?” The indignant
crowd fairly rose at this; but the swindled navvy
simply pointed at the ruins. The honest old ape, or
whatever he was, relied in his innocence on William’s
sense of justice to admit his claim. “No record!”
howled the desperate old fraud. Pannikin all this
time was looking extremely unhappy in a remote
corner. “No record” persisted the scoundrelly old
showman; till at last it dawned on the navvy that
he was being done. With a roar of rage, and a
reddened eye, he lumbered straight at Hercules.
Heaven only knows what would have happened, had
not a weedy little woman pushed to the front and
screamed, “ ’Ere, don’t yew ’it ’im ! I ain’t goin’
ter pay no more fines for yew! Yew come ’ome!”
The navvy stopped dead on the verge of murder,
wilted at the knees, and shambled guiltily towards
his unclean coat, while his frail little wife watched
in an agony of anxiety. When at last he clambered
safely out of the ring and stood miserably at her
side, she sniffed hysterically and started to upbraid
the badly-shaken A. W.-Cann and his terrified Hercules:
“Yew orter be ashamed o’ yerself!” she whimpered.
Gettin th’ father o’ eight ter risk ’is life competin’
with yer champeens an’ yer world’s record-ers.” The
crowd roared, but she went straight on, to the utter
confusion of the alleged strong man and his mana-
ger. “Wot’s goin’ ter become o’ me an’ them
eight kids if that there ’ulkin’ brute Herculers goes
an’ breaks ’is back for ’im ? W’y, a big, ’ammer-
throwin’ beast like that’s only fit ter ’sociate with
bullieks an’ things! Let ’im go an’ put some more
’orses on ’is big, fat chest if ’e wants ter make more
of is records, not go an’ get fathers o’ famleys ter
risk their precious lives ! W’y my man’s only outer
’orspittle two weeks—’e ain’t got ’is strength back
yet! An’ yet yew go matchin’ ’im with That! Yew
orter be ashamed o’ yerselves!” She then led her
husband away amid shrieks of merriment, and A.
William-Cann roughly shoved Hercules into the booth.
Can nothing be done to rescue Pannikin from the
clutches of this sinister old man ?
“Vulcan” : King Billy’s lady, who fre-
quently puts through her “interesting, event” job
in-a few hours, is by no means a lone circumstance.
A band of gipsies struck Uralla (N.S.W.), some
time ago. One black-eyed dame sought free pro-
vender from a local storekeeper, on the ground
that she had given birth to twins the previous night.
Inquiry proved that she was no liar, and she got
the fodder as a sort of reward for valor.

“S.S.S.” writes of lovely ladies: —“There
was Katarina, the Samoan, whose age would be after
30, and whose skin was slightly shrunk; yet she
looked and was of the Blood Royal. She walked
proudly into the little Island hospital, raised the
hem of her calico robe, and graciously indicated
where an “unhappy, grief” had resorted to her knee.
Katarina was that despised and rejected She, a
Samoan missionary, more or less nigger and wholly
barefooted. Yet we all felt that the making of her
medicine was no matter for a mere dispenser. The
overworked little doctor himself made her a! liniment,
and I a drink medicine. And when Katarina graci-
ously inclined her head by way of thanks we were
well paid. It was our humble tribute to the Sopl of
Beauty.... Marea, of the golden eyes, smiled sleepily
from her Manila mat under the frangipanis that blos-
somed above the little sandbeach beyond the scintil-
lant blues of Torres Strait. Gold were her eyes,
rose-ivory her skin, dark as the bronze on a bird o’
Paradise wing her rippling hair.’ At this loveliness
one gazed stare-eyed, in silence, as one gloats over a
perfect picture, and went away. There was nothing
to keep one. Her gift was beauty, without charm.
.... The 1 third I met in a Binundele village, some-
where south in Papua. Black she is, as the ace of
spades—a dusky Persephone, with wicked, mournful
eyes that she probably clutched from Cleopatra. She
is somewhere about 16, and has married—Papuan
fashion—and “chucked away” many, many men; and
many many men await that same fate. With poin-
sianas in her hair she sits and “sings” (it is like a
dog howling) on a hillside; and the men laze along
from miles around to listen—and, incidentally, learn.
Hers is the compelling charm of Absolute Individu-
ality. So it is in Papua and the Islands, as it is
everywhere else and ever was. Mere prettiness is
paltry. Beauty, without charm, is skin deep. Charm,
with or without beauty, is Supreme.
“W.8.H.” : Dunno about the rest of Aus-
tralia as a breeding ground for snipe, but I do know
and attest that the birds, or some of them, rear their
offspring in a swamp not 200yds. from where I am
writing this (Bundarra, N.S.W.). I have caught
plenty of young ones, either in the warm sand where
they delight to sun-bathe, or in the tufts of grass
where they hide when disturbed. And to this I take
my solemn and profound cuss, and attach my seal in
the presence of witnesses, as required by the Act.
“N or M”: Never heard, excepting from
“Tura” (B. 30/6/’l2), that there was such a thing
as a rabbit-cat, but I know the ’possum-cat, which
is not an excessively rare beast. Strange to say,
the hybrids are fertile. I have seen specimens with
characteristics almost entirely feline. A habit of
laying the tail flat along the back, ’possum fashion,
instead of half-masting it as an ordinary cat does

when crowded for room, was the most noticeable
difference. The first cross is, I believe, frequently
more ’possum than cat; the ears are rounded and
the fur has the peculiar ’possum bushiness, color and
smell. In one instance I knew of, the hybrid—a
bushy, short-legged beast—developed an inconvenient
taste for green peas, and became such a nuisance in
the garden that he died of old axe, which is worse
than galloping consumption. Another kitten of the
same batch had a curled tail. Cats sometimes adopt
and rear a ’possum joey, but that is not advanced as
a proof of affinity, for I have known a tabby to
mother an orphan chicken and two young ducks, and
almost wear her tongue out in an effort to keep the
family tidy.
THE OLD HAND.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In the town of Wagga Wagga, at the Golden tee
Hotel, ‘ b
’Mongst a lot of pleasant fellows after shearing time
I fell.
They were rouseabouts and shearers—some had come
from Mangoplah,
Some from Coree, some from Yanko, some from
Boomanoomanah.
There they talked of sheds and bosses, of damp
weather and of drought,
Strikes and cooks, and all the other things that
shearers talk about,
All save one who sat in silence on a cask behind the
door,
With his bluey in the corner and his eyes upon the
floor.
He looked careworn and dejected, so I went up to
his side —
“Have a drink, old man?” I asked him. “Righto,
matey,” he replied.
And we drank, and had another. Tom the barman
shouted, too,
And the liquor seemed to cheer him; for more
talkative he grew.
But he shook his head and answered—when I asked
him: “Do you shear?”
“ ’Twas a game I useter foller once, fer four months
every year.”
And he spent the rest, he told me, “earnin’ tucker,
weed an’ booze,
Up at Tumba-blanky-rumba, shootin’ kanga-blanky-
roos.” J
Then that man of many summers, who went shearing
as a youth,
Loosed a string of quaint bush logic that eohtained
a shred of truth:
“Times is changed, and men’s changed with ’em
things ain’t what they useter be,
When the old hands did the shearin’. Very few are
left to see.
Then we useter travel peaceful, packhorse cornin’ on
behind,
An’ if we’d to do some waitin’, well we never seemed
to mind.
“With a sort of cheerful music all the coves came
driftin’ through,
Jinglin’ hobble-chains an’ quart-pots, an’ a concertina
too.
Then the river-bends would echo to the chorus of our
song—-
Do you ever hear them ditties now, when shearers
pass along?
Nowadays its all a bustle, each one racing down the
track,
Just as if the very devil was a-proddin’ at his back!
An’ it seems to bring dejection—ah! I never had the
blues
Up at Tumba-blanky-rumba, shootin’ kanga-blanky-
roos.
“Yes; them other days were peaceful. Then a change
began to come;
On the old tracks leading shedwards you would hear
a kind o’ hum.
Then a cove would mizzle past yer, with a bluey on
his back,
Workin at his flamin’ pedals, like a spider on the
track.
An’ the fellers seemed to alter till the bush was
altered too—
But I’ll say this in their favor—to the union they
were true!
“An’ new changes kept arrivin’ till one day I seen
a tyke
Ruffin’ down the road like blazes on a gruntin’ motor-
bike !
you talk of toffs i —l didn’t have to travel very
far
When I seen a mob of shearers in a stinkin’ motor-
car !
An’ I knew the old-time shearin’ was a feature of the
past;
For the blades could go to smoke-ho !—the machines
was holdin’ fast!
An maybe you’l, live to see ’em makin’ time to
beat the trains,
Spdedin’ high above the dust-clouds in them flyin’
aerjqalanes!
Then there came a strict injunction ’gainst the
slayin’ of the ’roo.
He was gettin’ scarce an’ scarcer, an’ it had to
come, we knew.
It was proper to protect him, so we didn’t make a
fuss;
For we knew that he was goin’ like the native blacks
— an' us.
An’ the little towns arisin’ here an’ there about the
bush
Filled the campin’ grounds an’ crowded out the good
old-fashioned push.”
T ° ' va f.'i’ lst a worn old-timer, relic of a bv-gone day,
With his plaint against the present—it’s the olden-
timer’s way.
And I could not judge him harshly; for remember
you and I
May be railing at fresh progress when a few more
seasons fly.
he said, “I must be moving,” as his spell
was nearly done—
He was rabbiting, it seemed, on Eunonyhareenya
run.
But he wished that he was going, as we had a final
booze—
“ Back to Tumba-blanky-rumba, shootin’ kanga-
blanky-roos.”

PERSONAL
ITEMS
. Andrew Lang, the Jack-of-all-sorts of
literature, had some relatives in Australia.
Dr. W. H. Lang, of Corowa (N.S.W.), is
his brother. He, too, dabbles a bit in ink,
being the father of a novel called “The Thun-
der of the Hoofs,” an Australian yarn
which was never classed among the best
sellers. He has also executed a number of
tales and articles—mostly in staid weeklies.
Dr. P. H. Lang, of Lismore (Vic.), and
John Lang, of South Yarra, are both
nephews of the deceased Andrew.
“Harrison O.”: The Australian Ministry
is at present confronted with the serious
question of what to do with its O’Malley.
From the start, the long-haired Canadian
and rather bloated landlord has provided the
Tory press with more pegs upon which to
hang derisive animadversion than any three
other Laborites. His weird extravagances
and whirling oratory are bringing the party
into contempt, at any rate with many of the
unattached voters to whom he and his friends
owe their present positions. The preposter-
pus rhodomontade perpetrated by O’Malley
in the debate regarding the appointment of
Chinn to a transcontinental railway billet,
was the Wild Eagle’s most bizarre effort to
date. It was made up mostly of muck
worms, Pawnee maidens, guinea-pigs, blow-
flies, hybrids, Don Quixote, Sancho Panza
and other highly relevant circumstances. It
was _ altogether _ a fine example of vulgar
futility. And it was especially regrettable
coming from a member of the party which
has always set the standard of manners in
the House as against such shining, not to
say, deafening, Tory lights as Ryrie and
Willy Kelly.

1909, aged 7S years. He was the pioneer glass
manufacturer, and made the first pot of glass in
Australia, at. Darling Harbor, Sydney, on the nieht

Ihomas was born. “Surely he hath left his foot-

A ‘ Dobbie ’ def id in Adelaide at the

“S.T.S.” : Rc the deceased Andrew Lang.
It was he who wrote the famous London
Daily News yarn in 1884, begnning: “The
Champion Mean Man has often been the
subject of imaginings by Transatlantic hum-
orists. The London police are at present
searching for him.” And then it told the
story. Murdoch and Co. had collared the
English bowling at the Oval, the day before.
It was the celebrated innings in which every
man tbe side went on to bowi.
and brace personally filled every position in
the field, finally taking the gloves when
Lyttelton took them off. The stumper some-
how succeeded where the regular bowlers had
tailed, and took a bunch of Australian
wickets. Meanwhile a well-dressed man (who
was never caught) called at his house in a
cab. The Hon. Alfred Lyttelton would stay
to dine at the Oval; please send his dress
clothes and overcoat. They were handed
over—and never heard of again. “Mr. Lyttel-
t°n ls engaged in Saving his Country,” wrote
Andrew Lang; “and the Champion Mean
Man chooses that precise moment to sneak
his overcoat.”
Dear Bulletin,— l thought I noticed a
new acquisition to the House of Representa-
tives lately. A Mr. “Elliott” Johnson.
M. has been writing letters to the
press, and signing himself that way. Can >'t
be my old friend W. E. Johnson, the rabid
Single-taxer of the days of yore? There
seems to be a fashion growing up now of
discarding plain John and Bill as a political
or social name, and using a pompous second
name. It is an English device. When
“Bill” Reeves went to London from Maori-
land he was for some time Mr. W. P.
Reeves. Then he became Mr. W. Pem-
ber Reeves, then Mr. Pember Reeves,
and finally Sir Pember Reeves. He
halted on the threshold of the hyphen, so to
speak, for he was not so rich as the Tooths,
who became the Lucas Tooths, and then the
Lucas-hyphen-Tooths with a baronetcy.
Then there was my old friend plain Jack
Brunton,. the miller. - With a boom in flour
he became Mr. J. Spencer Brunton, and now
he has dropped the J., and develops into
Spencer Brunton. Another peculiarity in
Australian snobs “at ’ome” is the new pro-
nunciation of names. Thus, the Timothy
Coghlans have been disguised as the “Coai-
ans.” This lore will probably help to ex-
plain who Mr. Elliott Johnson, M.H.R., is.
Otherwise people might have thought he was
a new member.—Yours, Locust.
“Andrew Hogg” writes —and The Bulle-
tin cordially endorses :— i
What is wrong with filling the vacancy on the
N. S. Wales Public Service Board with a man who
has personal experience of country conditions ? In my
opinion the practice of handing over every attractive
job—whether in the Lands, Education, Justice De-
partments or what not—to city-dwellers, has been
largely overdone. Good men of the sort required are
easy enough to find. For example, Donald E. Fraser,
some time known and occasionally reviled, as “Jim-
mie Pannikin.” Fraser has a splendid record as an
inspector of schools. He has been an upholder all
his official life of Australianism as opposed to the
Decayed Fish cult, that was so fashionable in the
Civil Service until quite recent years. Long before
the Labor party interested itself in the question of
child slavery, “Pannikin” was plugging away on be-
half of the unhappy little servitors of the cow-bail;
and he has stuck to that nationally valuable, though
thankless, job ever since. He has been assailed pub-
licly and otherwise by agriculturists of the Patten
class—Patten, by the way, is president of the N.S.W.
Farmers and Settlers’ Ass., the body which Minister
Griffith asserted was composed mostly of agents and
squatters. Also, he has been cursed, with the utmost
heartiness, by Tory farmers, who hold it as an article
of faith that they have an inalienable right to work
their offspring to, death, if so disposed. Yet he has
gone placidly on denouncing and exposing bucolic in-
justice, with voice and pen, which is an indication
that his courage is all right anyhow. It is this type
of forceful, far-seeing and patriotic citizen that is
most needed on Public Service Boards and elsewhere.
The McGowen Government will do the State (and
more especially the country parts thereof) a notable
good turn by appointing Donald E. Fraser,

Among the McGowen Government’s pro-
posals for the new session is an increase of
the salaries of N.S.W. members. The sug-
gestion might come with a better grace if it
dicin t emanate from a Cabinet which, despite
cill its increased taxes and its huge borrow-
mgs, can’t keep out of the boghole of deficit
but that is a small matter. The main
point is that no increase should be allowed
I s P asse< i making it a punish-
able offence for any politician, during his
term in any elective House, to subscribe to
any religious, social or charitable movement
in his electorate—to contribute to churches
(barring his regular pew-rent, and not more
than bd. for the plate), cricket or football
clubs, race-meetings, sports of any kind,
widows orphans, beggars, drunks, or anv
form of charity whatsoever. Also, any
attempt to solicit the member for monev
should be punishable. Under the present
order of things, a very large proportion of a
members £3OO a year is reputed to go in
subscriptions of one sort or another. Some
give as a matter of real charity. Some give
out of careless generosity. The rest give in
order to buy votes —in other words, they are
blackmailed out of their salary under an
unspoken and unwritten threat that their
jobs will be taken from them if they don’t
pay. lliere is absolutely no guarantee*, under
present condtions, that even if the private

♦
A time like the present is often an Indian
summer sort of period with the banks. (The
Indian summer, it may be explained, is the
American term for a period of especial calm-
ness, beauty, warmth and radiance that
conies at the end of autumn, just before the
snow arrives, and the bottom falls out of
everybody’s contentment. It has some rela-
tion to that period when a beauty begins 'to
be described as a ‘'well-preserved woman,”
and isn’t yet called “the remains of a fine
female. ) When the boom is fading the
banks have to push up their deposit rates
in order to get money. They also shove up
their lending rates. But the increased in-
terest paid doesn’t cost the banks much for
a while. They hand out an extra \ per
cent, on the new deposits that come in, and
on the old deposits as they are renewed, but
it takes a year or two for all the old cheap
deposits to fall due for renewal. Also, the
current account money is still non-interest-
bearing as usual. On the other hand, the
price of advances goes up promptly all
iound. Thus for a while, at the beginning
of a period of gloom, there are big profits;
the money which a bank lends brings it in
much more, and the money that it borrows
costs it, for a time, hardly any more. And the
slump in the value of the bank’s securities—
the slump that always comes at such a time,
and which, in the end, usually eats up all
the extra earnings, and very often eats them
up several times over^ —has hardly begun yet.
It the depression arrives in real earnest, the
institution is liable to find that it has, in
many cases, been only adding an extra £1
per cent, on paper to a borrower’s bad debt,
and that a lot of the apparent new profit
never existed, but during the Indian summer
that is a case for the future. In theory,
and as a matter of appearance, the Indian
summer is _ a pleasant interval in financial
circles. Still, it is the sort of interval when
many a bank manager begins to notice an

accumulation of grey hairs or an extension
or the ravages and dilapidations of his bald
spot. He is something in the position of a
shipowner who is glad that his vessel is
heavily laden because it is earning more
money, and at the same time is darned sorry
it is so heavily laden because he sees bad
weather ahead, and fears that either the
vessel w’ill go down or a good deal of the
cargo will have to be thrown overboard.
ON THE RETIREMENT OF O’CALLAGHAN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Chief Secretary states that Mr. O’Callaghan
is quite ready to retire as soon as the Government is
ready to replace him. The difficulty, Mr. Murray
adds, is to get a suitable man. —News item.
When falls the baton from thy trusty hand,
A sigh like some expiring wind shall pass
Along the ranks of each broad-arrowed band,
And through the magsmen scattered o’er the grass.
Indoors and out each brow will furrowed be,
And on each man grey Care will set its trace.
One more O’Callaghan we cannot see!
Who will they get to fill thy topmost place?
A silence falls—likewise the penal tear ;
Another heart as kind does not exist;
Behind each “wanted” one may stalk blue Fear—
Steel bracelets deck each cold, unwilling wrist.
The shroud of Joy is wove on Sorrow’s loom !
Alas! Time Sikes has cracked the crib of Doom !
N.S.W.
CONSTABLE WINKING.
THE NEWEST “HOPE.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
According to the Sydney Sun, a difference o,
opinion between Colonel Rvrie and Senator Gardiner
was to have been settled by Marquis of Queensbury’s
rules in Snowy” Baker’s physical culture rooms.
But, to the great disappointment of a large audience
of legislators, the mill failed to eventuate.
Ihe game of politics is growing stale ;
We’ve wearied of the old cross-firing tale;
The bandied insults and the mutual jeer
Have grown monotonous upon the ear.
We now demand more value for our cash—
So hail! you legislators who can bash!
E’en Wood, escorted past the Speaker’s chair
To breathe the chilly, vacant midnight air,
Grows wearisome. A hurtled glass or two
No longer charms us as it used to do.
But Fate our lust of carnage satiates
With promise of two slugging heavyweights.
And, maybe, later on there will be scope
To find within our Parliaments a “hope”
To pit against Jack Johnson. He will bring
The scalps of all the negroes on a string C
Tied to his belt (a thing of dripping awe),
Because—poor fools!—they tried to hurt his jaw !
N.S.W.

This bank has an important asset that is only men-
tioned at the foot of the balance after the totals
have been added up. It is referred to as T. A.
Dibbs, General Manager-—the remarkable old gentle-
man who has been piling up experience during a
record reign of 45 years. It would be worth a great
ileal to a bank to have that sort of head, even
though his head-ship were only nominal. In this
case it has given the Commercial of Sydney, a mana
which it could hardly otherwise obtain, and business
has consequently pushed itself in through its doors.
And when business gets into the habit of doing that
one can be pretty sure that the bank has a lot of
the best to pick from. The following little table
shows the principal results of that push in 10
years:—
Liabilities.
.Tune, 1902. June, 1912.
Notes & bills £1,016,822 £9n,742 Dec. 4.4 p.c.
Deposits . . 11,964,545 20,823,525 Inc. 74.4 „
Assets.
Liquid assets 5,488,156 10.268,095 Inc. 87.1 „
Advances .. 9,140,293 14,378,424 Inc. 57.3 „
Premises . . 445,462 537,178 Inc. 20.6 ~
Thus, while deposits have increased 74 p.c., the bank
has only lent out an additional 57 p.c. ; consequently
it has made the remarkable increase of 87 p.c. on its
liquid assets. This very strong position with regard
to liquid assets has been one of the features of the
balance-sheet for a good many years past. As will be
seen presently, advances have grown very much dur-
ing the past 12 months ; nevertheless, at the end of
June, no less than 47 p.c. of all the moneys owing
to the public were held liquid. The size of this re-
serve is of course comforting to the creditor ; at the
same time, it is a matter of much concern to the bor-
rowing customer as well. Australia is a country of
very big harvests and very little ones, of booms and
slumps; a country where a man is liable to be a
million or so in credit this year and to need an
overdraft of the same amount 12 months afterwards.
It appears to have been the deliberate policy of the
management to hold itself in readiness to make the
big advances which a turn in the tide would in-
evitably call for; and though an extra £1,892,801
of advances have been made since June, 1911, the
Commercial is still in a position to back up its cus-
tomer when circumstances require it.
This policy of holding large liquid reserves, while
it reduces the risks of everybody concerned, has a
tendency to reduce profits under normal conditions ;
consequently there is nothing very striking in the
following table, which shows the earnings calculated
on capital and reserves combined-
Dec., 1907 .. £6 14 7 p.c. June, 1910 .. £8 2 11 p.c.
June, 1908 .. 7 5 7 „ Dec., 1910 .. 8 110
Dec., 1908 .. 618 7 ~ June, 1911.. 719 8 „
June, 1909 .. 7 9 0 ~ Dec., 1911 .. 719 10 „
Dec., 1909 ..8 4 7,, June, 1912 .. 8 211 „
The last line somewhat overstates the facts, because
in March last the shareholders confirmed a resolution
to increase the capital by £250,000, and the bulk
of this money has since then come in, and been no
doubt put to use. As at June 30 the capital
was £1,718,594 ; the full amount, including the last
£250,000 authorised, will be £1,750,000. As a re-
sult of the addition of this new capital the propor-
tion of the public’s money (deposits, etc.) to share-
holders’ funds has been brought down to about £6
11s. to every £l. Certainly, one cannot expect de-
posits to go on accumulating as they have done
lately, but even a couple of good seasons would see
this bank holding more than £7 of the public’s for
every £1 of its own, and that would be the signal
for a further increase of capital. Times would, how-
ever, have to be very good, and internal reserves very
solid to justify a further issue at par.
Taking a quick view at the past 12 months’ opera-
tions, these results show up:—

As before mentioned, the liquid assets still represent
a very safe 47 p.c. of the total liabilities; and in
this, as in every other respect, the visible position is
one that calls for three cheers.

This unfortunate company is in more trouble. It
can’t pay its dividend. The directors declare the
usual 15 p.c., but tell shareholders that the money
cannot be divided because the State Parliament has
not passed a Bill giving the co. further privileges;
also, the Treasury hasn’t yet got paid for the Kent-
street property, though why that should make any
difference, seeing that the co. still has the use of that
property, isn’t obvious. The whole thing is, of
course, a piece of transparent humbug. If tney
wanted to they could pay the dividend, and they
know it; but they want to make shareholders kick
their Parliamentary representatives, and, therefore,
they adopt a device worthy of a cheap showman,
rather than of the managers of a big and solid finan-
cial concern. In June, 1904, the company owed
£739,942, and there wasn’t a word about deferring
the dividend; now, with debts totalling only
£620,662, the dividend must be deferred. And
money is certainly much more plentiful now than it
was in 1904, while the co. is also in a far stronger
position. This sort of manoeuvring is not at all
likely to improve the status of the company in public
esteem.
The reserves shown above are made up of a formal
reserve fund ( £110,450) and unappropriated profits
( £8337). Besides these, there are premiums on
shares, £282,225; meter replacement fund, £18,258 ;
total, £300,483. Also, there is an unknown quan-
tity of internal reserves, consisting of undisclosed
profits invested in the business. The existence of a
large hidden fund of this character is quite plain.
Take the following comparison:—
June, 1902. June, 1912.
Book value of fixed pro-
perty and plant ..£1,217,074 £1,320,664
Gas sales, etc. (12 months) 377,854 650,047
Thus, 10 years ago, the plant was producing a re-
venue of 31.0 p.c. of its alleged value, whereas now
it is producing 46.7 p.c. The obvious explanation
of this remarkable difference is that during the 10
years very large additions have been made to the
plant, etc., out of profit, and, consequently, have not
been added to book value at all. If it took £1,217,074
of plant, etc., to produce a revenue of £377,854,
then, on the same basis, it would take £2,093,856
to produce £650,047 of revenue in the past year.
No such calculation can give a quite accurate view,
because works that produced £1 in 1902 might con-
ceivably be producing either 18s. or 225; 6d. now;
but the figures suggest something anyhow. During
the past 10 years Sydney has slopped over in all
directions, and the Gas Co. no doubt claims to have
kept up with the procession. Yet the capital (apart
from undisclosed profits) put into all the plant ad-
ditions to meet the increased consumption, has
amounted to only £173,590. If the truth were told,
probably half a million has gone that way, the bal-
ance, of course, being provided by consumers them-
selves. That is to say, in addition to providing the
15 p.c. dividend, consumers have added to the co.’s
property to the extent probably of £400,000 or
£500,000 during the 10 years. It is a gorgeous re-
sult ; it remains to be seen whether it will be
permitted to be reproduced indefinitely.

This was the gorgeous bank of the boom. Even
while that madness was at its height it built itself
a palace in Collins-street, and the magnificence wasn’t
completed when a ribald small boy chalked upon the
front door the great truth —“Bung.” When the per-
fect bankers who ran the concern had put wet
towels round their heads, they realised that things
were so shockingly bad that to give the bank a
chance the most rotten part of the business had
better be transferred to a separate Assets Trust. This
co. took over a large amount of securities and a
corresponding amount of debt, the latter guaranteed
by the bank. Certainly, as it stood, the bank wasn’t
in a position to guarantee anything much more
ambitious than a hot-pie stall; but its position was
vastly strengthened by the conversion of £2,117,350
of liabilities (deposits) into preference capital. The
ordinary capital was cut down bit by bit, until it
now stands at £95,619, in 10s, shares. There
wasn’t much of the old capital left, therefore. Yet
in view of the terrific deficit, it is doubtful whether
any of it should have been left at all. The preference
capital carries a maximum of 4 p.c., and 2 p.c. of
this is cumulative. So far no more than 3 p.c. has
ever been paid. For a good many years past enough
profit has been made to pay the full 4 p.c., but the
bank has been busy filling up the chasm represented
by the Trust’s deficit. As at June 30 this deficit was
stated to be £902,956. Regarding the Trust and
the bank as one concern, the position is, therefore,
as follows:
Capital.. .. .. .; £2,212,969
Reserves. . . . .. . . 6,227
Total funds . ..£2,219,196
Less estimated deficiency in connection
with Assets Trust . . . . -. . 902 956
Capital intact £1,316,240
The first table shows how the bank has fared; the
next shows how the two together stand:—

This means that the bank has still to make £896,729
of profit in addition to the dividend, in order to
restore the capital, provided, of course, that the
valuations are correct. During the past 12 months
the deficit has been reduced by £52,350, and at that
rate of progress it will take about 17 years to com-
plete the filling up of the hole. There is a suspicion,
though, that the recovery in values has helped the
Trust, and, consequently the bank, more than the
published accounts indicate. The last complete valua-
tion seems to have been made in 1902-3, when the
present general manager took charge. Even then the
new man was not likely to have over-valued the
assets, and, generally speaking, values are at a much
higher level now. No one need, therefore, be sur-
prised if the Board has a considerable contingency
reserve up its sleeve; and unless some bad slump
happens that reserve should cut something tangible
off the 17 years mentioned above. This, however, is
merely supposition, and it is about a fair thing to
ask that a new valuation should now be submitted.
The question also arises whether it is incumbent
on the bank to make good the whole of the deficit.
Exactly the same result could be accomplished by
writing down capital. The difficult problem is to
decide on the capital to be written down. As men-
tioned above, pref. capital is limited to a 4 p.c. divi-
dend, and it has never yet had more than 3, while
ordinary capital has had nothing at all since the
small boy did the signwriting mentioned above. It
should not be impossible to arrive at a fair com-
promise between the two sections, so that the one
might forthwith have the present limitation re-
moved while the other got a taste at once. No doubt
the ordinaries think they will be in clover by-and-
bye. For instance, supposing the net profit of the
bank is £125,000 a year, a 4 p.c. pref. dividend
would absorb £84,694, leaving £40,306 available
for ordinaries ; and £40,306 on the ordinary capital
would amount to 40 p.c. That looks very luscious ;
but, unfortunately for ordinaries, they are outnum-
bered by pref. shares, and each share has the same
voting power. Prefs. are not at all likely to approve
of anything so luscious; their policy would rather be
to limit the ordinary dividend, and thus, by build-
ing up big reserves, make their own 4 p.c. secure
for all time. Besides, it cannot do a bank any good
to have it constantly proclaimed that it has a big
deficit on the premises, or to have the market reports
screaming every day that its shares are at a heavy
discount. Admittedly, this bank has done pretty
well, but it might have done a great deal better had
it not been obliged to make a daily exhibition of the
skeleton in its cupboard. This, for instance, shows
the Commercial of Aus.’s progress, compared with that
of the Bank of N.S.W.:—
1890. 1912.

Apart from the change which has followed the intro-
duction of Commonwealth Notes, there is nothing of
much importance in that. The total liabilities of
the bank run to £7,212,159; the liquid assets are
£2,891,705. Consequently the latter represent 40
p.c. of the former —a solid enough proportion. In
fact, the bank might on its present resources let out
a few thousands more without straining itself. Alto-
gether it would be a highly presentable balance-
sheet but for the occurrence of the ancient deficit;
and, as before mentioned, there are ways of burying
that gibbering skeleton out of sight.

Six months ago a calculation was made here showing
that this company could pay its 10 p.c. dividend and
provide for renewals, even though its net revenue
was reduced by one-fifth. The management evidently
came to the conclusion that the time was ripe for a
reduction in the charges, but it didn’t knock off any
one-fifth; it merely offered consumers the small
mercy of a reduction, amounting to one-twelfth. Syd-
ney thinks itself badly enough treated when it has
to pay 3s. 9d. per thousand feet; Melbourne, in
spite of its company’s last burst of generosity, has
still to pay 4s, 7d. Of course, coals have to be car-
ried a few miles further to reach Melbourne, but
when they are in the ship’s bottom those few miles
are not a sufficient justification for an additional
charge of lOd. per 1000 ft. of gas. The Melbourne
company works under an Act which provides that it
may pay a 10 p.c. dividend when it sells gas for
7s. per thousand, and for every reduction of ljd. it
may pay an additional \ p.c. From this it will be
gathered that the co.’s Act is entirely out of date,
and offers no protection worth mentioning.
For years past the co. has known that it is skating
on thin ice, and the fear of something drastic has
done what the law failed to do, and steadied the
rate of dividend. But while this fear of drastic
action has steadied the dividend, it has not sufficed
to steady the profits. The company, instead of divid-
ing the money available, has used its surplus over
and above 10 p.c. to pay for new works. In 1904
the expenditure on works charged to capital account
was £2,149,842 ; the total now is £2,182,737.
Thus, only £32,895 of capital has been spent on
works since 1904. Meantime the profit in a half-year
has increased from £48,971 to £109,437. From
these figures alone it will be plain that either the old
works have become vastly more remunerative, or a
great quantity of new works have been paid for out of
profits. As a matter of fact, the company does not
go to much trouble to hide this expenditure, and the
first table, compiled of course from the co.’s
accounts, shows the profits that have been absorbed
in this way. In the past three years £132,584 has
thus been provided by the consumers; this is in
addition to large amounts spent on reconstructing,
and no doubt improving, works, as well as upon re-
newals of all sorts. The result of the past year’s
work is somewhat like this:—
Dividends (101) p.c.) .. ..£90,007
Put into new works .. .. 57,146
Reconstructing works, written off,
etc 55,190
Added to Formal Reserve .. .. 12,264
Total profit for the year £214,607
From this it will be seen that though the rate has
now been put up, the dividend consumed actually less
than 42 p.c. of the profit. The other 58 p.c. was
socked away in the shape of new works, etc. Admit-
tedly a small part of this was written off, but the
bulk of the depreciation is provided for independ-
ently, being charged direct to Revenue Account. The
company has by such methods put itself into an extra-
ordinarily strong position ; and it may be difficult to
shift it. The Act previously mentioned provides that
the municipalities may buy the whole outfit, on pay-
ing six times the amount of the last three years’
profits, and on that basis the purchase price might
easily reach £3,000,000. Yet, even at that figure,
it looks as though it would pay Melbourne hand-
somely to make the deal.

It is 17 years since tiie shareholders got so large a
plum out of tins pie. While other ana stronger
uanks were closing their doors and driving bargains
with their creditors, this one was able to pull through
after a fashion. Without a doubt it would have
been better oh if it had admitted its losses and
made all or most of them good by writing oh capi-
tal. Instead, it set itself the long and dreary job
of making good the deficiencies out of future profits.
Very likely if the public had not known that any-
thing was wrong, the job would have been a com-
paratively easy one; but not knowing how bad the
position really might be, possible customers at the
bank stood off and as a rule business only turned
into the City after it had been turned down by
other concerns. Luckily the prosperity of the past
few years came to its assistance, and the increase of
the dividend to 5 p.c. may be taken as a hint thai
in the management's opinion the old dead horse has
been pretty well removed from the premises, and
tuac the assets are now worth very neariy as much as
the accounts declare them to be.
As the situation has improved the bank naturally
finds it easier to do new business ; and it is helped
along that road by some fairly solid supporters. The
following figures show some considerable movements
during the year:—
Liabilities.
June, 1911. June, 1912.
Deposits £1,637,602 £1,890,900 Inc. £253,298
Notes . . 68,813 12,783 Dec. 56,030
Assets.
Coin, etc. 526,399 423,537 Dec. 102,862
Public se-
curities 92,000 92,000 ——
Advances 1,343,013 1,636,201 Inc. 293,188
Premises 173,839 185,941 Inc. 12,102
Eight years ago the City had only £1,082,103 of
deposits; now it has £1,890,900, and the amount is
being added to rapidly—a very good indication of
the change that has taken place ip the public’s
opinion of the bank. Even now the public’s money
in the business is only 41 times greater than the
shareholders’ funds (capital and reserves), and that
would be regarded as a very moderate proportion
under normal conditions. In fact, if the capital and
reserves were represented by assets worth 20s. in the
£, they would justify a further increase of, say, 50
p.c. in the deposits, etc. On its present resources,
however, it appears to have lent out quite as much
money as a bank can afford to if it wants to sleep
soundly at nights. In liquid assets it holds only
27 p.c. of the amount it owes to the public—cap'tal
is £400,000, in 50,000 shares, paid to £B. The
new shares (with a prior liability) were down to
71s. in Dec., 1904. Their present quotation is £5
—a very substantial rise, although plainly the
investor is still in doubt as to the value of the
assets. Since a bank’s very existence depends upon
its credit, and since it cannot be to the credit of a
bank to have its shares quoted at a heavy discount,
the management might do much worse even now
than to revalue the assets and let the shareholders
and the public know what the real position is.

This is the oldest trustee company in Australia.
When it was formed a big capital was thought to be
necessary. Subsequent companies learned a new
trick; they made their capital big, but called up
only a very small part of it, leaving the shareholders
liable for the balance. Consequently this co. has to
pay dividend on £90,000, while somewhat younger
companies make almost as much headway on a paid
capital of from £25,000 to £50,000. This ex-
plains why this co.’s dividend is only 7 p.c., while
that of others not so well situated is 10 p.c. or more.
Moreover, a company doing trustee business has
almost, of necessity, to put its money into gilt-
edged securities, and that, of course, means a low
interest return, so the dividend doesn’t get very
much help from that quarter. In the present case
two-thirds of the capital is represented by office free-
hold- —an investment chosen partly, no doubt, to save
rent; partly, also, for the value of such premises ,
for advt. purposes. The other one-third is repre-
sented by public securities and mortgages. The re-
serve fund is separately invested in mortgages .
( £18,288) and public securities ( £24,212). This;,
fund has been added to at an average rate of about
£4200 per annum for a good many years past, and;
the management doesn’t make any secret of its in-
tention to keep going till the reserves equal the
capital. Probably few trustee companies take so,,
serious a view of their liabilities; but that may be
due to the fact that, unlike this one, they have not-
yet made a serious acquaintance with the law’s uncer-
tainties. In any case, the existence of large capital ;
and reserve funds is all in favor of clients, for the .
bigger the funds the bigger the guarantee that the •
Trusts will be properly administered. At the endi
of the half-year the co. had estates valued at
£9,457,494, under administration. Ten years ago.
the total was £6,646,727. This shows a very healthy
growth of the good habit of entrusting work of this;
kind to people who are trained to do it. As to cost,,
this and similar cos. are paid (1) by an annual 21.
p.c. or so on income, and (2) by a single charge of
p.c. or so on the capital value. The T. E. and A.
nowadays bunches its receipts from all sources, but
it looks as though the co. charges for its services on
an average about 4s. 6d p.c. p.a. of the capital
value, and a heap of people charge much more for
doing much less.

business. Both General-manager Delprat and the

issue of a further 540,000. It is not clear whether
these will be at once sent to market; probably the
necessary money will be raised on debentures secured
by this uncalled capital. Nominally the new shares
will be worth Bs. like the others, but the market
would naturally appraise them considerably higher,
so that no doubt a million could be comfortably

Every engineer in charge of factories and mills
should give close attention to the power wasted in
transmission of power. The Hyatt Flexible Roller
Bearings save 20 per cent, power and 75 per cent,
of oil, so one of the first duties is naturally for
engineers to direct their special attention to these
bearings and to car boxes for trucks. Hyatts are
in continuous use in Cement Works, Ore Dressing
Mills, Factories, etc., in Australia. Bushings of
flexible rollers may be applied to loose pulleys or
truck wheels, etc. Should engineers require further

W. and J. Lempriere, Melbourne; or E. W. Hurst-

The Bigelow Patent lioekbreaker is cheaper to
maintain and repair than gyratory and other crush-
ers. It is of the well-known Blake type, but has im-
provements which has justified all the claims of the
patentee. Crushing is done on the down stroke of
the pitman, so there is no strain on the pitman cap.
Side rods resist the thrust of the toggles. The back
toggle will resist legitimate strains of crushing, but
will shear under exceptional conditions, thus saving
the machine. The Austral Otis Eng. Co., Ltd., of
South Melbourne, have the manufacturing right for
this machine.* [1

If Stanley Low, the former manager of
Block 10, had had more friends at Broken Hill, it
would long 1 ago have leaked out there that he had
made a new estimate of ore reserves prior to his
hurried departure. But Low was a man who stood
aloof from the rest of the mining crowd, and neither
gave help nor expected any ; consequently, though it
was well known that new measurements had been
made, nobody felt interested in mentioning the fact
after he had gone. This was unfortunate for Low,
because the public was thus left to suppose that, in
his opinion, there were over half a million tons of
payable ore in view, whereas the new manager could
only find 282,000 tons. The drill had shown that
there was ore at 415 ft., but a crosscut had disclosed
that it was under milling grade, and no doubt this
unfavorable development led Low to cut down his old
figures. What his new estimate was has not trans-
pired, though Broken Hill mining men assume that
it was under 300,000 tons. Presumably the office
has the figures, though it naturally wasn’t concerned
about giving the old manager’s estimate when a new
manager had been put in charge.
The New Burragorang silver lead mine
(N.S.W.) has its shaft 222 ft. deep, and now that an
air-compressor has been provided development will be
pushed on more rapidly. The 6in. veins carry fair
values occasionally, and it is a feature of this silver
mining field that small veins live down to a great
depth. Prospecting work will become more interest-
ing as levels are driven for, then it is to be hoped
the small vein the co. is now sinking on will open
out into a larger ore-body.
B. H. Junction North shares continue to
weaken. The slump started at the end of last week,
when it became known that, as a precautionary meas-
ure, the men had been taken out of the mine. An exami-
nation of the workings failed to disclose anything
alarming, but there has probably been a settlement
of ground. Since the four lowest levels have been
opened, the totals mined from each, to March 31,
1912, have been: —
< Tons.
No. 9 level.. .. .. .. 108,749
No. 10 level.. .. .. .. 204,799
No. 11 level 268,989
No. 12 level 25,292
607,829
Metal contents recovered in concentrates cannot
be given with exactitude, but approximately they
are from 50,000 to 60,000 tons of lead and 3,000,000
oz. of silver. Gross value of the lot, with lead at
£l2 per ton and silver 2s. per oz., is £900,000.
The remnant left is not of so high a value, for reserves
are about 300,000 tons. How much ore there is
above No. 12 level, and along its north-western ex-
tension towards the North B. H. Co.’s boundary,
must depend on future developments. A level has
followed the footwall, but crosscutting has yet to be
done.
The Mount Bulga Mines and Smelting
Co. (N.L.) is to be formed, with a capital of
£30,000 in ss. shares. Of these 47,000 are offered
to the public, while 33,000, with £lO,OOO payable
in instalments, will satisfy the vendor, who parts
with the Bulga copper mine as a going concern.
This show is eight miles outside of Orange (N.S.W.),
and contains a copper lode at the south end of the
lease—-80 acres —and a gossan outcrop to the north.
The latter has been worked by an open cut to a
depth of 50ft., and is reckoned to be the leached
upper portion of another copper lode. The gossan
is estimated to average 20s. per ton for its gold and
silver. The workings on the copper lode proper con-
sist of a vertical shaft 300 ft. deep, with a couple
of levels, exposing a shoot of ore about 100 ft. long
and perhaps 6ft. to Bft. wide. But the ore has
been mostly stoped, leaving a possible 14,000 tons
to go on with. About 1000 tons are at grass. A
report by T. H. Fawcett, of Mount Oxide fame, testi-
fies to the ability of the mine to earn a profit with
copper over £6O per ton, but how much over is left
unsaid. It also recommends the acquisition of the
property at a moderate price, and £2OOO is consid-
ered to be sufficient working capital for present
requirements. It seems evident that the largest
part of the purchase price is in the prospects, and
the future of copper doesn’t gild the edge of the
proposition.
Another snasm hit the bosom of the
Melbourne Stock Exchange last week, because Bill
Toole, manager of the New Langi Logan,
wired , that the drill was through clay at 77ft. Of
course Toole needn’t have sent anything till he had
stopped boring from the crosscut on the gutter.
But he sent progress wires, and because such scoot-
ing was done to such an extent by the cold feet
crowd as to knock a lot of gilt off the ornamental
value of New Langi shares, the cry of hot stuff went
up. This was based on the fact that on the evening
of the first wire Ballarat sopped up all the cheap
scrip, and because, on the morn of the next day,
Toole reported that the drill, above the clay, had
gone into good wash carrying gold. To prove hot
stuff it has to be demonstrated that Ballarat knew on
the Wednesday night that the bore was into good
wash, also that the manager or the directors were aware
of this, and passed on word to clever people in Balla-
rat and Melbourne. A Stock Exchange inquiry is
ordered. This is a bit of Joseph Surface duplicity;
for if there was any hanky-panky—which hasn’t been
established —it isn’t half as bad as some of the deals
that have gone on in Broken Hill stocks within the
past year. Yet no busybody has been sent round to
stir up mud over what looks to be equally hot stuff.
The only body that could get at the truth would be
the talked-of Royal Commission on mining. This,
the Age says, isn’t needed. Why, dear old penny
Gehazi —why ?
* * * *
Cock’s Pioneer, Eldorado (Vie.), is out
of debt. Smith, of Wangaratta, and the jaynial
Jarvie, as the backbone of the little alluvial bonser,
can be slapped on the back because of the feat.
There’s a widow and orphan or two, and a few
priests who offer prayer over this miracle. A lot of
Scotch bone has been needed to get out of the slough
and on to the new face. That the co., having reached
the 53rd call, is still going strong, isn’t won-
derful to those who know the claim. The barge is
now fairly well down to the 70ft. floor, and, as it hit
fairly payable wash between 50 and 70 feet, the co.
has carried along of late without financial troublings.
The 70ft. cleans should bring divs. Then they will
cut down to the 90ft. floor, where the best gold lies.
Still, what is wanted is more cash to provide more
barges. It is a pity that this isn’t forthcoming for
so genuine a show.

Rugby footer isn’t in it with the game
being played at Ararat (Vic.) just now. Central
Cathcart shot up to 60s. and went back as quickly
to 505., in the face of rich samplings and a slap-
up outlook. The old jangle between the two camps
on the field may be responsible for the bumps that
are given, and for the most being said ot the way
in which the Central pumping plant is taxed, in a
friendly way, of course, the whispering on the other
bank isn’t kindly to Cathcart. So the sight is wit-
nessed of a sharp slide from the 30s. to the ZUs.
mark. New Langi also piled up a heap ot misfor-
tune. Another bore didn’t poke into wash below
79ft., though it should have done so at 7 (Jit. ipe
drill proved that the gutter runs flat, and at tne
same time dipped the market a clear half sov. per
share. But the Langi light isn’t quite doused, for
the Cathcart lead has to be got yet on the west
boundary. The Cathcart high bank, too, has to be
reached in that claim, so the punters don t despair
about regaining their wind.
TV * * *
Word has gone round in London that the
old Briseis mine is to have a market. This surely
explains the drift of the co.’s shares across the
water for the past six or eight months. The bait
thrown out is that, because the visible supplies ot
tin in the world’s markets have declined during the
past year or two, every reason exists why for a
corresponding term ahead, the price of the metal
should not fall below £175 per ton. All this is
very pretty, but “a prolonged period of prosperity-
built on such an hypothesis is resting on very treach-
erous foundations. Tin is getting scarcer, but the
manipulation of the tin market is worse than the
trickery of a three-card gang. So there isn’t an
element of security about the structure any more
than there is any element of certainty that the over-
trading of to-day mayn’t end up with some big
financial upset. Then heigh-ho for high prices ot
metals for a while. Again, there is the question
whether the mine has such an assured_ future that
it can return the present price, of scrip. Lindesay
Clark says there is 1000 tons of tin ore in the Krushka
flat. Neither he nor Currie will testify as to
what is under the Kingarooma basalt capping. So
the plain calculation based on all that is known of
the actual reserves of drift in the Briseis leases is,
if 6115 tons of metallic tin won have yielded 11s. 3d.
per share in divs. since 1900, under relatively easy
conditions, what will 1000 tons in the Krushka flats
give? Not Bs., the present price of shares, to a
certainty. What remains to make up the difference
is the drift under the basalt which Clark is working
off the Kingarooma leases. Of its value no one can
speak, though the word is passed round that the work
done has resulted “in a reserve of several million
cubic yards of tin drift, from which substantial
profits will be obtained in the future.” Puffing of
this form looks very nice when put in type, but the re-
cords of the past ought not to have been set beside
it to warn the dubious.
. * * *•
Lebrina Gully, near Scottsdale (Tas.),
has been successfully floated in 30,000 shares of ss.
each. Favorable reports are to hand, including one
from a former underground manager of the Beacons-
field (Tas.). The lode, averaging 12in. to 18in.
wide, gives prospects up to an ounce per ton, where
proved by a number of prospecting shafts over a
distance of 500 ft. A second-hand 10-head battery
and winding plant are to be erected on the spot.
* * * *
A new man from London is getting ready
to confirm the low temperature roasting and subse-
quent cvanidation tests successfully made with
Gwalia Consolidated ore last year. He exnects to
have a 30-to-50-ton-a-day experimental plant in
operation at the end of August, and if good results
can be obtained from this tonnage the co. proposes
to try to raise more money for a larger plant. If only
the treatment problem could be solved, the property
would stand a good chance of becoming a big thing,
but this bait has been dangled so often before share-
holders, with ruinous results, that they must be now
loth to nibble at it.
Directly it bought the Gwalia South, Sons
of Gwalia (W.A.), with a knowing look in its eye,
continued a No. 7 level crosscut into the former
property, and at Bft. inside the boundary, cut 82in.
of 31s. ore. The existence of this shoot was un-
known to the now defunct Gwab'a South. Co., but it
now transpires that the Gwalia authorities “unex-
pectedly” struck it with a drill shortly after nego-
tiations for purchase were started, and it influenced
them in making a deal.

In addition to an unknown tonnage re-
maining in old stopes, Great Fingall (W.A.) was
estimated at December 31 last to have 52,926 tons
of reserves above the No. 14 level of an average
value of 28s. Bd. Below this level there are reckoned
to be 31,812 tons of ore worth 425. 10d., but these
reserves will, it is mentioned, not be available for
the mill until after the completion of the new
internal shaft from the No. 13 to No. 18 levels.
Developments at Nos. 17 and 18 levels are said to
be still satisfactory, and, taking as a basis the
extent and value of the ore opened up at these two
points, there appears to the management to be good
reason to believe that the top of another huge lens
of ore, similar to, and possibly as good as the one
which made the mine famous in days gone by, has
been met with. After charging to revenue the whole
of the internal shaft expenditure, the surplus for the
year amounted to £19.116. Of this £12,500 was
mooped up by an interim dividend, and, after pro-
viding for other exes, £4263 remained. The whole
of this, together with £16,248 transferred from
reserve account, has been applied to writing down
to a nominal sum the book value of the co.’s West
African delusion.
More capital expenditure was outlined at
the meeting of the Mount Morgan. The copper smelt-
ing plant is to be improved, along with sundry sur-
face changes. There are only five furnaces, not
nearly enough for this tonnage under pyritic smelt-
ing conditions. Because of the general lay of the
works additions are likely to be costly. Casey, the
new chairman, makes the remark that the directors
consider the cost of producing copper in the past has
been too high. Everyone has known for a long time
that it has been ridiculously high. He also makes
the surprising statement that the silicious gold ore
treated at the mundic works has yielded very little
profit of late. This looks as though it were merely
intended to shield the copper costs. However, con-
sidering for the moment that the copper works
yielded all the profit, the following results: Output
of copper for the year was 7440 tons; average gross
price realised per ton was £63 13s. 2d., not count-
ing the gold contents, which averaged 11.120 z. per
ton of blister. The net profit, after allowing for de-
preciation, was £208,585 (increased largely by
bringing forward the copper on hand at £5O, and
carrying it forward at £7O).
7440 tons, at £63 12s. 6d £473,370
Fine gold in copper .. . . ~ . 354,000
Total value of copper production 827,370
Net profit .. . . . . . . 208,585
Total cost .. .. ..£618,785
This is equal to a total cost of £B3 3s. 4d. per ton
of copper. But it was worse than this, for the mun-
dic works must have yielded part of the profit of
£208,585. Of the silicious gold ore, 93,413 tons
were treated, averaging 10.96dwt. gold and 0.91 p.c.
copper. The gold average is the highest for many
years. The copper from precipitates is also credited
to this ore. With copper at £5O, the yield from
this ore was equal to 555. 7d. per ton.. To say there
was no profit on a grade such as this is absurd. The
last costs published ini connection with the mundic
works gave crushing, roasting and chlorinating, 14s.
3d. per ton. If no profit resulted, it means that the
increase in working costs, the cost of mining and the
overhead charges must have totalled another 41s. 4d.
Mount Morgan mining is bad enough, but not so
bad as that, especially as the open-cut yielded a fair
proportion of the ore. If the siliceous gold ore
yielded 10s. per ton profit, the copper cost was over
£9O per ton; if 20s. be the mundic profit, the copper
cost aviates up to the aerial regions of £lOO per
ton. According to a statement given to the meeting,
the gold contents of the ore in reserve have increased
over 60 p.c. After 82,6550 z. were extracted, the
mine now contains 482,0000 z. of gold more than it
did a year ago. The copper also bumps up. Practi-
cally no development took place to account for it. At
last Mount Morgan begins to stir in the matter of
further exploration. To find out what its probable
life is it has shown little curiosity on this score.
There is talk of reducing the Many Peaks’ ore
treated; but it is probably merely talk. Wesley, of
Mount Lyell, is the new metallurgist, and he has
been used all his life to dealing with a pyritic flux,
so it is unlikely that he will start experimenting
with ironstone when he can get the other. Provided
the mining manager keeps the pace going without
getting into trouble, the improved smelting will
make a big difference in the costs. There is ample
room for double the usual dividend, but a lot of ex-
penditure will first be necessary.
Columbia Extended (Gympie, Q.) is giv-
ing most of its attention to the 1465 ft. level, where
a i*eef of 4in. to 6in. recently gave some fine specimen
stone. Some of this will catch the public’s vacant
stare at the Brisbane exhibition. From a crushing
of 71 tons, during June, gold to the value of £B6l
was returned. The No. 4 North Phoenix has got on
small stuff, the reef managing no greater corpulency
than an average of 2in. to 4in. Still, the concern
dragged together 139 tons. The 3670 z. acquired
from this parcel gave the shareholders a present of
£lOOO. Other Gympie dividend givers during June
were the North Great Eastern Tribute, which scat-
tered a mere £125 in threepenny bits, and the Scot-
tish Gympie. The latter crushed 7500 tons for
21660 z., and its usual quarterly distribution of lid.
a share ( = £4125) was the result. For the half-
year Gympie put 67,432 tons under the stampers for
23,4200 z. of gold. Galls are still top-dog for the
half-year ( £18,597, as against dividends, £14,008).
Croydon (N.Q.) at present is talking
mostly about the old Empire claim. Twelve months
ago, the co. started out to test the Nos. 2 and 3
levels with great expectations. But £62 worth of
gold from a mine expenditure of £865 wouldn’t
“boil the billy,” and the ghost was stalking up the
shaft without much ceremony when one Gill, an old
Croydon mining man, had something to say.
Amongst it was an opinion that the co. had not
really got into the gold-bearing country. It accord-
ingly handed him the job of testing his own theories.
A rise of lift, from a crosscut on No. 1 level opened
,jut a “3ft. reef, averaging 25dwt.,” while another
make of stone fan Bin. reef), discovered at the back
of the shaft, prospects for 3oz. Gill, accordingly, is
king. The Content Block had a decent crushing as
far as Croydon goes; 1133 tons from the Big stope
returned 47890 z., valued at £7443. The Balcony
stope supplied 553 tons for treatment, of slightly
better-quality stuff. It returned 9430 z. (= £1821).
But the low value of the gold (30s. to 40s. the ounce)
is the blot on the landscape.
St. Mungo copper (Cloncurry, Q.) started
with a modest capital of £5OOO in 10,000 shares of
10s. each. It is now proposed to enliven the gamble
by increasing the number of shares to 40,000 of
2s. 6d. each. Dividends, amounting to £3OOO (6s.
per share) have been paid by sending away 25 p.c.
copper ore (chalcopyrite) to southern smelters. These
shipments have been made with copper, most of the
time under £6O per ton. During the past half-year
two points of development—the shaft and a winze
from No. 2 level —have disclosed lateral deposits of
high-grade ore. The output for six months was 180
tons of 25 p.c. ore, and the net return was £2211.
On June 1 the credit balance was £484. There is
£BOO on fixed deposit. Reserves above No. 2 level
are 400 tons, containing, approximately, 100 tons of
copper.
Vulcan (N.Q.) has something more cheer-
ing at the 750 ft. level. Here the new make of ore
has been opened out to 30ft. long by 31ft. wide,
bulking 3 p.c. A little sinking and rising haven’t
poured any cold water on the importance of the
development. An attempt is being made to locate
the Same ore body at the 900 ft. level. For the
month, 1018 tons were crushed, producing 31 tons
2cwt. of concentrates, worth £2817.
Thompson’s Oaks (N.Q.) is saving up
another trial crushing from a vein of stone at the
168 ft. level. That was the depth where the reef was
met with in the straight shaft, and deeper sinking
having failed rather miserably, the co. is going to
prospect a continuation of the stone worked by the
prospectors. The term, “width of the ore shoot,”
may confuse some people into thinking that there is
a lode Bft. wide. That isn’t the case. The reef is
a few inches thick. There are too many shares for
this little prospecting venture to stagger under.
Oaks Rush Amalgamated (N.Q.) is closed
down, pending some kind of adjustment of the
finances. The brilliant show made from erushings
taken out above the 100 ft. level was deceptive, for
the mine turned out badly at depth, in spite of heaps
of encouraging and nicely-worded reports. The 10-
head mill, fed with surface and other low-grade
crushing stuff, failed to keep this huge quarter-
million concern on an even keel.
The Elliott, always on the look-out to ex-
tend its life a bit further, has acquired yet another
option, this time for 12 months, over the Native
Companion. Area is 160 acres and price £3BOO.
Which seems reasonable enough on glancing through
the list of parcels from the mine, ranging from 20
to 49 p.c. copper, with an average of 15dwt. gold
and 9oz. to 15oz. silver per ton. The question, of
course, is—How many tons of crude ore were broken
to make up these parcels? A year ought to give a
fair idea as to whether this is another of the Clon-
curry surface squibs. Manager Corbould will, long
before that time, be enjoying sweet rest from option-
hunting expeditions.
New Queen Cross (Charters Towers)
struck the Moonstone formation at 78ft., and after
sinking a well hole will drive on the quartz leaders
(lightly mineralised) cut in the shaft. Locally this
stock is gambled in because there is a reef 24ft:
thick showing at the bottom of the Papuan co.’s
shaft, and it is thought that it may possibly make
good at depth. The reef has always been variable and
patchy, so it provides a low-priced toss-up.
* * * »
Brilliant Deeps (Charters Towers) cleaned
up a crushing of 157$ tons for gold concentrates and
sands worth £1377, the average being £8 15s. Id.
per ton. Since the reef was struck in No. 4 level
(2540 ft.), 1165 tons have averaged £7 7s. 3d. per
ton. Chairman Millican told shareholders that 200 ft.
of driving along No. 4 west level had produced 413
tons of stone, so it is practically certain that the
poorer section of the reef extended for 85ft., in ad-
dition to which there are two blanks. Charters
Towers doesn’t appear to be a buyer of Deeps at pre-
sent in any large quantity. A London register is to
be opened. With the new machinery in going order
a very much increased output of stone is to be ex-
pected.
