A
WOMAN’S
LETTER
Sydney, July 29, 1912.
My Dear Arini, —
“Mother of Ten” drops in a fierce note of
interrogation, re the very wet rainfall you
may have noticed splashing over N.S.W.
“Where does the rain go to? I want to
know if it falls down into cracks in the
earth, and gets lost before it has time to
make the grass grow, or the young calves
jump for joy. It must go somewhere. But
butcher’s meat is steadily climbing to famine
prices. Milk (sometimes the rain falls into
it) remains firm at sd. a quart. Every
article of food, and most of the garments my
flock wear out before the rain stops raining,
are 50 per cent, dearer (like rents) than
they were seven or eight years ago. The
only thing that stands still—like Balaam’s
ass —is my good man’s wage. I want to
know whether we are in the grip of a beef
and mutton trust, and would be glad to hear
why a ‘beneficent rainfall’ is a harder thing
to live up to than a good old-fashioned
drought?” This suburban explosive (to be
handled with care) will he passed later on
to this office’s financial Dreadnought. He
is at present hiding from the weather in a
sun-burnt isle that rocks itself to sleep in
the arms of the Equator.
But I know a certain capitalist—or per-
haps I should say a certain capitalist con-
descends to know me; and I showed him
this clamor, and asked for advice. He was
the angriest man in the street. “People like
that,” he shouted, “don’t know what they
are talking about. Meat may have gone up
id. a pound or so. I don’t know. But look
how shares have gone up ! Do you know, I
paid over £5 each the other day for some in-
surance shares that I could have bought for
£1 in 1594. Why, if meat went up to 2s. a
pound it would be nothing compared to what
I have to put up with in my business !” So
I saw that the case was a complicated one,
and fled.
An inter-State Congress of the National
Council of Women is purring over its tea-
cups, as I write (Monday), at the invita-
tion of Sydney’s small Mayoress, Miss
Clarke.
The affairs of every State in the Com-
monwealth will be carefully half-soled and
heeled by the National Council of Women
this week. They will occasionally remove
their august lorgnettes from the backslidings
of Man (regarded as a law-maker) to pur-
sue the ham sandwich under the vice-regal
shingle. Lady Denman’s evening party will
have received female cavalry in council
before this beats against your far-off shore,
Arini. The Chelmsford lady has com-
manded the presence of all inter-State chat-
ter-boxes on Thursday afternoon (August
1). The intervals will be filled up with X
Bay remarks of dazzling effulgence, which
will daily flood the King’s Hall with recipes
on the Right Way of Thinking. Any dele-
gate who takes more than five minutes to
tear the arguments of any other delegate to
tatters will be warned off the platform as a
person suspected of sleeping in red bed-socks
and weai’ing republican chest-warmers.
The Chapzander mission to the Lost has
found a fairly good collection of threepenny-
bits in its prayerful plates. But I fancy the
novelty and the cash receipts have worn
rather thin since the first great onslaught
on trembling half-fledged sinners. Most of
the sinners, by the way, who are saved while
you wait by these penny-in-the-slot Yankee
methods seem to be quite tame. They have
buff or pale brown consciences, instead of
the black-and-sulphur article, and don’t look
fierce enough to rob a hen’s-nest. The Gen-
uine Article (with horns above and cloven
walk-upons beneath) either finds business
too flourishing to retire to a green hill far
away, or creeps stealthily by night into the
soul-saver’s snug den, and secretly offers his
speckled career, and one per cent, of his
bank overdraft to the Hymn-zander treasury.
The Y.M.C.A. has recently broken out in
the same old place—debating rounds in
which it takes on tongue-pugilists from mild
suburban temples devoted to Y.M.C.A. and
the art of getting on in life. Summer Hill is
alleged to be repairing its fallen debaters
who nearly perished under the wild Chris-
tian Young Men’s talk-who-talk-can the other
day. This flowing river of debate will flood
Melbourne in the immediate future.
The “Alliance Frangaise” will hold a
“Soiree Artistique et Litteraire” on Fridav
evening, August 9. St. James’s Hall will
be the scene of this determined attack on
French verbs.
In spite of many false alarms, whiskers
that trail across the manly waistcoat don’t
seem to be coming in. The Central Metho-
dist Mission in this city sent along its 28th
anniversary booklet, and (tempting Fate)
politely asked for a few remarks thereon.
The frontispiece picture shows the head par-
son _ of its local circuit and superintendent
of its charity bed and board department,
etc.. Rev. W. G. Taylor, and his wife. He
evidently fell into the hands of a London
barber during his recent walks abroad. A
reformed set of society whiskers, a short
thick grey hedge round his Methodist chin,
is all that remains of the untidy half-yard
of beard and door-mat that used to wave in
unison with the Taylor coat-tails on a windy
day. With his beard at one end and his
coat-tail at the other he vaguely suggested
a pious elephant with a Bible in its ear.
Getting off the track of religious whiskers,
I note that the recent tramping of Parson
Taylor up and down the grey old city by
the Thames was not innocent of autographs.
A letter from the Dowager Queen Alexandra
is not exactly _ signed, but is the next best
thing of its kind —if its kind will put an
extra pot of soup on for the Burwood “Alex-
andra Home” for friendless girls. It con-
veys her ex-Majesty’s best wishes to Mr.
Taylor and his co-laborers. G. H. Reid
also committed himself in ink, to best wishes,
etc. The Alexandra Home is said to be con-
ducted on the Open Door system—no locks,
and no mention of latchkeys.
The Sydney Methodist Mission claims to
have saved a centenarian Frenchman (in his
year), a champion lightweight boxer
of N.S.W., a chimney sweep with a sooty
record, a Prodigal Son, various pickpockets,
burglars and other undesirable citizens. So
the Chapzander duet will have to look to
its prize salvage.
A Sydney paper publishes a statement
that m a few weeks 10,000 persons have
petitioned that when they die their bodies
S&y be burnt. The Cremation Society of
. .W. is, therefore, presumably, in high
spirits. How soon this band of 10,000 enthu-
siasts will die to help the cause has not been
advertised. Dr. Creed, M.L.C., has always
been a zealous advocate of the white-ash
remnant. The ordinary woman shies at the
thought of human dust _on the mantelpiece.
Dust has been her specialty—dust and wash-
ing up—since the erection of the first humpy
gave her more troubles than she was born
to. Funeral baked meats are bad old habits,
but the family urn is another story.
Invitations have already posted round the
Very Best suburbs for the wedding of Miss
Gladys Bowker and Mr. Jim Gordon.
August 17 is the date of the orange blos-
soming.
Miss Dita Kerr, a Darling Point damsel,
is going to shake her bridal plumage
at an evening ceremony in the parental
nest The groom is the elder son of the late
Justice Cohen, but I fancy the wedding bells
will be of the Christian persuasion. A re-
ception at the Australia later in the even-
ing (August 7) ought to gather together an
expensive assemblage of Darling Point’s
best ostrich feathers. The picture hat (as
a top-dressing to evening frocks), gets its
rare chance of walking out, at gaslight wed-
dings. Whether its wide roof shelters the
bare neck below it from the winter blast is
another matter. A sou’wester would be a
more certain cure for cold ears these nights.
That indefatigable pillar of Flemington
(V lc -) Racecourse, the strenuous Byron
Moore, sent a few post-mortem remarks re
the late airman Yanniman, to a friend on
Mosman’s leafy brow. The obituary notice
he saw in this letter, a few weeks ago, has
extracted the following Byronic In Memor-
iam :
I met Vanniman (who was killed recently by the
explosion of his aeroplane in the States) when he
was out here. He was a great enthusiast at anything
he took in hand, and secured a very fine panoramic
view of the Cup, but the best thing he did with a
camera was a bird’s-eye view of your harbor. I be-
lieve he made four balloon ascents before he got the
atmospheric effect that satisfied him.
Byron Moore’s numerous friends in this
watery town will heave a sympathetic sigh
to hear that Victoria’s artistic forerunner of
the horse has been wrestling with three fierce
little devils —lumbago, bronchitis and
asthma.
At a rainy time, when anything that can
be alluded to as “festive” is an aquatic per-
formance, or a duck, or a drunk or a rescue
from drowning, there is some solace in
plunging into an aggravated attack of dry
star-itis. Do they wear goloshes in Mars’?
Is Venus a dressy kind of planet, draped in
the latest cloud paniers? Does Tannhauser
pursue her golden curls—anyhow Catherine
de Medici knows all about these things.
Catherine was a dabbler in astronomy long
before the Woman’s Vote gave us upoisli
ideas.
Glancing through a lengthy list of famous
dames who made a noise with their mathe-
matics, centuries before Mary Proctor was
thought of, I recalled a painful fact. Hypa-
tia, who lectured on astronomy to Greek
students, in or about a.d. 400, had no press
agent. _ Even hairpins were not invented
when this learned lady was scraped to death
with sea-shells, in church, under the super-
vismn of a lay reader or local preacher
(probably because she knew too much to be
considered respectable) at Alexandria,
lagans, Jews and Christians nearly
strangled science about this time, and the
woman astronomer was snuffed out till stay-
laces and brains began to accumulate
together in the person of the saintly blue-
stocking Hildegarde, in the 12th century.
In spite of a quarter-mile or so of women’s
names which shine in the annals of famous
star-gazers, Mary Proctor was hailed by the
local press as “the only woman astronomer.”
.Shades of Elizabeth von Matt, W. Bottcher
Mrs. Lepaute, Mrs. du Piery, that famous
prodigy Maria Agnesi, and a hundred she sky-
walkers of later date! Where does the “only
woman” fable come from?
Mrs. Lawson, the frisky London society
dame who married Lord Hamilton, of Dai-
zell, 24 hours after the decree nisi had been
made absolute, narrowly escaped being Mrs
Levy for a start. Her first husband, Colonei
W. Lawson, is a son of the first Baron Burn-
ham, who was born plain Levy, being a son
of the late J. S. Levy and Esther his wife.
A Royal license helped him to “Lawson,”
which he took under the will of the late
Lionel Lawson, liis uncle. Burnham got his
gilded lordly prefix in 1903. He is chief
proprietor of the London Daily Telegraph,
the press in Bull’s island having a tendency
to run to peers.
Mayor Clarke and the slim Miss Mayoress
Clarke gave a municipal snack of lunch to
Miss Eva Mylott, before confronting her
with the outstretched hands of her numer-
ous friends. This revel occurred one day
last week. Town Clerk Nesbitt and Deputy
Aide Layton marshalled the 3.30 p.m. com-
pany into the Presence. It was a damp
company, with part of the rainfall trickling
oft its best hat, and the state of the streets
published on its patent leather toe-caps.
Eva Mylott is off to the South Coast for
a concert tour on the shores of the spouting
whale and the hills of the smoked hog. The
recently returned singer maintains that a
Flying Johnson, or whatever we call the
night train to Forbes, lacks the home com-
forts of an American Pullman car. “We had
to get out of our train at Bathurst at
3 a.m.,” says she, “because some part of it
had caught tire. It was a freezing wait, and
about 4 o’clock we were put into some bleak,
second-class carriages, and sat bolt upright,
packed together, till we got to Forbes about
11 a.m. After engaging a sleeper, it was
poor comfort to hear that we were lucky to
get to our destination when we did, for I
caught cold in my vocal chords, and had
laryngitis as a souvenir of the Forbes trip.
At the Dubbo hall most of the weather came
in through the windows —a piercing cold
sample it was —and when they tried to stuff
them up, the panes fell out; so I haven’t had
quite such a good time as I expected. How-
ever, the country looks well, and that’s the
main thing.” Then, I believe, the singer
broke down and wept bitterly.
Mrs. Jeanne Brola, of the Quinlan opera
co., had a ’busman’s holiday at the Bellevue
Hill Russell’s party early in the annals of
last week. Generally speaking, a profes-
sional singer never opens her mouth off the
stage during her season in important con-
cert or operatic thrills, except to ask someone
to pass the mustard, or to fling a compliment
at the local scenery. Mrs. Brola, however,
“obliged” the Russell’s company with several
vocal efforts. This rejuvenates a bad prece-
dent which professionals in this town have
been trying to bury in its shameless old age
for years.
The dramatic American songster may have
ottered to pipe her lay at the aforesaid
suburban assemblage of pearl pendants, etc.
Hut it’s up against the rule which Sydney
musicians are hanging on to, with what pot-
luck they can get. Pianist Charbonnet-
Kellermann fled from the meanness that
commandeered her time, “in a friendly way,”
and fed her with an indifferent oyster or
two, or gave her toothache with an acid
iruit salad, as a fee for her “de-lightful per-
forma nee.” If Mrs. Brola will sing for
nothing, she can eat a free dinner first
every evening the Quinlan management lets
her ott the chain.
■t T ™ e , Lottie Dearn concert occurs on August
•i, t , Concordia Hall (Elizabeth-street)
will be the place or battlefield. This young-
ster has been so carefully taught by Mr.
Asprey that, granted the cash to fill little
Bottle s purse, and send her off rejoicing to
Leipsic, she has nothing to unlearn when she
goes abroad. As I remarked, after hearing
her—on one of the numerous Nights of Icy
Blasts—-she has the forearm of a juvenile
blacksmith to aid and abet the technique of
a . brilliant young student of the hard-worked
piano.

Robert Parker is the most rollicking

He wore two emaciated feathers, sprouting

Oscar Asche and his Lily are thinking of
a course of physical culture—so an unreli-
ahie rumor says. Asche hopes to relieve him-
self of a few rolls of fat, preparatory to
some future part in which he wants to
achieve a more soulful expression about his
brawny chest. The Lily is presumably onlv
thinking of her partner’s efforts for a more
ethereal figure,_ for her own tall and slender
frame is the ideal clothes-prop proposition
that every first-class costumifire yearns over.
. Composer Puccini, in his latest excursion
into liellowdrama, “The Girl of the Golden
Yv est, says: Its general characteristics
are that every note of it is Puccini pure and
simple. To an extent it is less sentimental
than my other works. It is, let me see
it is more picturesque, there is more distinc-
tive color in it. In the music you have the
rough miners lives, the intimacy of camp
life, and the silences of the great redwood
forests. Then the orchestration is much
more complex than anything else I have
done. It is much more elaborate ”
Puccini on Puccini is not only an idolater
but he kindly records, for the benefit of the
press, the details of his Great (?) Work.
“Your poor old granny is no more,” sings one
of the Californian boys in the Puccini yell-oh.
Bang goes something in the orchestra.
“Whisky and wa-ter” continues the camp
troubadour. More noise from a few dry-
throated instruments. “My poor dog Tray,”
or something of the kind, is another theme
for this composer’s alleged “complex orches-
tration.” The “Girl” may draw well as a
fine drama. As opera, the worst I wish
Puccini (who must require a very large
size in hats, judging by his remarks) is
that he should be chained to a front theatre
stall every evening for a month, and forced
to listen to his own production without any
interval for rest and refreshment.

Deluge having resumed,
VANDORIAN.
Exile” : The fact that a furious though
noiseless scrimmage is raging in Melbourne
for the shoes of Agent-General Jack Tav-
euneb, while that individual is still wearing
them, moves me to consider this expensive
species of bird. An Agent-General belongs
to a special class. He is a cross between a
consul and a diplomat; and is more fre-
quently ornamental than useful to his
State. The job was created early in Aus-
tralian history, apparently to supply a re-
turned colonist, who had done a little public
work at a big salary, with a comfortable
billet. It was held by such as Dan Cooper,
Saul. Samuel, Andrew Clarke, George
Verdon and Julius Vogel. Then it be-
came the refuge of worn-out politicians—
party backs that, although useless for further
lighting, could not well be sent to the
shambles. Of this class were Murray
Smith, Graham Berry, Duncan Gillies,
Horace Tozer, Dillon Bell and Frank
Dobson. Not one of these was of the
slightest use to the passing Australian in
Condon, or to the State he represented.
Most of them attended the Agency, gener-
ally a dingy, dusty den somewhere in West-
minster, on Monday, when the Australian
mail arrived, and on Friday, when it left.
I he chief duty was, of course, to call on the
stockbroker and tell him to float a loan, and
get it underwritten as quietly as possible.
Some Agents-General have made money by
doing a little underwriting on their own ac-
count—but such cases are rare. There was
a curious aloofness about the old-time Agent-
General, who always seemed to imagine
himself the State he represented; and he
did his duty manfully at all functions which
promised either entertainment or refresh-
ment. But the modern Agent-General
does have to show something for his money.
He has on his hands cold rabbits, colder but-
ter, live immigrants, hot loans, dead sheep
and cows (male and female), as well as
visiting nobodies from “Do,wp Under.” So
it is quite possible that the man who jumps
Taverner’s shoes will receive a mild sur-
prise. The job has changed with the times.
England.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mr. Winston Churchill, First Lord of the Ad-
miralty, explained that the Supplementary Estimates
were made necessary owing to the new German Navy
law, under which four-fifths of the vessels were to be
kept in instant readiness for. war. Such a propor-
tion, he said, was unprecedented in modern practice.
Britain’s programme of construction during the
coming five years would, Mr. Churchill said, be in-
creased. .. .He denied that the transfer of four battle
cruisers to Malta would reduce the Home Fleet below
what the Admiralty considered to be the “irreducible
minimum.”
. The transport workers on strike at the docks are
becoming vicious.... After the Tower Hill meetibg
2000 dockers marched to the docks in two sections.
One upset and plundered two bread vans and com-
mandeered a van loaded with foodstuffs.
— '-Cables.
The proud name, like the clash of bells that
tell of victory,
How many years?—how many months shall
it command the sea?
How long before the colors sink that now so
bravely float,
And England’s pride for aye goes down in
England’s briny moat?
They played the game on sea and land—they
made the sword a law;
They had the money and the men—they
took whate’er they saw ;
The golden loot of half the world went
northward to the Thames;
By right of sword they took it all to make
their diadems.
Wherever treasure lay, or fame, their, fierce
commanders led ;
For ev’ry English stone and field a legionary
bled;
But with each drop of English blood a flood
of treasure went
To fill the coffers brimming full o’er which
grey London bent.
And thus with splendid insolence their end-
less wars they made,
Yet scorned as rulers of the earth to guard
their English trade ;
The boast of Freedom ’neath the flag that
preyed upon the earth
Must still apply to English trade, else what
were Freedom worth?
And now behind the English guns the Eng-
lish cry for bread,
Ydiile all around the conquered earth are
wheatlands colored red !
What madness can it be that turns so
quickly Fortune’s wheel?
Freetrade has made the North Sea home a
workhouse hedged with steel!
N.S.W.
E. FRANCIS.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, July 29, 1912.
My Dear Yarrie, —
Another nightingale is singing of love to
her mate-to-be J. L. McMillan, one of the
young bloods of the Lands Department, has
pledged himself to a nice girl who was lately
a popular member of the nursing staff at the
Homoeopathic Hospital. Mac, these times,
carries Edmund Fisher’s book of verses in
his pocket, and quotes:—
Fair Day Nurse, in her smiles arrayed,
Is graceful to the eye;
Her chatter is a song of life,
Of strength to do and healthful strife
For one and each and all.
Ilis particular fair one is a granddaughter
of John Zevenboom —wise old John, who
was a Melbourne alderman, and vice-consul
for the Netherlands, and a pioneer of the
brush-making trade in this city.
Mrs. Andrew Lyell, who shone in Mel-
bourne’s expensive circles in earlier days,
drifted quietly through this city during the
winter horse-racing, taking note of the many
changes, and here and there recognising a
familiar face. She has gone on to Sydney
to rest and watch the falling rain from a
Potts Point flat.
Jack Thompson, who dropped bachelor-
dom late in life, and married one of the
smart and wealthy Western District Robert-
sons, is now jaunting through Britain with
his wife.
Bridegroom Baillieu dodges about ’Change
with an arm in a sling, the result of a spill
in the hunting paddock. The black scarf
is tied at the back with a dainty bow,
which . plainly bears the impress of many
wifely little pats.
Tom Stone, a Melbourne man, and brother
to the Doughty lady (once “Tryphena,” of
this column), is departing to take up a posi-
tion with the Canadian Fisheries, at Vic-
toria, B.C. His wife and little daughter
follow him in three months or so, when they
have finished the packing. The Doughty
knight was a member of the recent British
Fisheries Commission which journeyed to
Canada, and probably put in a good word
for brother-in-law Tom.
Mollie Atherton, a pretty society damsel
of these parts who has a habit of lionising
theatrical folk, has annexed a fiance abroad.
Captain Sinclaire White is the name of the
lucky man. He is reported to own a com-
fortable income, and Mollie herself will have
a pretty dowry.
Mr. and Mrs. Meyers, of the London
Stores, gave their employees a dance in the
Masonic Hall, last week. The best people
of the softgoods world were unloaded at the
door by overawed horse and oil cabmen, and
collected again with a loud whirring and
clattering of hoofs in the dark and fearfully
cold hours that crawl serpentwise before the
dawn. The dance, which was sandwiched in
between, was quite a model affair.
Johnson, a dental doctor from the U.S.,
with a dressy wife and two smart daugh-
ters, has been dined and smoke-nighted by
the local fangsmiths, and listened to with
respect by the dental students to whom he
has spoken noble words — i.e., that they are
to work for the good of the people, and put
thoughts of amassing wealth into the back-
ground. He didn’t absolutely forbid them
to send in a bill, but went as near it as
humanly possible. The absolutely uncom-
mercial dentist is an impossible ideal, like
the uncommercial grocer.
Mrs. Arthur Payne, mistress of the big,
lonely Scotsburn mansion, is grass widowing
while hubby makes a brief business dash to
London. Later on, they are to take a more
leisurely journey together towards the wil-
derness of top hats.
Mr. and Mrs. Peter Mitchell (he is the
turfy squatter who races Trafalgar) packed
a cabin trunk not long ago, and fled to Fiji
to see how the uncles were looking. The
uncles failing to hold 'their interest, they
repacked and went on to Honolulu, and
thence to America. They were last heard of
in England.
Vivienne, the John Highett chick, who
arrived here last Monday by the Mongolia,
was a delightful revelation to friends and
relatives, who last saw her in the white
frock and blue-sash period of her existence.
Nowadays, she is a smart girl. Grandma
Payne is a cousin of an old-time Governor
of Victoria, the most noble Marquis of Nor-
manby, who cultivated chickens, and was
very economical indeed with regard to en
tertaining. Vivienne, the beauty of these
times, was born in Australia, reared in Cali-
fornia, and spent four years in England and
one in India. So her taste in climate is
fairly cosmopolitan.
Tiny Robertson, whose expensively clothed
little form was once a familiar and welcome
sight on the Block, has become a victim to
frayed nerves and the usual rest cure. Since
her marriage to the late barrister Purves’s
second son, Philip, the pair have dwelt in
the Western District cot which was left to
Tiny, along with a comfortable income, by
her thoughtful father.
Another well-known Melbourne girl who
has given way to an ’itis is Mrs. Norman
Fenner, once Lilian Stirling. These days
she is mostly a boarder in father’s hospital,
and the damaged nerves are giving her
parental adviser much trouble, not to men-
tion anxiety.
Passed through Melbourne recently, pretty
Dora Ohlfsen. She came from Italy to pay
a flying visit to her mother, Mrs. Bagge, and
was in time to see sister Rita before she died.
The little pianiste, who had just come back
from India, passed out very suddenly. The
Bagges, rich in looks and brains, are the
daughters of Charles Ohlfsen, a Ballarat
engineer. Of late years the family’s head-
quarters have been in Sydney.
Maoriland’s newly-stocked Cabinet con-
tains at least two persons of social interest
in these parts. F. M. B. Fisher, who is to
look after the Customs, came to Melbourne
last year. He talked well at every oppor-
tunity. Between times, he played tennis with
his left hand amongst Chirnsides and Powers,
and accumulated a heap of popularity.
Fisher, who looks young with his hat on, is
proud of having been beaten only by the
superhuman Wilding on the Maoriland
courts. He journeyed to Geelong last year,
and took the doubles trophy, with the aid
of Parker, from Sydney. Another new Min-
ister, Captain H. R. Rhodes, is one of the
Rhodes legion, and brother to Mrs. Alister
Clarke, of Bulla ; whilst Alister’s sister shares
liis name and cash. He carries the port-
folio of P.M.G., and wears a South African
medal, gained in the great henpaign that
hatched out Barnum-Powell.
The smart crowd which glides at the
Glassy Harem is talking of a fancy-dress
carnival, which is to happen next Tuesday
(6th). These occasional gaudy nights at
the great cooling chamber in City-road are
usually humorous, and the coming event
promises to be as merry and bright as the
best of them.
The Shakspeare Society will be at it
again this Friday night in the Independent
Hall. The programme announces ’“Macbeth”
as the “business” to come under discussion.
Seems almost sacrilegious to refer to the
Bard’s immortal tragedy as “business”!
Mrs. Bramley reads a paper on Lady Mac,
and Edward Stevens is to argue the question
whether Shakspeare’s text has been tam-
pered with, and, if so, who was the tam-
perer? Sol Bloom and Miss Lazarus will
recite scenes and passages, and Rev. Sugden
will have a say before the wild night closes.
But, seemingly, no member of the society
is game to take the platform as the trinity
of witches.
The vagaries of the Victorian Artists’
Society, as_ regards dress, baffle the truly
“Bohemian” section. The dauber’s ordinary
working clothes, which accord so well with
the official premises’ general air of dilapi-
tion, are mostly the order of the evening.
But suddenly, without warning of any kind,
the place breaks out into evening dress, and
the cheerful casual who arrives in plain duds
with the stains of work still on his fingers
is terrified by a roomful of bare shoulders,
white shirt-fronts and the sad black coat-
tails that are the carnival sign of Utter
Respectability. The unconventional soul
who loathes evening dress would as soon be
confronted by a roomful of lost souls. In
fact, he would prefer it. A batch of lost
souls would make an excellent study,
whereas a number of people in and out of
uniform are utterly commonplace.
The Ladies’ Kennel Club held its annual
bark amidst mud, in Wirths’ Circus, last
week. The Pomeranians and pugs curled
up disgustedly, and snorted at the wintry
draughts as if to say that drives in closed
motors were the only fit amusements for
such weather. One pug was priced at £250.
I didn’t buy it. The nerve tension involved
in seeing that Capital didn’t leave the back-
yard would have killed me in less than a
week. Besides, so far as I am aware, you
can’t hurry into a bank and get the cashier
to accept your dog as a fixed deposit, suppos-
ing that you desire to temporarily leave the
country. I think I would prefer to have a
£250 bear or some large furred animal that
could be made into a coat and muff when
dead. Mrs. Elliott had a wonderful run of
luck with her field spaniels, and got away
with three trophies and a blue ribbon amidst
a howling wilderness or blizzard of dog.
The Arts and Crafts people cordially in-
vite loan contributions of pottery for the
next exhibition. Anyone with an elephan-
tine and disastrous Mary Anne on the pre-
mises should seize this chance of temporarily
putting the fragile household treasures be-
yond reach of her reckless duster and raw,
barbaric elbow.
The two women’s clubs here (they are
known respectively as “The Gas Club” and
“The Sour Grapes”) are flourishing. The
Gas Club recently almost enrolled 70 mem-
bers ; but only 69 managed to pass through
the needle’s eye. The Sour Grapes is also
putting forth new shoots, and the hallstand
bears a heavy crop of damp umbrellas these
wintry days.
The Malvern second edition of the “Kis-
met” dance, held in aid of a charity, wasn’t
entirely successful from Mrs. Grundy’s point
of view. Some of the smartest people went
along; but they mostly flung discretion to
the draughts, and dared to perform the
Bunny Hug, the Turkey Trot, and the
Grizzly Bear. Then the guests—or some of
them —considered that the supply of liquid
refreshment was inadequate. Reserves were
called out from the Mayor’s cupboard, and
additional supplies were procured from a
neighboring hotel. Some couples, later, mis-
took a private garden for a sitting-out pad-
dock, and others wandered sentimentally into
an adjacent churchyard. The ticket money
is not exactly pouring in, and a puzzled
Mayoress is wondering how hard she will
have to jog some of the revellers’ memories.
Mrs. Walters, an attractive young matron,
has written three songs for tenor Paul
Dufault. The dainty bits of melody were
not quite ready for the concert co.’s Mel-
bourne season, but are to be warbled else-
where in the State.
Cisneros, arrayed brilliantly in Paddy
green and gold, and wearing a gold snake
with emerald eyes wound round her hair,
gave a farewell matinee concert to a good
house. Bland Holt shed tears in the front
row at “Kathleen Mavourneen,” whilst Mrs.
Bland hid under a black hat fitted with
white wings. Possibly she wept unobserved.
Mrs. Mayoress Wilks, of Malvern, sat among
the most select suburban set. The propor-
tion of men in the audience was fully 50
per cent. —an unusual matinde spectacle.
The Fuller lady came to the Repertory
Theatre’s production of Louis Esson’s
comedy in a splendid fur overcoat, accom-
panied by Captain Wheatley. Prime Minis-
ter Fisher left a sitting House to watch the
Prime Minister on the stage. He wore a
workaday grey sac suit, and contrasted dem-
ocratically with the beautiful evening-dress
Help who sat on the other side of him. But
then the life-work of a Help is to look beau-
tiful in immaculate evening dress.
Melba is delaying her flight Londonward
in order to attend the Newsboys’ Ball, on
August 7. Lady Fuller fans with a most
expensive hat the enthusiasm of society
dames, who are motoring round the city
leaving placards at shops with priceless, gold-
stuffed smiles, and, where possible, getting
help from charitably-inclined firms. “Fancy
and plain dress” is the order of the evening.
Henri Hoile, a young, six-foot artistic
sun-worshipper, who dwells with his wife
somewhere in the hill country round Mel-
bourne, has been putting some of his god
on canvas. The pair have brought half a
hundred pictures into this sunless city, and
hung them for inspection in the Besant
Rooms, in the new Centre Way. A glimpse
of a big white gum with trunk stripped of
bark, and its proud head high in the air, is
calculated to catch the eye of every home-
loving Australian. A breezy seascape, “Off
Williamstown,” might be any salted view,
with a stormy sunset brooding over it. Most
of his sea bits have been captured round the
bay. An interesting show, and well worth
a pilgrimage!
From my Adelaide correspondent
The Point-to-Point Steeplechase transpired at Sea-
ton on Saturday. Frank Downer —a one-time M.F.H. —
drove the Governor and his two daughters to the
scene of action, on the Hunt Club coach. There were
only six starters, and three of the steeds were owned
by the Master —W. S. Bright. Black Sea, a previous
winner of this event, was steered to victory by Mr.
Neville Colley, whose own horse, Mark Time, was
second, ridden by Mr. P. D. Bright. Tea was rushed
by an immense crowd, with an enthusiasm that was
absolutely embarrassing. It was ever thus where free
feeds were concerned. After tea there was a run
with the hounds over the Point-to-Point course, and
a dinner at Bricknell’s cafe wound up a strenuous
day. Amongst the crowd on the road were Dr. and
Mrs. O’Leary, who had a horse in the race ; the Fen-
ners, John Corbins, Bronte Smeatons, Albion Tolleys,
Hylands, Alan Macfarlanes and a host of others.
Mrs. Private Secretary Rowe and Mrs. Nat Camp-
bell gave a bridge and skating afternoon at the Elite
Rink last week. Lady Bosanquet and her daughters
were there, and the Governor looked in for a few chill
moments. Mrs. Row’s frock was of black taffeta,
with touches of vivid green. Her colleague wore pale
grey and pink. Among the guests Were Mrs, Ralli,
Mrs. and Miss London, Mrs. O’Halloran Giles, Mrs.
Herbert Rymill, Miss Cudmore and her sister, Mrs.
Way and Mrs. Julian Ayers.
Costume balls are going very strong just now.
The recent one, in aid of the Ladies’ Clothing Guild,
seemed to fill the Exhibition Building with all the
bright-hued rags in the Commonwealth. Another in-
stitution to profit by humanity’s innate love of gyrat-
ing to music was the Home for Incurables.
Mrs. Michael Hawker gave a so-called flapper dance
at Walkcrville on Friday evening. It was not what
one might call intolerantly flapperish, since no dance
can live by flappers alone, so to speak, nowadays.
The adult female and her friend, the grown-up young
man, were there in considerable quantities, and the
thing was a vast success.
Mrs. Maxwell Fowler, Mrs. R. Colley and Mrs.
Julian Ayers asked their friends to tea at the Cafe
Rubeo on Friday afternoon. The Rows gave a dinner
there on Tuesday, with the object of buoying people
up for the mask and domino ball, and Mr. “Bob”
Melrose did the same by about 30 guests, with a like
kindly object. The ball at Bricknell’s went well, and
hostesses Fenner, H. and A. Rymill, Murray and
O’Leary had the glad hand of congratulation and
approval extended to them, with great heartiness.
Mr. and Mrs. Harry Dutton left for Sydney last
week, and I see they have been foregathering with
Vice-Royalty in that much-wronged old city—a
chronic habit of theirs, by the way. Miss Luscombe
and the Sidney Ayers deserted us about the same
time; likewise Captain Fletcher.
Mr. C. J. Symon was formally admitted to the
South Australian Bar on Tuesday. He is a son of
’Siah, and is understood to be afflicted or menaced
with an intellect.
Ambassador Bryce hustled good and hard, as they
are accused of saying in the U.S., while in these
parts. He visited every public building, from libra-
ries to hospitals, interviewed everyone, inquired into
polities and railway schemes, and generally made
things hum. His final effort was to dash down and
see the Point-to-Point, and then tear back and catch
the Melbourne express, and so away like a grey, hard-
bitten old streak of lightning.
A lady who calls herself “one of the unclaimed
Blessings,” has been clamoring in the Register for
Protection. It appears that an English girl sent her
photo, to the editor, and asked to be put into com-
munication with “some nice Australian boy,” at
which inequitable and unsportsmanlike bit of poach-
ing the local Blessing was naturally exasperated. She
wants the editor to discourage all such designing
minxes, on the ground that there are “hundreds o'?
Australian girls, hard-working, smart and prettv
who can’t marry, because the young men don’t give
them the chance.”
Yours affectionately,
JOHANNA.
On the evidence of recent happenings, every
penny spent on the improvement of Nam-
bucca “harbor” (N.S.W.) has been wasted.
For three months the wretched place has
been silted up. Vessels inside couldn’t get
out, and vessels outside couldn’t get in, and
enormous quantities of produce have rotted
away while waiting for a chance to escape.
Nambucca is one of the horrors of the con-
gestion policy. It ranks among the many
places which have been encouraged because
of their rottenness and incapacity—in other
words, because they are so bad that only tiny
vessels can use them, and these vessels, being
too small for long voyages, must tranship at
Sydney, for the profit of the Sydney middle-
men. Their recomrdendation is that they are
feeders of Sydney—very poor feeders, too —
but can never be rivals. A sane policy would
develop the few good deep-water ports that
N.S.W. possesses, and concentrate the adja-
cent trade there by a network of railways
as is done in the case of Sydney, which
doesn’t profess to run little subsidiary har-
bors at Botany Bay or the mouth of the
Hawkesbury. And it would give up try-
ing to patch hopeless penn’orths of slop
like Nambucca. This careful cultivation of
bad harbors .to the exclusion of good ones
grows too expensive. A stereotyped cry of
the Tory when any reform was threatened
used to be: “What will they think of us in
England?” If some day England is sup-
plied with a full account of the policy which
is responsible for Nambucca and such like
places, it will probably think a great deal
about us.

From the Oldest Inhabitant:—
A glimpse of Governor Bourke , is to be obtained
in Thomas Carlyle’s “Irish Journey,” made in 1849:
“Wednesday, 25th July. Sir Richard Bourke, a
fine old soldier, once Governor of N. S. Wales, a man
of 75 or 80: Something still military, mildly arbi-
trary, in his household government (I find), and
ways of procedure. Interesting kind of old Irish-
British figure. Lean, clean face, hacked with sabre
scars and bullet scars; inextinguishably lively, grey-
bead eyes; head snow-white; low voiced. Bought
this place on his return 30 years ago; a
black, bare bog then, beautifully improved
now, shaded with good wood, neat little houses
and offices, neat walks, sunk fences, drains and
flourishing fields ; again the ‘stamp of a man’s image.’
I smoke, and, lounge about the grounds all morning,
having breakfasted with ‘Master Richard,’ who is off
to Limerick for the day. Welcome enough solitude.
The two ladies kind and polite, ditto the young Eng-
lishman. Solitude is preferable.”
There are other mentions of Richard Bourke —one
after he had driven Carlyle into Limerick to pick up
Charles Gavan Duffy at the hotel, where he (Car-
lyle) is transferred to the care of a Galway editor,
Edward Butler—the same Butler who was afterwards
Attorney-General of N. S. Wales. The “Master
Richard” of Carlyle’s account was in N. S. Wales
as Private Secretary, but was packed off in 1834, as
the result of some scandal at Parramatta, with a
young girl in the case. Sir Richard left his wife
in St. John’s Cemetery, Parramatta. Numerous
descendants of the family remain in Sydney in the
Deas-Thomson family. As for “Master Richard.” he
never returned to his wild oats, but lived on at Thorn-
field, in Ireland, where he died only seven or eight
years ago, aged 92.

POLITICAL
POINTS
“8.J.H.0.”: Bloomfield Rees, M.L.C.
(Vic.), who has just returned from Pata-
gonia, where he has been endeavoring to
secure Welsh settlers for this country, states
that the Six Hatters lie is still alive and
kicking over there. At Valparaiso a ship-
ping company objected to taking immigrants
to Australia, unless they deposited sufficient
coin to pay their return passage in the
event of this country pushing them into the
sea. Everywhere Rees found that the old
lie was swallowed whole by the inhabitants,
even as good Christians swallow the quaint
narrative of Jonah and the whale. And the
Stinking Fish Tories who ushered the
shameless Six Hatters prevarication and its
twin brother, the Petriana myth, into the
sunlight, are now howling loudly for immi-
grants and demanding to know why they
aren’t arriving faster.
In the commercial columns of Melbourne
Age there lately appeared a brief paragraph
stating that, according to usually reliable
calculations the area sown with wheat in
the Weary State this year is approximately
2,274,275 acres. That is a fair-sized pad-
dock—but. according to the Victorian Gov-
ernment Statist, the area sown last year
was 2,408,454, and the year before it was
2,702,460, and those figures don’t look
pretty. Practically, they mean that some-
where near a thousand square miles of land
have gone out of wheat cultivation. Mean-
while, the State has placed something like
2000 families on closer settlements, including
about 000 irrigators, so evidently the real
progress the State is making in breaking up
and settling its locked-up lands would do
little credit even to a snail.
Within the last 12 months the stock of
gold in the N.S.W. banks has diminished by
£2,243,866, and this outflow wasn’t caused
the exchange of gold for Australian
notes,_for the stock of notes only increased
by £253,671. It is merely a case of capital
leaving the country. Exactly how it is
leaving is shown afresh by the trade re-
turns. For the first six mouths of 1912 the
two most populous States, N.S.W. and Vic-
toria. imported from over sea £26,746,945
worth of foreign goods, which is £4,615,515
more than during the first half of 1911.
There was no corresponding increase in the
export of goods, so the two States sent
away over £4,000,000 of gold in part pay-
ment, and as they didn’t produce anything
like that amount their stock of coin dimin-
ished alarmingly. The flight of capital is
plain enough—likewise the reason of the
flight.
A MATTER OF BUSINESS.
We are here to talk not only to this audience, but
to the people of New South Wales, and beyond and
above all -we want to talk to the people of the rest
of. the world. — Premier McGowen.
McGOWEN: “Go on! Git! I have something
to day to these more important gentlemen."
Some day the world will be able to decide
whether Britain is really having a revolu-
tion or not. In its beginnings, a revolution
and a mere period of unrest are often so
much alike that the case can’t be immedi-
ately diagnosed. The famous French up-
heaval had been going on for a few years
before it was identified. Meanwhile, strikes
and riots appear to grow larger in Bull’s
country, and the Suffragettes who throw
axes at the Prime Minister are acquiring a
better aim. Also, lie has been seized and
shaken by a disaffected person, and wdien a
Prime Minister is shaken immediately after
a heavy dinner, it is unpleasant.
The defaulting or levanting husband hav-
ing, of late years, grown into an absolute
custom of the matrimonial trade, each of the
six States is now inviting itself, and the
other five, to pass measures designed to force
married males to do their duty by their
families. The badly-needed reform could
be achieved more easily, less expensively
and with absolute certainty by the Aus-
tralian Parliament were the Constitution So
amended as to give it power to do so. As
things are, the chances are that one can-
tankerous House in one of the States will
reject or radically amend the measure, as
a result of which that State will acquire a
sudden increase of population through its
being a sanctuary for the basest variety of
grass widower that the world wots of, and
the whole scheme will fall to pieces, and the
six blessed Bills will be things of naught
and wads of waste paper.

“Leo Lear” : Talking of lighthouses—no
one was, but what matter? —the far wes-
tern Victorian ship-warner at
Cape Nelson has a history. Just
after Berry had mown down
opposition throughout the Cab-
bage Garden in the elections of
1877, several members met to
pass the time o’ night in Stutt’s
Hotel, Bourke-street. Peter
Lalor was there. He mentioned
that Berry had offered him
the Customs. However, he said
that his majority in Grant was
too small to allow him to
accept. One Tytherleigh, from
the Far West, promptly said
that Peter could have his seat
in Normanby in the event, of
defeat. Lalor was duly grate-
ful, and 'threateHed Tyther-
leigh that he would yet do him
a good turn. On becoming a
Minister he sought out the
friendly stranger and asked
if he wanted anything. “Yes,”
replied Tytherleigh, “I want a
lighthouse for Cape Nelson.”
And he got the one that is there to-day.
A quaint feature of the Parliamentary
shindy over the appointment of engineer
Chinn to supervise the construction, of the
Ivalgoorlie-Port Augusta railway was the
mock indignation of King O’Malley, who, pro-
fessed that the attack on a man who was not
able to speak there in his own defence was
“one of the most shameful things that had
happened.” Nobody can speak in Parlia-
ment unless he is a member, and if a man
is jobbed into a salaried position for which
he is wholly unfit, he can’t be a member,
there being a law to prevent that kind of
duplication. So O’Malley’s heated argu-
ment was ' really to the effect that no sus-
pected job should be questioned in Parlia-
ment, because the alleged jobbee is always
out of Parliament. Even the wildest insur-
ance agent should recognise the inadequacy
of that defence.
FREEDOM IN POLITICS.
We are not without our impracticable cranks in
Australia, who consider themselves too large-souled
and too independent to work with any party.... The
party system honestly and reasonably worked on
Liberal lines, is still the best device which Britons
have discovered for carrying on Government.
—Argus.
THE PRESS AND COK.: "Not that we f' wun
moment b’leeves in caucuses ; but we finds
'em just as easy t' manage this way!”
The direct result of certain persons’
assumed superiority to Royal Commissions
is seen in the Amending Bill now before the
Representatives. The measure isn’t long
but it is sudden. It
raises the penalty for
failing to attend, when
summoned to give evi-
dence or to produce
documents, from £5O
to £5OO, with a similar
increase for refusing
to give evidence. If a
witness persistently
refuses to attend he
may be 1 arrested and
detained until released
by the chairman of the
commission that wants
his evidence, power
being given the ex-
ecuting officer to break
into and enter any
building or vessel in
order to effect the cap-
ture. Arrest does not
absolve the culprit
from the payment of
any fine inflicted, and
a second offence will
cost him from £5OO to
£lOOO. Five years’
imprisonment is the
punishment for giving
false evidence, or for
bribery or attempted
bribery of a witness.
The perpetration of
fraud on a witness or
th e destruction of
documents will mean
two years’ gaol; while
a £5OO fine or one
year’s gaol is the guer-
don of anyone who
prevents a witness
from attending, or in-
jures him on account
of evidence, or dis-
misses a witness from
employment. Common
or garden contempt
of commission will
mean £lOO or three months. A final clause
makes it not retrospective as to facts or
evidence, but applicable to future proceed-
ings and evidence to be given before com-
missions already in existence —the Sugar
Commission, f’rinstans. So things are going
to be cut very short —thank Heaven.
Another boost is given to the world’s food
scarcity and its rising prices. The Nether-
lands Government has forbidden the export
of rice from Java and the rest of the Dutch
Indies, and has taken them definitely out of
the list of planes on which England and
such-like lands can rely for their food supply.
Population has become so dense in the Dutch
Indies that the paternal Government reckons
there is no longer any surplus food. Specu-
lators are still willing to buy the grain for
export, but the old baggy white father at
Amsterdam thinks he should consider the
health and stomach of his own nigger first.
This habit of prohibiting the export of food
is likely to grow.
“Melb” writes: —
Taffy Rees, a member of the Victorian Parliament,
incurred some newspaper jokery last year, through
going to Patagonia to fetch Welsh people. Yet he is
justified, for he has secured 31 families as a first
batch. Each has not less than £lOOO, and one has
£3OOO. The first consignment of Welshers went to
Patagonia in 1856. There are nearly 4000 settled in
the fertile Chubut Valley, under the Argentine Gov-
ernment. Yet they are not happy. They are like
Jews or Boers preserving nationality l and religion.
The Argentines want them to conform. The Welsh
won’t naturalise, partly because they hate military
service, and also because the Spanish language taught
in the schools hasn’t enough consonants for them.
The Canadians enticed some away to Calgary, N.W.
America, but they find the seven months’ snow nasty.
A particular grievance is through the Argentine Gov-
ernment not allowing the seed export of alfalfa or
lucerne from the Chubut Valley, because it is so pecu-
liarly rich. Nevertheless, the old fogies and fogiesses
(in the peaked witch hats) are intensely attached to
the Chubut, and say to the ardent younger people:
“You may go to Australia and fare worse.” What
do we say? Anyhow, the Welsh do better than poor
Apostle Lane, in Paraguay.
“Galleryite” on the Chinn outbreak: —
I’ve dangled my ears over various Parliamentary
galleries long enough to hear many unsavory thing's
about giving away Government billets ; and the accu-
mulated wisdom of my years leads me to the pro-
found conclusion that it would be a condemned hard
thing to find an intelligent officer outside the Public
Service who has not taken an intelligent interest in
politics. Therefore, I rule the objection that Chinn
was a Labor supporter to be out of order and in con-
travention of the rules of May which forbid irrele-
vance. But, nevertheless, I want an answer to this
statement by Oppositionist Fowler: —
“An advertisement appeared in the Commonwealth
Gazette on February 24, 1912, weeks after the ap-
pointment had been made, calling for applications
from engineers for the Port Augusta-Kalgoorlie rail-
way line (two required).”
Were there only two billets available, and had Chinn
already got one? Kink O’Malley should explain this,
or I’ll remain dissatisfied.
“Locust” writes :—■
Professor Bryce (or Mr. Bryce, as he prefers to be
called now), may not be aware how profoundly his
book on the “American Commonwealth” influenced
the Australian Commonwealth Constitution makers
in 1891 and 1897 and ’9B. The book was quoted by
tlie column. It obsessed Barton, O’Connor, Downer,
the draftsman in the latter convention, as it had
Griffith, Deakin and Barton, and even Kingston, in
the earlier (1891) convention. It is probably the
cause of a number of our constitutional difficulties to-
day, inasmuch as our leaders followed American pre-
cedents as slavishly as was compatible with the
equally slavish copying of the “unwritten” Consti-
tution' of Britain with party government at its apex.
Continental examples, useful as many of their con-
stitutional devices are, were hardly considered, and
a quotation from Bryce easily demolished any refer-
ences to them.
The prospect of having to turn themselves
into decently honest, patriotic citizens is
sending the Rodney (Yic.) landowners
(where Sammy Lancaster and the Kya-
bram movement came from) crazy. Pressed
by Irrigationist Mead, Premier Watt and
Minister Graham have ukased that “the
water is available for irrigation, and it will
be charged for, at the rate of ss. per acre
per annum, whether it is used or not” ; to
which Rodney has replied, through its pre-
cious municipal council, that, “if that is so
half the present settlers will be gone inside
five years.” That would be a dire calamity
were it not for one drawback, viz., that
these departing settlers cannot take the
water and the land with them, consequently
they must be replaced by other settlers. Iu
which case the public, which has footed so
many and such heavy bills for Rodney,
would merely urge the departers not to de-
lay their trek for five years. Five months
would be long enough.
“Vulcan”: The report that the Yankee
Meat Trust was about to plant . its large,
competition-obliterating hoof in this country
at Queensport (Q.) was denied with the
sudden promptitude one usually expects from
a non-existing person. A few days later the
“Australian Meat Export Company, Limited,
capital £200,000, in 200,000 £1 shares,” was
registered in Brisbane, with the following
subscribers: —
Andrew J. Thynne, solicitor; William E. Lalor,
solicitor; David J. Hickey, accountant; Thomas
Goodall, law clerk; George H. Brittain, law clerk;
Michael A. R. Becher, law clerk ; and Bernard T.
McShea, law clerk, all of Brisbane.
It merely remains to remark in passing that
some of the above are the class of gentlemen
one usually expects to have £200,000 lying
about handy, and that it is quite natural,
and in the usual order of things, that they
should use it: — &
To purchase, sell, exchange, import, export, grow,
prepare for market or export, and turn to account
cattle, sheep, pigs, and live stock of every kind, and
carry on the business of butchers, etc.
What two solicitors, an accountant and four
law clerks don’t know about the meat busi-
ness isn’t worth knowing.
THE WOOING.
Luck isn’t Tasmania’s strong point. Dur-
ing the quarter which ended on March 31,
1912, the Federal Statistician estimates that
the population of the island diminished by
2116. That of the mainland increased by
35,761, and all the mainland States had a
look in. But the Strawberry Isle got the
glassy stare from Providence, as usual.
WHEN CONSOLS WENT TO SEVENTY-FOUR!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
London, July 19.
Consols sold to-day at £74. — Cable.
When Consols went to Seventy-four—’twas eighty-
seven years ago—
John Bull was getting over AA r ar, and Trade was very
(scarce and slow ;
And bread was dear, and strikes not few,
And mighty was John’s I O U ;
And Banks were closing by the score—
When Consols went to £74 !
When Consols went to Seventy-four—’twas but last
week, as you may see—
John Bull was half expecting AVar, while Jobs were
scarce, but very Free;
In fact they had begun to fly
AVhile Englishmen stood idly by—-
Perhaps this is the reason why
Consols have gone to £74!
J.SAV.
OLD ALLEYNIAN.
WHERE ARE YOU GOING?
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Where are you going, my pretty trade?”
“I’m going to foreigners, sir,” she said.
“Why can’t you stay here, my pretty trade?”
“Because you won’t help me, sir,” she said.
“What is your father, my pretty trade?”
“My father’s Protection, sir,” she said.
“Say, will you marry me, my pretty trade?”
“If you’ll protect me, sir,” she said.
“What is your fortune, my pretty trade?”
“I would be your fortune, sir,” she said.
“But Cook wouldn’t like it, my pretty trade!”
“Nobody asked him, sir,” she said.
“You are a simpleton, Alf,” she said.
N.s.w.
E. O'F.

Having beerf in the piano business so long, and having
accumulated so much experience, we decided some
years ago to have a piano made specially to our
own design* This instrument had many improve-
ments specially adapted to local conditions, but the
chief object we had in view was the construction of
a piano that would stand true in tone in any climate.
So successful was the first piano, that we immedi-
ately placed large standing orders with the makers
for the supply of these instruments, and since then
we have sold thousands upon thousands to pleased
