SPORTING
NOTIONS
Barry’s overthrow of Arnst by four
lengths on the Thames, last Monday afternoon, hit
most Australasians like a blow on the chin from a
bar of iron. But the minority were not surprised.
To begin with, Arnst made the mistake of under-
estimating his opponent. Then he had not been too
well. Also, a lot of time, which might have been
more advantageously occupied, was devoted to pigeon-
shooting. Pigeon-shooting, although it is the sort
of vice that rots a man’s soul, is not physically in-
jurious in itself. But it is damaging in a round-
about way. Most pigeon-shooters drink like fish
when the shooting is over, probably to drown con-
science ; and mixing up with a crowd of boosy bird-
murderers is no good to any athlete. Then again,
Arnst is a professional athlete, and his only exercise
is his training. Barry is a coal-heaver, and when
lie’s not sculling he’s heaving for his life ; and he is
a careful-living young man. Having no means where-
with to paint the town red, he leaves the town its
natural color. Arnst learned to scull in smooth
water, and when he met Barry on the Zambesi the
race was rowed in smooth, sluggish water. Arnst
was accustomed to that and Barry was not. Also, on
that occasion, Barrv was far from being in the best
of health. This time the race was rowed on a swift-
running, jobbly stream, to which Barry was accus-
tomed and Arnst wasn’t. Arnst was the man who
had not been too well, and, altogether, he was in
much the same position in England as Barry was in
Africa. He won the toss, and, choosing the southern
side of the river, started at 38 strokes to Barry’s
36, and immediately went to the front. But he
never got more than a length and a half away.
Barry let him lead about half way, and then began
to creep up, and passed him. Next he made the pace
hot —in fact, he boiled it- —and cracked up Arnst
800yds. from the finish, dead beat. Barry hurried
on, and covered the distance in 23min. Bsec.
The dearth of motor cycle operatives in
Britain is likely to lead to the establishment of a
new Australian industry. So far behind demand are
the British suppliers that some of them, with a
remnant of that magnificent hide which enabled the
race to do a world’s record in national stealing, are
actually asking for £l2 premium for the prompt
delivery of a machine. A Melbourne firm has laid
down a large assembling factory, and has also,
somehow, managed to break through the British
makers’ determination not to supply parts for
assembling abroad, and arranged for a regular sup-
ply. At present, at least 80 per cent, of the bikes
used in Australia are locally assembled; and if the
patent parts can be obtained at a reasonable figure,
there is no reason why the motor-bikes should not
hold the same high place on the list of home
manufactures.
* * * *
The cyclist is the wharf-lumper of ath-
leticism —for ever in a turmoil. The bodies that
govern cycling seem to be largely composed of bull-
headed ruffians, who have neither the sense to avoid
going out into the rain or to come in out of it when
they find they are getting wet. The latest trouble is
the riot between the Victorian League of Wheelmen
and the Victorian Inter-Club Cycling Association, a
body which has the strenuous support of the Vic-
torian Cycle Traders’ organisation. They are fight-
ing for the control of'Victorian road-racing; and by
the time they have finished there will be no cycling
to control. Already the big Dunlop Rubber Go.
has notified both parties that while the war is raging
there will be no action taken towards promoting the
Warrnambool to Melbourne road race; and that if
the row isn’t ended quickly the classic race will be
dropped altogether for this year, if not for ever. It
will be a killing blow to the sport if the Warrnam-
bool is allowed to drop, but the Dunlop Cos. is de-
termined to abolish it unless a conference is held and
a working agreement come to between the rival
factions.
The League of Wheelmen has itself to blame
for the trouble. It was apparently quite un-
interested in road racing, and if it did not dis-
courage it, damned it with such faint support that
open hostility would have been better. So the road!
racers asked to be allowed to appoint a governing
body of their own, to be affiliated with the League
of Wheelmen. Then the League rose, and in an
arrogant manner talked about mutiny and sudden
suppression, and other drastic things. It declined
to allow the road racers to manage their own affair,
and it flatly refused to do anything towards properly
managing their affairs. So the road racers formed
a strong body of their own, and seceded from the
League. What else the League expected them to do
would be interesting to know. A little tact, and
some decency and fair play when the first vigorous
blast of protest blew along would have soothed
things. But the League boxed up what little tact it
had, and reached for the axe —to find that the edge
was chipped and blunt, and the sprung.
That attempt to fly by means of machines
propelled by human power only, recently promoted in
Paris by the Peugeot Cos., was a dead failure, the
conditions demanded that the machine should be
made to fly or jump 33ft. in one direction, and 10
minutes later fly or jump back again to where it
had started from. No less than 200 entered, but
only 25 faced the starter ; and of these -not one flew,
or ’even raised himself off the ground. Which re-
minds that there is a weird tale in a far back num-
ber of an old-time English monthly, the Gentleman s
Magazine, of a flying man who gyrated over London
on a pair of planes, shaped like wings, propelled
by some mechanism which was driven by his feet.
He was ordered by public notice to desist, but ignored
the orders, and a warrant was issued for his arrest.
As nobodv could serve it he continued to fly at in-
tervals, but came to an untimely end through being
caught in a sudden gale of wind, and dashed against
a chimney, off which he ricochetted to the ground.
Somehow, this writer has always had his doubts about
the veracity of that yarn; and the experiment in
Paris makes him even more doubtful. #
New York authorities have made an ex-
haustive series of tests as to what speed motorists
should travel at while scudding through towns. In
Parramatta, and other municipalities close around
Sydney, the motorists are warned by red discs with
the figures painted on in white, to reduce speed to
four miles an hour. No motorist ever does reduce
speed to four miles an hour, and as there are prac-
tically no police, the motor-hog is a law unto him-
self. But when one goes hogging himself, he has to
admit that four miles an hour is a ridiculous speed,
and a dangerous one, for the car is well nigh unman-
ageable, owing to lack of proper way. The N’Yorkers
have decided that in the busy city streets 15 miles
per hour is a safe and reasonable speed ; 20 m.p.h.
in streets where houses are 50 yards apart, and 25
m.p.h. on open roads. And that it is about correct
will be endorsed by every fair-minded motorist. At
these speeds any pr.operly braked car .should be cap-
able of being brought to a standstill in one second. A
car that cannot should not be allowed on the roads.
Saturday was a horrible day m Sydney —
a day to be spent close to a fire, with a good book,
a bottle of the best, and a sweet cigar tucked into
the middle of the face. And yet—amazing as it may
seem —there were thousands of folk in Sydney so
devoid of home comforts that two League football
matches alone drew 21,000 people. Talk about
sending missions to the heathen! What is wanted
is a huge crusade to force women to make the
home comfortable, so that a man shall not have to
go to a football match on such a day to get away
from dull, drab monotony. However, “the play’s
the thing,” as some defunct immortal once remarked.
Eastern Suburbs was booked to meet South Sydney,
and it was a generally expressed idea that it would
be “a great lark” if South downed the premier team.
Give it its due, South “did its darnedest,” but its
darnedest wasn't darned enough; and Eastern Sub-
urbs, sadly bedraggled and mud-stained, climbed out
pantingly with 10 points to South’s seven. New-
town, apparently, had been looking on the liquor
when it is yellow, instead of attending to training,
for, when it bumped Glebe, it fell flat. Glebe did
the steam-roller act, and finished up 23 points to nil.
Newtown citizens arc now wearing crape round
their hats. Balmain fell on the struggling Western
Suburbs, like the dingoes on the bunnies —25 to two.
Annandale put up a great scrap against the moun-
taineers from the crags of the tram-line at North
Sydney, and was defeated only by seven to three.
So Eastern Suburbs is still top dog in the competi-
tion with 18 points, Glebe has 16, Newtown and South
Sydney 12 each, and Balmain 10. Below that are
sundry clubs wallowing in the abyss of despair.
* * * *
In the Union division the struggle of the
day was that between University and Eastern Sub-
urbs, and, in soite of the evil conditions, about
3000 people looked on. University opened with
dribbling tactics, keeping the ball at the toe till
quite close to the enemy’s line, and then suddenly
whipping it out to the waiting backs. Eastern
Suburbs made valiant but vain efforts to let loose
passing charges—which, considering the state of the
soil and the fact that the ball was like a slow-
moving ham with butter on it, was folly. When it
Was too late the error was recognised and tactics
changed; but recognition came too late, and the
figures were 11 to three. Western Suburbs had a
woolly time with Balmain, and looked like sustain-
ing a defeat. But, somehow, Balmain lacked the
ability to take advantage of. its opportunities—
opportunities'made by haird, solid work; and Western
Suburbs, which was playing for a draw, suddenly
fluked a lucky score and won by six to three. Manly
made mincemeat of North Svdney by 11 points to
three, and South Sydney and St. George abandoned
their match, as their playing ground was under
water, and the ball had to be dived for. In the A
division of the competition. University tops the pole
with 21 points, closely followed by Glebe with 19,
and Eastern Suburbs with 17. In the B division
Western Suburbs leads with 20, and Newtown and
St. George have 16 and 12 respectively. #
In the Australian division, Paddington
S”stained its second defeat of the season at the clogs
of East Sydney. The disaster was probably due
to the absence’ of the four cracks, whose places bad
to be filled hurriedly by less experienced players.
East Sydnev got going well, and finally won by 51
points to 33. The only other match of the day was
a run-away victory of Y.M.C.A. over the Railway
team, the ’figures being 140 noints to 16. Newtown
forfeited to North Shore. Paddington and Svdney
are level in the competition with 46 points. Y.M.C.A.
and North Shore have 36 each, and East Sydney 32.
The rest are nowhere.
* * * *
In the Soccer world the ’bailers are lead-
ing the strenuous life. At Newcastle the Tasmanian
team went under by four goals to one. In the Gar-
diner Cup two more clubs were cut down, the victims
being Balmain B and Canterbury. Balmain was
beaten by Sydney with three to two, and Canterbury
went down to Cessnock bv three to nil. In the
Rawson Cup riot, Balmain beat Newtown by two to
one, and Glebe out.ed Granville with four to one.
The order of precedence stands: Balmain 16, Glebe
15, and Sydney 12 competition points. In the struggle
for the Association Cup, West Sydney, which shares
with Balmain an unbeaten record for the season,
smote Y.M.C.A. beneath the fifth rib by four goals
to one. Northern Suburbs smashed Newtown by four
to one, and Glebe downed the Police with three to
nil. So in oomoet’tion. Balmain and West Sydney
are pegging level with 19, while the other six clubs
have failed to get past single figures.
* * * *
Footballers had some sport on Sydney’s
sodden fields on Saturday. At Balmain one club
pushed the referee into the churned soup in the
early match. The referee is said to have taken the
mud-rolling as a joke. Had he not done so there
almost certainly would have been a riot. The rough
players also had a good time when Rugby Leaguer
A. Halloway broke the first-graders’ record by being
the first player to get marching orders for rough
play during the season. Two junior matches ended
in free fights, in one of which a Y.M.C.A. team
mixed the rage of the vulture and the love of the
turtle very excellently. It certainly turned the
other cheek, but as it did so it remarked. “Kick
it, and see the mess you’ll be in five minutes!”
The other crowd did all these things, and five
minutes later there was a mess indeed.
Football in Adelaide is robbed of its in-
terest this winter through the supremacy of Port
Adelaide, which has not been beaten at all. The
other clubs win among themselves in an in-and-out
sort of way. While “Ports” were away winning
some picnic’ games in Tasmania (S.A. does not allow
payment of players, and the clubs have to do some-
thing to get rid of their accumulating gate-money),
East Fremantle visited Adelaide to play the leading
team, and West Torrens second on the list, had to
do the honors. As it chanced, four of the visitors
got hurt in practice, and two more during the match,
so it was no disgrace for the Westralians to lose
with five goals seven behinds to seven goals 12 be-
hinds recorded by the home team.
* * * *
Maoriland’s “country week” of Rugby
football didn’t gather a halo for the gum diggers, pig
hunters and potato growers. The city folk trod them
unceremoniously in the slush. Canterbury had a
little trouble with the North Islanders, and just
climbed out with a win of 12 points to 11; and
then Taranaki blew down and sank the South
Island by 31 points to three. A fearful thing in a
country where the people take their football more
seriouslv than their prayers.
* * * *
Those weariful gasbags who are always
prating about unexpected reversals of form are hav-
ing plenty of subjects in Victorian hoofball. But a
few weeks ago the League premiers, the kickers of
Essendon, were reckoned almost invincible; to-day
they are scratching for points to get into the finals.
After being thoroughly thrashed by Carlton, and
moderately walloped by South, they went down to
Geelong on Saturday, assuring themselves of an easy
win—and before they got properly going the game
was gone. At half-time they were over eight goals
in arrears —14 points to Geelong’s 63 —and after that
it was just a question of getting out of the road. of
the conquering hoofs of the Seasiders, and avoiding
as much mud as they could. They ultimately
acquired 40 points to Geelong's 92, and, if they have
any explanation to oiler for the feat, now is the
time to hand it out. Geelong’s exceptionally clever
goal-kicking won’t account for it, by a jug-
ful. Carlton, by the laws of logic—if they have
anything to do with football —should have donkey-
licked Melbourne, but its amazing inability to kick
reasonably straight nearly lost it the game, five
points only differentiating the teams at the close —
Carlton, 42; Melb., 37. Collingwood, nursing its
outside chance of reaching the finals, went over to
Richmond and gracelessly piled up points to the
measure of 75 to Richmond’s 40. ’Roy, for a similar
reason, heaped up 86 to ’Varsity’s 50. One sweet
feature about the Students is that they never get
thrashed by a weak team one week and then capsize
a lot of experts the Saturday following. Their con-
sistency in getting trounced is a touching circum-
stance. South had the better of its argument with
the Saints, by 91 points to 60, the latter’s sloppy
kicking accounting for most of the disaster. Just
here this writer would urge Chief Sec. John Weary,
as soon as he awakes, to declare the common, female
hatpin a pest and a nuisance and a thistle under
the Act, so that it may be abolished and destroyed
out of the land utterly. The newest caper of the
ill-mannered Lizzie who wants to avert her team’s
impending defeat is to seize the ball when it gets
among the spectators, and drive her dagger into it,
thereby occasioning delay in getting anew ball, and
much bad language generally. Four balls were thus
treated on Saturday—‘two in one match- —and the
caper grows annoying.
* * * *
In the Ass. games another “surprising
reversal of form” was even more unaccountable. After
keeping among the leaders, and beating almost all
comers throughout the season, Brunswick went over
to Essendon, anticipating a hard game and a win;
but when the board, at half-time, showed ’Don 27
points, ’Wick three, they became persuaded that
something was wrong. The same something got a
good deal wronger as the game went on, and when
finally ’Don finished with 83 points to Brunswick’s
six, the Brickies went home to have a heart-searching
talk with themselves. Even the hopeless, dreary
Melb. Citizens made a braver show than that. Asa
result of this catastrophe, the Brickies must either
beat Brighton on Saturday week or pass out, and
Brighton is just now what the share speculator calls
“a sound proposition,” having handed Williamstown
a • considerable castigation on Saturday—ll 4 points
to 30. The other leading teams. North and ’Scray,
spanked Port and P’ran respectively—7o points to
30, and 73 to 48, being the measure of the pum-
melling—and ’Cote mled up the usual century—
-116 —against the Citizens’ 42. During the week
the Junior Ass. considered the trouble that occurred
during the Yarraville-’Scrav Jrs. match, when lan-
guage and stoush were freely dispensed. After put-
ting on a pained expression, the Ass. disqualified
one player till the beginning of ’l4, and “repri-
manded” four others. Also, ’Scray’s delegate was
requested to produce an unknown player, who
“squirted a mouthful of water in the umpire’s eye.”
What will happen to the delegate if he fails to
discover the unknown gentleman wasn’t stated.
Possibly the whole team may be “reprimanded.”
And its troubles!
Asa Test matcli was pending, captain
Gregory spelled some of his stars before tackling
Hampshire ; so Macartney and Hazlitt stood out with
McLaren and Webster. It was a comparatively weak
XI that burst into Hampshire. Gregory, as usual,
lost the toss, and the county, going in, knocked up
371, of which C. P. Mead accounted for 160 and
Jephson for 55. Carkeek kept the sundries down to
15. Kelleway got C. B. Fry for 24, and the rest of
the wickets were divided between Matthews, Minnett,
Whittv and Emery, none of whom averaged less than
30 per victim. Australia’s first innings, broken by
rain, totalled 197. Much was expected of the Glebe
pair, Bardsley and Kelleway; but though the former
reached 60, his mate was stopped for one. Minnett
made a frantic effort to knock the bowling silly, but
was run out for 58. One Kennedy rattled out six for
90. In the follow-on it was Bardsley who made 46
and Kelleway who got two. Backs to the wall,
skipper Syd came along with 85, and Jimmy Mat-
thews added 45 n.o. The same Kennedy refused to
ring off, and bagged five for 91. The total of 256
saved an innings’ defeat, and left the county with
only 50-odd to get. Whitty and Emery bowled the
first pair that came along for 20 each, and then’ Mead
(33 n. 0.) and Fry (12 n. 0.) hit off the runs, and
made the county a winner by eight wickets.
* * * *
Sussex afforded the last practice before
the Test. Taking no more risks, Macartney was
called in, and Mayne was dropped for McLaren. Also,
Gregory staggered creation by winning the toss. He
joyfully rushed a Wicket made specially for batting,
arid made the most of it. Kelleway 28, Jennings 48,
Macartney 142, Bardsley 43, Matthews seven (this
doesn’t look like a case of making the most of it),
Minnett 23, Gregory 35, Webster 10, McLaren 27,
Emery 16, Whitty n.o. Sims got three of the best,
but 123 runs were knocked off him. A. E. Relf got
four for 159, and Holloway bagged three for 52. In
the total of 398 the century and eight double figures
constituted a record for the tour. The county made
389, Lo-the-poor-Indian Ranji. hitting up 125 in
something like his own old style. Relf 72 and Fen-
der 69, were the only other large smites. Emery
captured three of the early wickets, and had 105 hit off
him. Matthews took two for 74, Whitty one for 84,
and McLaren polished off three tail-enders for 22.
With only nine to the good, Australia continued in run-
getting mood. Jennings fell for six, but Macartney’s
121 (his second century in the match), Matthews’s
93, Kelleway’s 63, Gregory’s 30, did a heap to make
it seven for 338, when Gregory declared. The county
had lost two for 101 when time ran out. The county
wanted 247 to win with eight wickets in hand. So
it ended in a draw.
London Times jumped on P. F. Warner’s
book on the tour of his last team in Australia, and
regrets that he did not take advantage of his enforced
position as an onlooker to write a quite impersonal
review of the proceedings. That he praised the cap-
taincy of Douglas is somewhat discounted by the fact
that, throughout the book, he carefully points out
how much better the team might have been handled
—presumably by Mr. Warner. Also, the Times is' of
opinion that “the good of crick avowed
object of the book —is not advanced in any way by
the devotion of a whole chanter 'to details of the
scrap between Hill and McAlister. Generally, it
sums up the work as a record of disappointment—dis-
appointment in which Mr. Warner will have the full
sympathy of every cricketer. *
Sydney Gregory added to his already in-
glorious career as a “tosser” by calling wrong in
the Test this week. This made 20 failures to call
correctly in 24 matches. Meanwhile, the N.S.W.
police authorities are determined that no practice
will be allowed in two-up schools, and a host of fine
young fellows who were skying the copper with a
view of captaining Australian Xl's some day were
grasped at Broken Hill on Sunday and another lot
at Sydney on Monday night. If McGowen is not
careful he will find himself accused by Wade as
the man who caused Gregory’s ghastly run of bad
calling. # *
N.S.W. Cricket Association opens the
coming season with a credit balance of £4OOO a big
jump, considering that, in 1910, treasurer Green
crave a personal guarantee to the Bank to keep the
Association afloat. The South African and English
visits bulged the coffers somewhat, and the Aswicia-
tion, after stowing some of the boodle away at fixed
deposit, proposes to use the balance in lifting the
game. #
The big winter meeting at Caulfield opens
next Saturday, when the Australian Hurdle Race
will be the richest item on the bill. At time of
writing there are punters prepared to take three to
one or less about AVimmera, and the favorite’s chance
of doing the trick is probably up to the odds. Among
the others there is nothing that, looks to be thrown
into the handicap. Some 17 of the horses that were
cherry ripe at Flemington three weeks ago may have
staled off, and the writer has only a mildly apolo-
getic fancy for Uxbridge, who is not too kindly
treated on his recent form, seeing that it was not
quite good enough to win. But if he has made any
improvement rather than disimprovement, since
Flemington meeting, Uxbridge should be busy at the
end of the long journey.
* * * *
“0.K.”: The story that the V.R.C. com-
mittee proposes to raise its prices and members’ fees,
in order to recover the loss on anew grandstand, that
collapsed whilst a-building, turns out to be a mere
invention of the enemy. The facts of the case, as
supplied by a member who seems to know the figures,
are pretty much as follows: The V.R.C., haying de-
cided months ago to spend £25,000 on improve-
ments to the show place, was suddenly confronted
with a request from the Board of Works to get the
property sewered, as though it were a private resi-
dential estate. The sewering will cost £12,000,
making a total expenditure of £37,000 to be pro-
vided for. Well, the proposed improvements are
essentially a business proposition, incidental to the
Cup Day 'question. Melbourne Cup seemed in danger
of losing some of its crowd, owing to complaints
about the awful want of seating accommodation for
the women on that one day of the year. The new grand-
stand is to give seats to another 6000, and it will
cost £15,000. On the Hill a lot of money is needed
for enlarging the present stand and building a
lounge, or haven of rest, for women under the new
wing. Bookmakers are to be cleared away from the
front of the Hill stand to an outlying part of the
ground, and all these improvements to the half-
crown show place justified an extra “tanner” to the
admission fee. Also, in consideration of the new
grand-stand, it seemed a fair thing to propose an
extra shilling for the “toffs,” who have hitherto paid
half a guinea. These charges being projected, the
committee reckoned that democracy would have cause
for protest if the members, likewise, were not asked
to increase their payment to the upkeep. Hence the
proposed extra £1 in the annual subscription. As
for the “collapse” of the grand-stand whilst a-build-
ing, it happened before the structure was far ad-
vanced, and proved the necessity for spending two
or three thousand pounds on a solid foundation.
The actual cost of pulling down what was already
erected will not be more than £350, which is the
whole of the loss the club has made. All things
considered, the members and the public have nothing
to grumble at. The £5 subscription dates back to
1864, when the Y.R.G. gave only five days’ racing
per annum for the money, and .Flemington racecourse
was a comparative wilderness. Now it is something
of a paradise and much of an Eden, and the racing
days are 16 in the year. Again, as compared with
members of the A. J.C., the members of the Y.R.C.
will not be victimised by a £6 fee, for it costs Is.
to enter the outer gates of the Randwick ground.
An A.J.O. member, who takes himself and two ladies
to each of the 16 days’ racing, pays 48s. in addition
to his £5 subscription. So the malcontents at next
Monday’s meeting of the V.R.G. can already consider
themselves blown out..
* * * *
We’ve changed color since David Gregory
took the first Australian XI to England in 1878.
During that visit, an English parson, meeting the
famous A. G. Steele in front of the pavilion, re-
marked : “Well, I hear you’re going to play against
the niggers to-morrow!” “I am,” said A. G., “and
let me introduce the demon nigger bowler, Mr.
Spofforth.”

Another venerable London land-mark is
told off for execution. The Elephant and
Castle Theatre, South London, is under
offer to the authorities of the neighboring
Tabernacle for £100,000, and as the thou-
sands were rapidly rolling into the hat of
Pastor Dixon when the mail took up its bed
and quitted England, it is highly probable
that the required sum will soon be raised.
This theatre has fallen on evil days, and
has, of late, been only casually occupied by
touring companies. The adjoining hotel is
one of the oldest in London. It was known
to Shakspeare, who alludes to the Elephant
as a London locality in one of his plays.
It is alleged that he acted in its
yard, with people looking on from the
projecting galleries on each floor. In
the pre-railway period the Elephant
was the great coaching house to and
from the South of England. The
theatre has had many vicissitudes and
changes of fortune. It was at one time the
headquarters of the followers of Johanna
Southcote, the servant girl who proclaimed
herself to be the predestined mother of the
“New Messiah.” The event did not come off.
and the gorgeous silver cradle that was pre :
maturely manufactured found a resting place
in the museum of Madam Tussaud. After the
death of Johanna, one of her followers, John
Wroe, gathered up a number of her fol-
lowers into a new sect called the “Christian
Israelites.” Wroe made several missionary
tours in Australia, and died at Fitzroy,
Melbourne, in 1863. Melbourne House, at
Wakefield, Yorkshire, the headquarters of
the sect, was so christened by Wroe in com-
pliment to his Victorian adherents who pro-
vided most of the money.

‘(Melb.”: The pleasaunce, once known as Dream-
land, at St. Hilda Beach, Melbourne, was opened by
Salambo, the fire-eater, a few years ago, as a kind of
rival to Hew York’s Coney Island ; but it turned out
badly. It has since been a waste, a howling void,
and a footless and tangled blank. Now the J. D.
Williams Co. has taken it, and proposes to run all
sorts of variety shows next summer. Also, the three
rival musical shows of St. Hilda —Follies, Jesters
and Pierrots —are polishing their armor and their
harness, and putting the song-and-dance battle in
array.
James Liebling, the ’cellist of the Cisneros concert
co., is a chess-player when he isn’t at work on .the
big fiddle. He ranks as one of the three boss ama-
teur chessarians of George Washington’s country, and
his first thought, when he landed in Sydney recently,
was to get an introduction to the local chess club.
Liebling dropped in there with an apologetic smile,
and had a giddy, silent time of checking and check-
mating. He wiped the board with every ruminating
brother who would take him on. In Melbourne he
repeated his indignities to local talent. When two
or three players in succession had succumbed to the
unsuspected stranger, they went forth and fetched
a champion to push pawns and bishops and suchlike
for the honor of the club. But Liebling overthrew
the champion. Noting his rather melancholy de-
meanor on the concert platform, one would guess
him to be a veritable bobby-dazzler at the saddest
game on earth. Nobody would slap Liebling on the
back and say “Mate!”
One corner of the site bought by the Common-
wealth as the official home of Australia in London
was occupied by the Olympic Theatre, and workmen
are now nosy digging up the foundations of this old
“shop” where 0. V. llrooke made his first metro-
politan appearance on January 3, 3 848. Brooke,
who had already served an apprenticeship of some
years in his native Ireland and the English “pro-
vinces,” scored such an instantaneous success as
Othello that his salary of £25 a week was promptly
doubled; and £5O a week was then regarded as a
princely remuneration for an actor. That season
established Brooke’s fame, and would have established
his fortune had he known how to take care of his
money ; but that was an art he never learnt.
“0.K.”; “Everywoman” is to be staged in London
for the first time, at Drury Lane, next September,
and local theatrical journalism is gravely asserting
that the late Mr. Browne’s “morality play” has
achieved considerable success in Australia as well
as America. Possibly the surface effects of the
melodrama would have been more impressive on
Sydney and Melbourne audiences if .they had opened
their hearts to the sweet tones and good acting of
Hilda Spong, without consideration of the fact that
she was physically unsuited to the name part, except
in the middle scenes. She was neither sufficiently
light and frolicsome at the beginning, nor thin and
careworn at the end. The Drury Lane management
has been trying to get Ethel Irving for “Every-
woman,” which is what the Combine no doubt would
have liked to arrange when that sinuous little artist
was “starring” in another co. Ethel Irving, by the
way, is to appear anon in a new play (from the
French), at George Alexander’s theatre. Meanwhile,
she is doing a comedietta “turn” at the London
Hippodrome, which is a comparatively easy way of
earning a few millions a week. After fulfilling the sub-
sequent engagement with Alexander, and collecting a
new stock of plays, she will start again in manage-
ment on her own. Ethel Irving had to wait a long
time for a big reputation, but, having made it, she
promises to be able to star for many years. Her
art is not confined to young parts. It is adaptable
to any age of woman who has suffered (Lady Fred-
erick was no chicken), and one can conceive her
making a big hit as a white-haired heroine.
“Melb”: Apropos the gallery packer, The Mel-
bourne Board of Health has just adopted a series
of regulations, for approval by the Chief Secretary,
who is the responsible man. They are based on
what has been done at Brennan’s Amphitheatre,
where even the sixpenny seat is numbered, so that,
if only the right quota of tickets are sold, there can-
not possibly be overcrowding. Then there are the luxur-
ious threepenny stalls at the J. D. Williams “shops,”
the Britannia and the Melba. There have been terrific
rushes outside those places, but never the least con-
fusion inside, and the packer is unheard of. Sauce
for the hoss is sauce for the mare also, and what
has been done at three places can be done at all.
George Coppin is one of the Australian mummers
who finds mention in the new vol. of the English
“Dictionary of National Biography.” It was a little
love affair in Dublin that brought Coppin to these
parts. “While playing at the Abbey-street theatre,
Dublin, he met a fascinating American actress, Mrs.
Watkin Burroughs, the wife of a provincial actor-
manager, with whom he eloped to Australia.”
(N.B. —Coppin bolted with Mrs. Burroughs, not
the actor-manager.) Old Melbourne playgoers were
aware, in a general sort of way, that the first “Mrs.
Coppin” was not legally entitled to that name, but
this statement in the “Die. of Nat. Bi.” is probably
the first appearance of the facts *i print within the
memories of most of us. The lovers landed in Syd-
ney on March 10, 1843.
Pinero was asked recently whether he had any
comparison to make between the production of “The
Amazons” at the Court (London) in 1893, and the re-
vival by Frohman at the Duke of York’s last month.
“I remember little or nothing,” he answered, “of
the original presentation. Had that production been
an event of last year only, I do not think I could
give you a reliable impression of it. Having written
a play and seen it through rehearsals, I usually for-
get all about it, and get on to my next job. To the
best of my recollection I have never been to a public
performance of any of my plays after their first-
night introduction.”
Soon you won't have to go even round the next
corner to see how Mr. Flynn looked after Mr. John-
son had had his eleventh say. In a Collins-street(Mel-
bourne) tea-room the other afternoon, the_ home
cinematograph” was on view—as handy a thing as
the gramophone—which threw lucid pictures on a
sheet the size of a table-cloth. Did you feel any
picture showman shiver?
For a time the deceased Canterbury (London- the
music-hall, not the Archbishop) specialised in
ballet dancing, and during this period the Prince ot
Wales, afterwards Edward VII., who had always a
weakness for shapely legs and the female form
divine, was a pretty regular patron. Sometimes he
brought other royalties with him, among them the
Duke of Teck and that gay old moral conflagration,
the King of the Belgians. Old Belgium was a good
hater ; he died in a sort of summer-house because he
had sold all the contents of the palace and given
away the money to his worst girls to prevent his
family getting anything, and he had either to die in
the summer-house or perish on the dining-room floor.
But he was also a good lover, and when the rest of
the royal party went home from the Canterbury, he
used to lurk behind with a bouquet, and whistle in
a minor key to girls with well-shaped extremities.
An elderly London merchant had occupied the same
stall at the Canterbury every Saturday for nearly 50
years, and his emotion on the closing night was, one
presumably sober reporter says, “pathetic to wit-
ness.” But the old boy may cheer up. He may yet
sit in a new, up-to-date, palatial Canterbury on the
old site. A wealthy syndicate is reported to have
made a handsome offer to the railway company which
now owns the show.
Melba’s gift of £lOOO towards a “Melba” Hall, at
Melbourne University Conservatoriupi, is equally
practical and handsome, and will go much further in
advancing musical education than her sundry dona-
tions to the fund of a Permanent Orchestra, which
can never be got going on the narrow party lines
laid down by its trustees. Professors may come and
go, but the University Conservatorium must go on
for ever with varying success, whereas the “Perma-
nent” Orchestra scheme appears to be pinned to the
identity of Marshall Hall. Properly managed and
popularised, the Conservatorium that Francis Ormond
endowed in the long ago should eventually become
rich enough to subsidise an orchestra of professionals,
who will work in harmony with student amateurs,
whilst appointing their own conductors and running
their concerts under union conditions. Melba’s £lOOO
carries with it an equal amount from the State Gov-
ernment, and ought to induce the local dilettanti to
turn their thin stream of musical interest into the
durable channel which leads to a permanent result.
“Aero”: Australia has just been witnessing a bio-
graph adumbration of the aerial Derby round London,
81 miles, starting from the Aerodrome, Hendon, in
the north-west, and circling right about to the same
place, first going southward, then turning east and
north. Seven started, and four got home. _ The win-
ner took about an hour and a half. Sopwith arrived
first, but was disqualified for going four miles inside
a post, otherwise a steeple. So the prize was awarded
to Hamel, who came second. He carried a passenger
(Miss Davies), who has thrice accompanied him on
his man-bird trips across the Channel. The race was
witnessed by enormous crowds. Sometimes they
helped to mark out the course to the fliers, but at
other times they obscured familiar roads. The
weather was not good. There were two rainstorms,
and such a fog blotted out the Thames that a com-
petitor, who descended to within 200 ft., could
hardly see it.
An English parson has written an historical-ecclesi-
astical play, called “Glastonbury,” which is per-
formed at matinees only by amateurs, who are sup-
posed to be professionally equipped for their parts.
Clergy characters are played by clergymen, legal
personages are represented by lawyers, and the mimic
mayor is a mayor in fact. The idea of engaging a
real alderman to impart municipal “color” to the
figure of a fictitious city father strikes one as being
purely parsonic. The reverend author would pro-
bably cast a rdhl postman for the dumb character
of a bloke who had to come along with a mail-bag
on his shoulder, and he would hire a reporter and
a donkey to represent Dogberry, because he wanted
to be written down an ass.
SHARSPEARE IN THE MAKING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Professor Wallace, who occupies the chair of Eng-
lish in Melb. University, says that Shakspeare “often
sacrificed his art most egregiously out of considera-
tion for the exigencies of the theatre. .. Many of the
peculiarities of his plays were obviously due to a
desire to fit a certain actor with a part.”
In fair Elvsian fields one day
Two souls did argument provoke;
( Dramatic specialists were they—
\ Bill Shakspeare and another bloke.
The other chap was blue of chin,
And dragged a histrionic toe.
■ Said he: “My troth, it is a sin,
How ill they treat me down below !
“It’s Shakspeare always, never Binks.
; Each act I had to oversee!
This generation little thinks
How much of Shakspeare’s only me.
: “I staged your ‘Hamlet,’ and ’twas I
Who planned and worked the ghostly cuss,
And forced you make Ophelia die,
And slaughter old Polonius.
“You know the play went flat as bricks,
Till Binks sent poor Ophelia mad,
To suit the few peculiar tricks
Of some small actor-boy he had.
“Act 5, Scene 1., Binks had to save.
The Pit’s applause was very scant
Till I threw Hamlet in the grave,
And made him rant, and rant, and rant.
, “Admit r it straight and, honest, Bill,
’Twas I touched up the final scene,
: .And engineered the record kill—
Laertes, Hamlet, King and Queen.
( “While Shakspeare’s name is high indeed
Posterity my merit blinks:
You only wrote the wretched screed—
The man who made the play was Binks.”
VICTORIA.

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will he
taken of communications insufficiently stamped. Name
and address must appear on MSS. No liability is
undertaken re contributions voluntarily submitted,
whether sent by post or handed in; and no MS. will
be returned unless stamps accompany.
.T.B. (Westport) : The oil you refer to
was probably that of the dugong, which has, in cer-
tain cases, done wonders for lung sufferers. Senior’s
Pharmacy, 250 George-street, Sydney 8.A.R.:
Thanks; but we have already had word of the
break perpetrated by the World’s News poesy ex-
pert from 1407 different sources. .. .M.M.: The only
writers barred in the best Australian books’ compe-
tition are present members of The Bulletin staff.
. ...G.F.F.: Beer-sarsaparilla joke is good. We
always said so, even in childhood’s beaming hour...
Constant Reader: The club hand wins.... Pa: Nicely-
worded effort, but it is one vast 1ibe1.... Dido: At
least three of the gags were extant when Virgil was
writing about y0u.... Bro: Decline to start an
“enormous appetite controversy.” in the nature of
tilings there can be no finality to such a venture
till some liar comes along and says he knew a man
who used to eat the earth at regular intervals....
0.8.P.L.: Article, by “Joseph Leggett, Attorney at
Law, San Francisco,” on “The Effects of Protection
in the U. 5.,” has turned up. Tell us —was that
young man ever outside his own place? he like
Henry George, who became converted to Freetrade
through never living in a Freetrade country ? George
only started to look at the horrors of Freetrade after
he had written “Progress and Poverty,” and by that
time he had gone too far to turn back. With him
Freetrade was the distant hill that looked green.
Remember, that the man who lives in the country
is likely to be the worst authority on the country
if he has never lived in any other country. Writer
once met on a steamer a young W.A. politician, who
was going abroad for the first time. He said that
W.A. was the worst-governed country in the world,
because a Premier had appointed himself Agent-
General. Said he knew W.A. was the worst-governed
country in the world, because he had lived there
and travelled all over the place. He came back from
his first travels in a hysterical condition, saying that
Westralia was about the best-governed country in the
world. .. . J.: The w.p.b. counted your poem out. .. .
Caleb: Dead cat item reverentially buried. .. .W. 11.:
Can’t remember whether that joke about the horse
and the cart and the fence and the shickered driver
was originally Scotch or Irish. Anyhow, it was
doing hard service on the Victorian gold fields when
Bendigo was young. .. .W. 8.5.: Financial article cost
us 4d. deficient postage. Better float a loan and
pay it off. Article doesn’t look like a permanent
and reproductive work.. . .Bullswool: Can’t grant the
hotel a literary license... .E. 1.: Your rhymes are
disastrous, but you are unusually candid. Three
verses:—
TO A LADY FROM A LADY.
’Tis rouge that gives you your roses
Rouge your lovely blush
The lilies are but powder
Dabbed on with a puff. (First disastrous rhyme.)
Your eyes of blue are not so true
As they appear to be
Your glorious wavy nut brown hair
Does not curl naturally.
For your round curves I’ve plainly sighed
And you figure but to have it
But now I know ’tis not your own
And you are simply padded. (Second disastrous
rhyme.)
. ...K.G.: Musical verse but a trifle thin.... Brae:
“Till mighty Tristram’s splint’ring blade his form
did rent in twain.” (Curtain.) E.B. (Q.) : Faint
echo of an American verse-writer. .. .M.W.: Sunny
harbor poem firmly rejected to accompaniment of
loud sobs by wild, wet wind J.S.: You dare to
write (from a city address) —“I often linger in the
shade and often pace the meadows.” Not this 1
weather! We imagine you armed with goloshes,
umbrella, overcoat and leggings, splashing about in
the meadows and looking for the magpie, who is in
recess and chirping huskily that the weather’s rotten.
Too early for spring carols.... Spill: Another spring
chirp ! Despite the July reports of gales, sheltering
coasters, floods, washaways and floating wreckage,
you persist in singing about sunbeams and bursting
buds. Haven’t seen a beam or a bud for weeks. Our
only trouble is that the fuel merchant has not been
able to fill our order for coal, all the colliers having
been gaoled up in the bar harbor of Newcastle by a
drunk and disorderly sea. Put another log on, and
devote the winter to planing down your stuff....
McN. (Cape York) : You are our most northern poet,
and the “up and arm” song should be yours. But
where’s your voice? Also, where’s your music?....
C.I-1.5.: One is clumsy and the other too slight....
W.Y.: We will not call it “Enough.” We will
christen it “Heaps!” and by that name condemn it.
Kismet: So you would rhyme “peace” with
“meet,” would you ? The Vizier has rung, howled
and stamped for the sworder. .. . J.N.S.: That doubt-
ful old jest is footsore. How have you managed to
escape it up to now? Are you a hermit?. .. . Wollun-
day: Seems a pity to waste all that splendid fury on
the debased wretch who drinks but never shouts....
J.E.N.: William failed to conquer us W. 0.:
(M.L.) : Passed out. .. . J.L.1.: Promising item, but
it needs some alterations and repairs. Have put it
in with the literary carpenter’s batch. . . .G.P.: Your
poem did not cheer us a bit. .. .W.J.W.: Some grace,
but a bit below print 1eve1.... Searchlight: Wail
from cowdom has a whiff of the milking-yard, but is
too wild and raw. Also, in the last verse it crashes
away into the scrub, and its intellectual footmarks !
are not discernible... .Soma: A good theme, but you j
hardly do it justice... .Z. 8.: Promising batch. Put
aside for a second reading P.A.S.: Can’t say
whether you will ever do better work ; but you will
have to do better before you are printed. You
should get your stuff typed, for you write a difficult
fist, and verse is heavily handicapped if the reader
has to stop and puzzle over words. Haven’t the space
to point out all your faults, but this sort of line
doesn’t improve a sonnet —-“Yet oft I dream tliout
hear our countless groans.” Did you ever start to
count groans? You might credit us with a couple
next time you are making up your accounts....
G.P.: The poem (it isn’t suitable, by the way) boils
down to this: A young man remarked proudly (in
song) that the world was made for him —also the
sun, moon, stars and soaring lark. Then he started
off without his umbrella to walk round and' inspect
his possessions. But the weather broke up and the
rain fell on him, so that he caught cold and 1 died.
Or perhaps he only went home again. The conclud-
ing line—“A rover he’d ceased to be”—is a sort of
poetical open verdict... .A.0.K.: Don’t know what
it means—don’t care!. ...M.: You guarantee the
lady to be a capable writer and qualified to be “one
of our editors.” You head your letter of guarantee
and assurance “18 inst., 1912.” You say you are
“regretful to hear,” and use such gems as “comun-
ate,” “noveletts,” “competant,” “poecy,” “vacency,”
“I has,” etc. You also speak of some publication
which you describe as “the family journey.” Eccen-
tricities of this sort appeared also in your first letter,
and made us doubt your assurance that your
friend was qualified to be an editor even on this
humble paper. Your statement that she had written
poems and stories with great success left us cold
and doubtful. Now we find that she writes a very
bad hand on both sides of the paper; that she doesn’t
spell well, that she punctuates not at all, and that
the “poem” is of the infant school variety. The
verdict is that you invented the lady (who writes
exactly like you), and that the joke is too elaborate
for words.

CONTRIBUTORS.
Frank McL.: To begin with, you should make your
pictures about 12 times the size, to allow for re-
duction J.E.N.: Even if it had taken 60 police-
men to remove Murphy, we would be wholly unre-
sponsive... .Waihi: If you are very young, and have
never been taught to draw, and have not practised
drawing much, you have some faint hopes of success.
But they are very faint, and, speaking as a friend,
we don’t know but that you hadn’t better make tree-
| falling, or politics, or what not your life’s work....

A Special and noteworthy feature in onr High-grade Pianos is

acture we have produced an instrument that will withstand the
most severe climatic influences.

Why Suffer from Sore Throat?
You need not do so for one hour longer than you
choose, for Science has discovered a rapid cure for
Sore Throat and its allied conditions, like Tonsillitis.
This is Wulfing’s Formamint.
“ I have never had Sore Throat myself since I began
to use Formamint, although I suffered periodically before,”
writes a physician in The Practitioner , one
of thousands of doctors who not only prescribe
Formamint to cure their patients’ Sore Throats, but
also habitually use it themselves for that purpose.
Sore Throat and those infectious diseases of
which it is a symptom, are due to germs which find
an ideal breeding-place in the throat, and, under
certain conditions, multiply with terrible rapidity,
infecting the body with their poison.
Danger of Diphtheria.
Formamint destroys these germs so
rapidly that when a physician mixed a little
Formamint with water and added it to the
germs taken from the throat of a patient
dangerously ill with Diphtheria they were
all killed within ten minutes.
Formamint should always be borne in
mind, not only as a cure, but also as a
p-eventive, when infectious diseases are
prevalent. By killing the germs causing
them, it prevents such diseases as Tonsil-
litis, Mumps, Whooping Cough, Measles,
Scarlet Fever, Consumption and Diphtheria.
For this reason, doctors always use
Formamint to safeguard themselves when
attending infectious diseases.

Formamint’s marvellous power of curing and
preventing throat diseases is vividly shown by the
following abstract of a case recently published in
The General Practitioner :
Two children with Diphtheria were taken from
their home to a hospital, where one of them died.
The mother and another child who had occupied
the same room with the patients complained of
Sore Throat. They were in obvious danger of
Diphtheria. Formamint Tablets were given to
them and the other people in the hou.se, and not
one got ill.
Evidence of this kind might be multiplied
almost indefinitely to prove the wonderful power
Formamint has of curing patients of dangerous and
infectious throat diseases and preventing other
people catching them.
A Warning to the Public.
Stimulated by the success of Wulfing’s
Formamint, many preparations claiming to
be as good are being offered to the public.
This claim is false. Wulfing’s Forma-
mint marks the discovery of a new chemical
compound, which is manufactured by a
patented process and can only be produced
by the firm of Messrs. A. Wulfing & Co.
To obtain the certainty of cure of all
forms of Sore Throat and immunity against
germ diseases, insist on having Wulfing’s
Formamint and take no substitute for it.^
It can be obtained of all Chemists, in
bottles of fifty tablets. 'Write for free sample
to-day to A. Wulfing & Co., 17 Bond Street,
Sydney, N.S.W., mentioning “The Bulletin/'
and enclosing two penny stamps towards postage.

The Broken Hill Proprietary Company Limited.
AT OTICE is hereby given that an EXTRAORDINARY GENERAL MEETING of
Broken Hill Proprietary Company, Limited, will be held at the registered office of
the Companv, Equitable Building, 320 Collins Street, Melbourne, on FRIDAY the
27th day of SEPTEMBER, 1912, at 3 o’clock in the afternoon, for the purpose of con-
sidering, and, if thought fit, passing the subjoined resolutions as special resolutions.
And notice is hereby also given that a further Extraordinary General Meeting of
the Company will be held at the said registered office of the Company on MONDAY, the
14-th day of OCTOBER,. 1912, at 3 o’clock in the afternoon, when a report will be pre-
sented of the proceedings at the first above-mentioned meeting, to be held on the 27th
day of September, 1912, and the subjoined resolutions will, if passed by the requisite
majority at such first above-mentioned meeting, be submitted at such further meeting
for confirmation as special resolutions.
RESOLUTIONS ABOVE REFERRED TO.
1 That the provisions of the Company’s memorandum of association with respect to
’ the Company’s objects be altered by adding after paragraph “F” in clause 3 of
such memorandum a further paragraph in the words following, that is to say :
“F.F.” “The carrying on in all or any of their respective branches all or any
of the trades or businesses of iron or steel masters, manufacturers, smelters,
or winners or of producers of lead, zinc or other metals, and the manufacturing
or production in connection therewith of any merchantable articles, commodities or
products.” , . ~ .
2. That the capital of the Company be increased by the creation _ of 540,UUU new
shares of Bs. each. Such new shares shall not be issued otherwise than as fully
paid up to Bs. Such shares shall in all respects rank equally with the existing
960,000 fully-paid shares of Bs. each. v ,
3. That, notwithstanding anything contained in article No. 34 or any other ot the
Company’s articles, the directors be and hereby are authorised to allot and issue as
fully paid-up such number of the said new shares to such holders of debentures
hereafter to be issued by the directors, and in exchange for their deben-
tures at such price (not being under par), and generally upon and in accord-
ance with such terms and conditions as may be fixed by the directors on the issue
of the said debentures, or may seem fit to the directors, and that any ot the said
new shares not so allotted or issued may be disposed of by the directors in such
manner and at such times and on such terms as the directors may think most
beneficial to the interests of the Company. ......
Should the above-mentioned resolutions not be passed by the requisite majority at the
meeting to be held on the 27th day of September, 1912, due notice thereof will be
given to the shareholders that the meeting on the 14th day of October, 1912, ot which
notice is now given, will not be held. By <-
Dated this 17th day of July, 1912. F. M. DICKENSON, Secretary.
Dear Sir or Madam, — (CIRCULAR TO SHAREHOLDERS.)
As you are aware, the Company holds from the Government of South Australia cer
tain mineral leases over a large and rich iron ore deposit known as the Iron Knob, ana
comprising about 340 acres. Up to the present the ore has only been used as a flux—-
a necessary adjunct to the Company’s operations at Port Pirie in connection with lead
smelting.
Your directors are now of opinion that an opportune time has arrived tor an exten-
sion of the Company’s business, and the utilisation of these deposits to the best advan-
tage in other directions, by the manufacture of iron and steel, etc., and the general man-
ager (Mr. Delprat) was, therefore, sent to Europe and America in order to inquire
fully into the whole proposition. As a result, the services of an expert of high standing
(Mr. David Baker, of Philadelphia, U.S.A.) were secured, and that gentleman has visited
Australia, and gone fully into the whole question. His report, confirmed by the general
manager, is most favorable as to the results obtainable by the Company embarking upon
the industry. . ' . . , ,
Consequently the directors desire to obtain the necessary consent ot shareholders
to the proposed alterations in the memorandum and articles of association, as set out
in the enclosed notice of meetings. . . ...
This will enable the directors to make financial arrangements for obtaining addi-
tional capital on terms most beneficial for shareholders, -a_nd, in connection with any
scheme decided upon shareholders will be given priority in Participation.
By order of the Board. F. M. DICKENSON, Secretary.
320 Collins Street, Melbourne, July 17, 1912. ‘

ONE HUNDRED AND TWENTY-EIGHTH HALF-YEARLY REPORT of
The Commercial Banking Company of Sydney, Limited.
Presented to the Shareholders at an Ordinary General Meeting, held at the Head Banking House, George
Street, Sydney, on Friday, July 19, 1912.
The Directors submit to the Shareholders a Balance-sheet, showing the Liabilities and Assets of the
Bank on June 30, 1912, and present the following Report;—
The balance at the credit of Profit and Loss Account, after deducting rebate on current bills and accrued
interest on fixed deposits, reducing premises account, paying Note and other taxes, and providing for bad
and doubtful debts, as well as granting to the Officers a special bonus on their salaries, amounts to:
£169,539 10 5
out of which the Directors recommend the following appropriations:—
To Payment of a Dividend for the Half-year at the rate of 10 per
cent, per annum .. .. \. .. .. .. ..£75,000 0 0
„ Transfer to Reserve Fund . . . . . . . . . . . . 40,000 0 0
„ Balance being undivided Profit .. .. .. .. .. .. 54,539 10 5
£169,539 10 5
At an Extraordinary General Meeting, held on the 23rd February, and confirmed at another Extra-
ordinary General Meeting on the 15th March last, it was decided to increase the capital of the Bank by
the creation of 20,000 new shares, to be allotted to the Shareholders at par.
This meeting is called upon to elect a Director, consequent on the retirement, in terms of the Articles
of Association, of Norman Shelley, Esq., who is eligible for re-election, and has given the requisite notice
that he is a candidate for the office. It also rests with the meeting to appoint Auditors for the ensuing
year.
During the Half-year branches have been established at Oberon and Stockinbingal in this State, while
the Branches at Federal and Nymagee have been closed.
The Dividend is now payable.
GEORGE J. COHEN, Chairman.

Officers of
Sydney, July 12, 1912.
We hereby certify that we have examined the bills, compared the balances and counted the coin in the
Head Office of The Commercial Banking Company of Sydney, Limited, and have compared the returns of
the Branches, and have found the same as specified in the foregoing Balance-sheet.
F. W. HIXSON, l . ...
W. L. DOCKER, i Auditors.
The following resolutions were carried unanimously:—-
“That the Report and Statements of Account be received and adopted, and ordered to be circulated
among the Proprietors.”
“That Norman Shelley, Esq., be re-elected a Director of the Bank.”
“That W. L. Docker, Esq., and E. S. Cape, Esq., be appointed Auditors for the ensuing vear.’
“That the thanks of the Shareholders be presented to the Directors, General Manager, and Ofl
the Bank for their very satisfactory management of the affairs of the institution.”
. . T. A. DIBBS, General Manager.
In moving the adoption of the Report, the Chairman, Mr. G. J. Cohen, said: —
In accordance with our usual custom, I will briefly review the figures of the Balance-sheet and com-
pare them with those of the corresponding period of last year.
It is satisfactory to see that out of the quarter of a 'million payable in four instalments, spread over
12 months, in respect of the increase of Capital sanctioned in March last, there has been received the sum
of £218,393, many of our Shareholders having availed themselves of the option to pay up their instal-
ments in full on 29th ultimo.
The Reserve Fund has been increased from £.1,420,000 to £1,500,000, and provision has been
made to set aside a sum of £40,000 Out of this Half-year’s profits to further augment it, making with
the undivided balance, a total of £1,594,000.,
Notes of this Bank, to the amount of £26B,ooo,have been withdrawn from Circulation during the
year, and in this connection I may add that our Note Circulation, which stood at £675,000 18 months
ago, now stands at £48,000, showing that we have already *<aid £627,000 of our Notes.’
Bills in Circulation are less by £130,000. Deposits’ and Other Liabilities show an increase of
£733,000, and there is an improvement of £14,000 in our Profits.
The result of this is that the total figures have expanded about £646,000, compared with those of
12 months ago.
On the Assets side, the items —Coin, Cash at Bankers, and Commonwealth (legal Tender) Note? show
a diminution of £1,280,000, and Bills Receivable in London, and Notes and Bills of oth r Banks
respectively, £70,000 and £82,000, while Money at Short Call in London has been increased by £5B 000
and a further sum of £llO,OOO has been invested in Government Stocks.
Our Liquid Assets you will notice amount to £10,268)000, about 47 per cent, of the Bank’s Liabili-
ties to the public.
Bills Discounted and All Debts due to the Bank show the large increase of £1,892 000 and in this
respect I may say that during the period under review, and especially during the last’six’ months the
demand for money has been considerable.
Bank Premises Account show an increase of £25,000. We have expended a large sum in the pur-
chase of several sites in the City and Country, and are’ building thereon. 1
In common with other Banks, we have had to raise our rates for Fixed Deposits owing to the
State Government paying 4 per cent, at the Treasury for borrowed money. In consequence of this action
of the State Government, and of the contraction of our lending power brought about, as indicated in mv
remarks at a former Meeting by the withdrawal bv the Commonwealth Government of our right to issue
notes, which necessitated the redemption of our Notes in Circulation, and payment in Sovereigns to the
Federal Treasury for Commonwealth Notes for till money, we have with much reluctance been compelled
to increase the rates charged to our borrowing Customers. I say with reluctance, because our policy has
always been to make the charges to producers as light as possible for the welfare of the country at large
I would like to call your attention to the fact that we have, as you will see by reading the second
Paragraph in the Report, granted a special Bonus to the Staff, which is additional to the usual amount
„ On, the 7 ,th inst. our General Manager (Mr. T. A. Dibbs) completed his sixty-fifth year of service in
the Bank, and for 4o years he has held his present position.

I Householders in Melbourne are horribly
j suspicious of their Gas Co.’s burst of gener-
osity in granting a concession of fivepence
on the old five-shilling rate. They are look-
ing the gift horse in the mouth, and wonder-
ing whether the animal is about to cut a
new tooth. For no apparent reason —not an
apparently innocent one, anyway—the co. is
removing everybody’s old gas-meters and fix-
ing new machines in their stead. A rumor
circulates in South Yarra to the effect that a
thrifty spinster sat up for hours watching
the new meter “clock” by candle light, and
saw the hand stealthily moving after the gas
had been turned off. But all rumors of this
sort are treated with disdain by the gasman
who calls for the account. “I’ve never met a
meter that worked miracles in favor of the
company,” says the democratic rate collector ;
“but I’ve known a lot that didn’t work at
all. The trouble is to keep them from going
to sleep in business hours.”
the last night of Oscar Wilde’s “A
Woman of No Importance,” at Melb. Prin-
cess’s, a wild young man, who was sitting
in the dress circle with a girl, began to ex-
postulate in a whisper about the play. “It’s
immoral, that’s what it is!” he chattered.
Indignant hisses from those about them
served to fan his indignation into flame, and
he commenced a sort of impromptu sermon.
Ushers gathered swiftly, and a fierce discus-
sion was ended by the objector and his
blushing companion stumbling towards the
door. “She’ll not hear such talk!” was the
last defiant remark made to a giggling
house. It would be interesting to know how
the indignant saint came to attend a play
with such a signboard title.
A stylish flat refuge of Melb. squattocracy,
which has been abandoning its Maryannless
Toorak palaces, was shaken to its front mat
during Grand National week by a sort of
civil war. One of the wealthiest inhabitants
had made ready for a fearfully select dance,
for which she held a full hand of acceptances
from the Most Expensive Folk. Quite inad-
vertently the invitation of a young grass
widow flatting on the premises had been
overlooked —or perhaps the usual postal
muddle was responsible for its non-arrival.
However, the lady took offence and began a
crusade against dances in flats everywhere,
and the dance overhead in particular. The
two parties to the disagreement started a
serial argument by post and telephone, and
at last it reached the stage when the dance-
giver cried sarcastically: “Shall I insert a
paragraph in the morning papers—‘Regret
is expressed that Mrs. Upstairs’ dance is off.
It annoys Mrs. Downstairs and cannot take
place’?” The objector (call her Mrs.
Turkey) then threatened to barricade her
part of the Dardanelles staircase, or sow it
with rats and spiders. Mrs. Dancegiver
(hereinafter known as Mrs. Italy) retorted
oyer the wire that her guests would willingly
climb the backstairs to reach any function
she cared to give. But the war continued,
and finally Mrs. Italy agreed to pay an in-
demnity—that is to say, she agreed to put
Mrs. Turkey up at one of the best hotels for
the night. Mrs. Turkey accepted, and
motored off to the selected tavern, where she
dined a cosy party at Mrs. Italy’s expense.
And now Mrs. Turkey is proudly exhibiting
the Tripolitan bill paid by Mrs. Italy, and
generally regarding it as a trophy of war.
Mrs. Italy, however, points out that she held
her dance. So the war was won by both
sides, nobody was defeated, and a tall pub
has made a little extra profit.
N. S. Wales is not the only place with a
Speakership problem. According to the
Vienna correspondent of London Daily
Chronicle :—-
The Hungarian crisis is assuming dangerous pro-
portions. The exasperation of the Opposition at
yesterday’s “coup de main'’ is growing, and acis of
violence are expected.
At this morning’s sitting of Parliament the scan-
dalous uproar was renewed • with still greater force
than was the case yesterday. Many of the Opposi-
tion brought with them whistles, trumpets and other
kinds of instruments, and when Count Tisza, the
Speaker, and the Premier entered the Chamber they
were received with such insulting cries as “Scoun-
drels!” “Thieves!” “Hangmen!” “Traitors!”
Then commenced a deafening concert of whistles,
trumpets and bells. Count Tisza sat coolly in the
chair, calmly regarding the proceedings and making
a list of the deputies who were to be expelled,
'there were 38 of tnese selected interrupters. The wild
noises continued more than an hour, and insults
were flung even to the gallery, where Countess Tisza,
the Speaker’s wife, was seated.
On a signal from the Speaker a police detachment,
with three officers, entered the Chamber, saluted the
Opposition members, and surrounded their benches.
M. Julius V. Justh, once Speaker of the House and
now Leader of the Opposition (Kossuth is ill, and
left for Nauheim yesterday), was the first to feel an
officer’s hand on his shoulder, and with him 37 others
on the Speaker’s list were turned out. Each of
them flung insulting epithets at the Chair as he was
leaving.
A minute later all the other members of the Oppo-
sition left voluntarily under protest, accompanying
their exodus with the discordant noise of instru-
ments.
During the afternoon’s sitting the excluded
members attempted to resume their Parlia-
mentary duties: —
Blows were exchanged between the Members and
the police, and the officers were worsted. The door
was smashed, and the party forced their way into
the Chamber, and took their seats.
The lawless law-makers were ultimately
flung out again, whereupon Speaker Willis,
that is to say, Count Tisza
Stated that as a number of Deputies had persisted
in causing noisy and childish scenes, he had not orily
ordered their removal, but had also directed that
they should be excluded until the sense of the House
with regard to such improper conduct had been
taken.
It was subsequently announced that M. Julius
Justh is excluded from a further 30 sittings, and M.
Geza Polonyi, M. Martin Lorassy, and M. Sigmund
Eitner from a further 15 sittings. M. Justh is ex-
cluded from 45 sittings in all, and three others from
25 sittings, while 29 other Members of the Justh-
Kossuth People’s party are excluded from 15 sit-
tings. Four Deputies are ordered to make an apology
in the House.
At latest advices :
Budapest is practically in a state of siege, and the
public offices and buildings, as well as the houses of
prominent Deputies belonging to the majority, are
watched day and night by policemen and gendarmes.
The Parliament House itself is surrounded by cavalry,
infantry, gendarmerie and mounted police. Forty-
seven companies of infantry, eight squadrons of
Hussars, 1680 policemen, and 900 gendarmes are
engaged in preventing a surprise on the part of the
allied Opposition. The latter have resolved not to
allow any quiet discussion in the Chamber so long
as Count Tisza, whose election as Speaker they will
not acknowledge, remains in the Chair, and the
Premier, Ladislaus de Lukacs, is in office.
