IN BANANA LAND
Bananaland’s Criminal Investigation De-
partment brags proudly that last year “it
received stolen goods of a value greater than
the salaries of the whole of the Department.”
Sounds queer to hear the C.I.D. bragging
that it is the greatest receiver of stolen goods
in the country. In any case, is the above-
quoted boast a compliment to Queensland, or
otherwise ?
Eighteen months ago Jasper Harvey, the
wild and whirling Anti-Sosher who opposed
Prime Minister Fisher for Wide Bay in
1906, got the billet of Superintendent of the
Anglican Mission Station on Moa (or Maui)
Island (Torres Strait), and it was sup-
posed that politics would know him no more.
But Jasper is again to the fore, and has just
announced that he will stand for Oxley
when Edwards retires next year.
A couple of years ago the Queensland De-
partment of Public Instruction did away
with corporal punishment in the case of
girls over 12 years of age. Now the Teachers’
Conference, inspired by its lady members,
asks Minister Grant to restore the power.
Grant (a bachelor) refuses. The Banana-
land school marms clamor that the abolition
is ridiculous, “for a bad girl is much worse
than a bad boy.” No chivalric “sex” blither
pulls the wool over the eyes of these practi-
cal pedagoguesses.
Cloncurry (N.Q.) has just got a Chinese
cemetery gazetted. Of course, a Chow grave-
yard is only a cemetery pro tem., for a day
arrives when the bones are exhumed and
shipped, duly insured, to China. Some years
ago the s.s. Ventnor took aboard 500 bags of .
Chinese bones, which were insured in Maori-
land for £4500. The Ventnor was lost, and
Maoriland Chowdom was consoled at the
rate of £9 a bag. It is generally believed
that the Celestials bore their loss with truly
Buddhist fortitude.
“Papa” Pinnock, lately deceased, was for
many years the best known of all Queens-
land’s stipendiary magistrates. Pinnock was
quite a piece of ancient history, for he
arrived in Australia in ’43, and passed nearly
the whole of his time in Bananaland. He
did some pioneer squatting on the Downs in
the early days, but abandoned tlie shifty and
treacherous jumbuck to act as P.M. at Bowen
in 1864. Subsequently he served in the same
sort of capacity in Warwick, Dalby and
Brisbane. Was also sheriff of Queensland,
and marshal of the Supreme Court. Pin-
nock, who died in his 86th year, was a good
example of the degeneracy that overtakes a
white race reared in the tropics. In his 75th
year he was a fine, upstanding, stately old
fellow, white-haired and dignified, in spite
of the fact that he was born in the torrid
island of Jamaica, and hardly struck a cool
climate in his life.
Somebody moved in the Queensland Assem-
bly a few days ago for a Special Committee 1
to inquire into the claims of one Down
to a public' grant on the grounds that he was
the original discoverer of Peak Downs gold-
field. But what has happened John Memon-
itch? In 1902 Premier Philp received the
following quaint document —
THIS IS MY BILL.
Oct. 6th, 1912.
To the Honorable Robt. Philp, Premier.
Old Pioneer and explorer of Queensland, first dis-
covered payable gold in the Colony of Queensland,
namely, Peak Downs, Clermont District, from the
years 1861, 1862, 1864 until 1893, to explore and
prospect the field 1 spent £36,000.
£36,000 standing from 1861, at 4s.
per cent. Amount . . . . £19,954 0 0,
Interest .. .. .. .. 8,594 3 5
Total amount r* . . £208,448 3 5
This is the Amount ;df My Claim which is due to
Me by the Government, if the Govt, is not satisfied
to give me my money P will take my land 300 square
mi.es which I explored > and prospected. Since the
colony has been opened by me I claim a premium
on all kinds of Minerals raised in Queensland.
John Memonitch.
Memonitch is still alert and alive on the
matter of his grievance, and the Cabinet that
recognises any other discoverer will have to
reckon with the old warrior.
DOCTOR: “I hope your husband's temperature is a bit lower this morning, Mrs. Johnson?"
MRS. J. (weeping): “ It's gone up, doctor. He died an hour ago.”
Barcaldine lost its oldest resident the
other day, when James French, an ancient
carrier, passed onward where floods and
flies are not, so far as we are aware, though
they may be, there being no dead sure evi-
dence to the contrary. French’s itinerary
took him from Westwood to the Barcoo.
Westwood was, for a long time, the terminus
of the' only line in Central Bananaland ; a
30-mile streak from Rockhampton, opened in
’67, and not continued west at all till ’73.
A carrier who did the 400-mile journey by
the Dawson, Comet and such flood giants in
those days, must have carried a good deal
of unwritten history under his ancient and
dilapidated hat. By the way, it was the
Westwood line that the novelist, Anthony
Trollope, was much -amused with on his
visit in ’72. It “started nowhere, and ended
in the bush,” in which respect it was very
like some of Anthony’s own novels, notably
“The Kellys and the O’Kellys.” West-
wood, which grew tired of being bush,
cleared itself, and grew prickly pear instead
of bush, and then fell asleep, and disremem-
bered to wake again, in consequence whereof
it still slumbers. - .
Dear Bulletin, —After all, latitude is
only a secondary consideration in the adjust-
ment of climatic conditions. Take, for in-
stance, the case of Herberton, in North
Queensland, and the surrounding region of
the Evelyn tablelands. Here, well within
the tropics, you get a temperature compar-
ing favorably with that of north-eastern
Tasmania. It is altitude, not latitude, that
counts for coolness in the tropics. Much of
the plateau country of the Far North of
Queensland averages between 3000 ft. and
4000 ft. above sea level. Hence the delightful
climate on those tablelands. At Ruwenzori,
Kenia and Kilimanjaro, in Africa, there is
perennial snow right under the Equator.
Same thing happens in Ecuador, South
America. All a matter of altitude, my
brothers. In Papua, where you can stand
on an eminence and almost shout in the
Equator’s ear, there are alleged to be snow-
capped mountains in the interior, though no
really authentic person has ever reached
them and come back sober. Enough lias
been proved concerning the internal arrange-
ments of Papua, though, to warrant the
supposition that it could support a big white
civilised population in the cool high regions
of its interior. Peaks of the Owen Stanley
Range run up to 16,000 ft. within sight of
the sea; and residence at 16,000 ft. up will
do anyone who isn’t a perfect hog for alti-
tude and a whale for snow and fur clothing.
Same thing applies to the Northern Terri-
tory ; as remarked in The Bulletin 1 .fl-
ing article (2f/6/’l2). Almost the whole of
the tablelands country of the Territory’s in-
terior averages from 1200 ft. to 1500 ft. above
sea level, with odd peaks in the MacDon-
nells and elsewhere touching the 4000 ft.
mark. Any elevation ranging round the
2000 ft. level may be considered suitable for
white habitation, even right under the Equa-
tor ; and none of the Territory is within
coo-ee of the Line. The bulk of it lies be-
tween 16 and 26 degrees south latitude, and
the whole of that area is tableland country
except, perhaps, a little portion in the south-
eastern corner. When the south-east mon-
soons are blowing across the plateaux of the
Central Territory, as they do for about five
months of the year, from May to September,
it is a place where warm clothing is in much
demand, where dense blankets are required
at night, and where a nose-wipe is handy in
the early dawn, On those tablelands writer
has seen ice on the water-buckets every
morning for a month continuously; and
this in July and only 17 degrees south of the
Line. The greatest perishing writer ever got
from cold was on the tablelands of the Terri-
tory in the winter of 1904. We were over-
landing cattle at the time, and so acute was
the chill at night that we cut up our saddle-
cloths and the fly of a tent to make gar-
ments for protection against it. One mem-
ber of our party cut up a splendid Turkish
towel he had and made a waistcoat of it.
He preferred going without a wash to being
cold. The Bulletin can have his name
and address if it Wants them.—Yours, etc.,
Mallarapoonie.
The Navvy.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Discussing- the emoluments of the country clergy,
the Rev. said he knew of one case in which a
minister had turned navvy .—Brisbane daily.
Guess the parson showed his savvy
When he went and turned a navvy,
For it’s better belting roadways
Out of sods
Than be grinding out 1 a sermon
On the height of ancient Hermon,
As a means of getting unction
Into clods. |
If the parson, pinched and pallid,
Could but sing a plaintive ballad,
And recount in truthful accents
All his woes,
At the telling of his pickle
Every decent eye would trickle,
Till the teardrops ran in dozens
Down the nose.
But he’ll keep in lovely fettle,
If he pounds the stubborn metal
As he used to smite Apollyon
On the crown ;
And the matt who belts him madly,
He will clinch with grim and gladly,
Or with brawny fist and hairy
Knock him down.
Any man would ten times rather
Work his body to a lather,
As he swings his pick before him
Through the shale,
Than be harping on the phases
Of Beelezbub and blazes,
Or be dumping hapless Jonah
Down the whale.
And as soon as parson gauges
He can earn a navvy’s wages
By a grit and go and courage
All his own,
He will rather strain his muscle
With a friendly pick, than tussle
With the souls that part up threepence
With a groan.
QUEENSLAND.
PHILANDER F.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, July 27, 1912.
My Dear Thargomindah.-t-
The deep, rich baritone of the bull and
the tenor bleat of the sheep have been loud
in the land this week. Governor Macgregor
attended their recital at Nambour, on
Wednesday, and hurried away next day to
Gatton. At both places he threw open the
prize pumpkin and unlocked the oratorical
bottle of jam. On Friday, at the Boys’
Grammar School, he opened a new labora-
tory, wherein our embryo chemists and
medicoes may smellfully experiment. To-
day (Saturday), he entertained about 50
kiddies from the Deaf and Dumb School at
the panto., and had them fed copiously on
chocolate at half-time. As seen from my
observation perch on the roof, the vice-regal
pageant this week has been mostly cows and
kids.
Mrs. Marienthal, of Sydney, who is shel-
tering here from the New South Gales
weather, gave a theatre party last Tuesday.
The pretty Earwaker girls and Dr. Connolly
were included in the invited bunch.
Yda Buchanan, sister of our ex-Mayor,
has fled to Maryborough from this vortex of
gaiety, and is fairly in the social whirl of
those parts. (The velocity of the whirl
there has been estimated as two miles per
annum.) Mrs. Corser, whose husband, the
M.L.A., wears Dundreary whiskers of the
1864 vintage, is gallantly looking after the
fair guest.
Another bridge and euchre party convulsed
this city on Monday evening. The Clayfield
oroquet Club determined to dazzle the visit-
ing southerners, and fixed upon the cards.
“Let us throw off our wonted air of sedate
solidity!” they cried. “Let us forget that
we are croqueteers of Clayfield, and the
hard-baked foundations of society, and hit
things up for once. Here’s for one night in
Monte Carlo! Who’s got change of a shil-
ling?” (At least, I guess it must have been
something like that.)
Mrs. Bulcock incited some of the revellers
to small talk with tea at Eschenhagen’s, on
Monday. There was about a hogshead of
tea left over, as most of the visitors and
many of the players were engaged finishing
the tournament. The mallets are being
wearily wielded to-day at Ascot.
Samuel Griffith, C.J., and his dame booked
through to your part on Friday. They led
the strenuous social life whilst here. Some
nights they were out as late as 10 o’clock.
This place grows more like sleepless Paris
every day.
Mrs. Dick Casey, of Melbourne, who be-
longs to . the handsome Harris family,
returned to Melbourne by the Orient boat
last Wednesday. She farewelled all her
friends with tea prior to going aboard the
tall ship, and; rolling south in the broken
white teeth of the weather.
Ellis Rowan, the clever, fragile, little
flower painter, is at present in Brisbane,
where she is engaged. trying -to interest an
apathetic Government in her Australian col-
lection. It is about as encouraging as try-
ing to interest a sleepy elephant in a sleight-
of-hand trick, but the lady persists bravely.
Treasurer Barnes opened the Wellington
Point Show to-day. (This is the cow’s week
at home.) Pretty Mrs. Caine, wife of the
new member for Bowen, smiled on the cere-
mony, and Mrs. Stodart and Mrs. Williams,
with their respective husbands in leash, were
attentive to the opening remarks. At 1
o’clock the party attempted to lunch, but
was foiled in some manner, and returned to
town in an unfurnished condition.
The whole village has been footing it dur-
ing the past week. On Monday evening, the
Brisbane Rowing Club capered in the South
Brisbane Technical College. Mrs. David
Hamilton and Miss Doris Nicholson helped
secretary Finucan to weather it out. Miss
Sybil Gordon, from Sydney, was one of the
dancers.
A few of the moneyed bachelors conspired
together and entertained the village peerage
at a dance in the same hall, on Wednesday
evening. Palms, bachelors and other green
stuff were spread round regardless of ex-
pense, and a string band sawed and twanged
the gay entrancing strains that are so re-
mindful of tired machinery when heard from
without.
St. Patrick’s dance on the same night
was positively crowded. One Rubick, who
has already collected a fair number of
prizes for his cowboy costume, was again
successful, and repeated the offence at the
Scandinavian Masked Ball the following
evening. Someone will have to kidnap and
burn those extraordinary garments if the
village is to be saved from exploding with
suppressed jealousy.
On Friday, the Brisbane Ladies’ Rowing
Club gave a hop or bound at Kangaroo
Point. Partners were plentiful, and supper
was abundant, so all was well.
One of Brisbane’s popular young matrons
endeavored to farewell a scion of Adelaide
nobility this week with cakes in which a
distracted cook had substituted salt for
sugar. The function looked like being
ruined, when someone dashed out and hurried
back with some bread and butter. The chef
(he is suspected of Socialism and a hatred
of the upper classes) has been degraded in
rank and exiled to an English duke’s kit-
chen. As he is passionately fond of excite-
ment and fashion, he will probably pine
away or take to drink.
Three women just went past with their
best hats on. Must get up on the roof and
watch where they are heading. If it’s a
function, I’ll tell you next week.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.


THE HOWARD WATCH
The Greatest Practical Achievement
in Watch Accuracy and Refinement.
The Howard is a WONDERFUL Watch !
In its sixty years’-history are recorded what are perhaps the greatest
triumphs in accuracy and durability ever accredited to any time-
piece.
When Edward Howard —as long ago as 1840—set out to make the
world’s best watch, he determined that every timepiece bearing his
name should be good enough, in every feature, to present to the
President of the United States.
He thereby set a very high standard; and from that day to this the
Howard Company have faithfully maintained it.
The Howard Watch never varies. Whatever the climate, whatever
the usage, whatever the position in which it may be carried, it is
invariably accurate.
Peary carried one to the Pole; Wilbur Wright found it the only
watch suited to aviation; and Eear-Admiral Sigsbee (Com-
mander of U.S.S. Maine, at the time of the famous catastrophe in
Havana Harbor), whose Howard Watch was submerged live
days in sea-water, to-day finds it as accurate as ever.
The. Howard Watch has specially hard-temf A ice, which will
not displace with jolting or vibration. II » wjp. d adjusted to
position, temperature, and isochronism *-• * v.<- ns that it will
run true on its face, on its back, in differ*...,.,. % . .es, and under
intense heat or cold.
Treat yourself to a GOOD watch! The Howard will last much
longer than a lifetime; and is, consequently, the cheapest in the
long run.

turbine plants of several 1000II.P. Their superiority in economy is attained by
their faultless construction and in obviating the many losses, including radiation,
etc., which are unavoidable in steam plants having separate boiler and engine.

ance evpn by unskilled hands. —-Absolute Reliability and Greater Durability.

£2O REWARD for Information Concerning the
Whereabouts of CORNELIUS (CON.) BOURKE.
THE TRUSTEES EXECUTORS AND AGENCY
1 COMPANY, LIMITED, of No. 412 Collins
Street, Melbourne, Victoria (the Trustee of a Settle-
ment of C. Beatrke), is desirous of ascertaining the
whereabouts of one Cornelius Bourke (commonly
known as Con Bourke), formerly licensee of the
Prince of Wales Hotel, Hamilton, Victoria, and who
is supposed to have left Victoria about 22 years ago
arid gone to New South Wales or South Australia.
He sometimes went under the name of McDonald.
The above reward will be paid to the first person
supplying the said Company with satisfactory in-
formation concerning the present whereabouts of the
said Cornelius Bourke if living, or if dead, the date
of his death, name of the place where he died,
whether he was married, and if married, whether he
left a widow and children. . Information must be
supplied within two months from the date hereof.
Dated the 18th day of June, 1912.
ERNEST W. POWLING, Princes Street, Port Fairy,
Solicitor for the said Company.

HOW I ENLARGED MY BUST
SIX INCHES IN THIRTY DAYS
AFTER I HAD TRIED PILLS, MASSAGE, WOODEN CUPS AND VARIOUS
ADVERTISED PREPARATIONS WITHOUT THE SLIGHTEST RESULTS
A Simple, Easy Method which any Lady can use at
home, and quickly obtain a Large and Beautiful Bust.
BY margarette merlain.
Well do I know the horrors and intense
humiliation of being flat-chested ; of having
the face of a woman set on the form of a
man; and I cannot find words to tell you
how good I felt and what a terrible load
was lifted off my mind when I first saw my
bust had really grown six inches in size. I
felt like a new being, for with no bust I
realised I was really neither a man nor a
woman, but just a sort of Creature half-way
between.
’ SKSKraj:
mmm m m mrnmmmm
tSSSSSSSS
mm
m
m
vWJf.
mb
**
Mi
M ffiii £u
Keep this picture and see your own bust undergoing the same marvellous transformation.
With what pity must every man look at
ivery woman who presents to him a flat
chest —a chest like his own. Can such a
woman inspire in a man those feelings and
emotions which can only be inspired by a
real and true woman, a woman with a beau-
tiful, well-rounded bust? Most certainly not.
The very men who shunned me, and even
the very women who passed me carelessly
by when I was so horribly flat-chested and
had no bust, became my most ardent ad-
mirers shortly after I obtained such a won-
derful enlargement of my bust. I therefore
determined that all women who were flat-
chested should profit by my accidental dis-
covery and have a bust like my own. I had
been imposed upon by charlatans and frauds,
who sold me all sorts of pills and appli-
ances for enlarging the bust, but which did
me no good whatever. I therefore deter-
mined my unfortunate sisters should no
longer be robbed by those “fakirs” and
frauds, and I wish to warn all women
against them.
: The discovery of the simple process with
which I enlarged my bust six inches inisS|o
days was due solely to a lucky accident,
which I believe was brought about by Divine
Providence; and as Providence was so good
as to give me the means to obtain a beauti-
ful bust, I feel I should give my secret to all
my sisters who need it. Merely enclose two
penny stamps for postage, and I will send
you particulars by return.
I will positively guarantee that every lady
can obtain a wonderful enlargement in her
bust in 30 days’ time, and that she can
easily use this process in the privacy of her
own house without the knowledge of anyone.
—Address, Margarette Merlain (Dept.
1024 C.), Pembroke House, Oxford Street,
London, W., Eng.
FREE COUPON for Readers of Sydney “ Bulletin,”
entitling; the sender to full information regarding this marvellous discovery for enlarging and
beautifying the bust. Out out this coupon (or write and mention No. 1024 C), and send
to-day with your name and address, enclosing two penny stamps for reply, to Margarette
Merlain (Dept. 1024 C), Pembroke House, Oxford Street, London, W., Eng.

NOTE. —All ladies who wish to obtain a large and beautiful bust should write Madame Merlain at
once, as the above is an honest, straightforward offer on her part, made for the good of her sisters, and
she in no way profits by the transaction, but generously offers her help absolutely free to all who use tie
free coupon above. Ladies who fear that their busts wan become too large arc cautioned to stop the
treatment as soon as they have obtained all the development desired.

From “Trotter” :
Vastly important to Australia is an agreement
just concluded between Russia, Britain and France
for the construction of the trans-Persian railway.
Germany is not in this particular job. The whole line
from Constantinople via Bagdad (of “Kismet fame),
to Karachi, in India, will shorten the trip from Lon-
don to Bombay by five days. Add the two days that
will be saved by the Westralian railway, and you
have an entire week saved between Sydney and Lon-
don. The only sea worth mentioning will be Fre-
mantle to Bombav. From Constantinople there will
be a rattle to Ostend. Yet the British Government
has persistently, up till now, opposed every Central
Asian railway scheme. Persia has not one mile of
railway. Consider how Britain tries to keep large
. portions of the Globe in comparative barbarism.
There is fierce hostility in a majority of the British
newspapers, and, of course, by a great section of
politicians, to the trans-Persian railway. Thev say
it will bring Russia to the gates of India! Conse-
rmentlv; there should be no'civilisation.
The person who cannot swim is seemingly
as prevalent in educated Germany as in any
other old country. Hot weather lately in-
duced a lot of Germans to try a bath in the
Rhine, the cable said, and 22 of them were
ready for the undertaker when they came
out of the historic river.
The first sitting of N.S.W. Court of Criminal
Appeal, instituted by Holman last session,
was held in Sydney (19/7/T2), Justice Pring
presiding. Eight incorrigible optimists asked
for leave to appeal to the Full Court against
their convictions. Six of them were turned
down with a dull, cold bump; one was
granted leave on the ground that his sen-
tence was too heavy; and another was
allowed a second run for his money against
that part of his sentence which declared that
he was to be treated as an habitual criminal.

A Remarkable and Convincing State'
ment of the Success of Cuticura
Soap and Ointment in theTreat-
mentofthe Pain, Itching and
Burning of Eczema
“I, the undersigned, cannot give enough
praise to the Cuticura Remedies. I had been
doctoring for at least a year for eczema on
my foot. I had tried doctor after doctor all
to no avail. When a young girl I sprained
my ankle three different times, paying little
or no attention to it, when five years ago
a small spot showed upon my left ankle.
1 was worried and sent for a doctor. He
said it was eczema. He drew a small bone
from the ankle about the size of a match
and about an inch long. The small hole
grew to about, the size of an apple, and
the eczema spread to the knee. The doctors
never could heal the hole in the ankle,
•■he whole foot ran water all the time.
“My husband and my sons were up night
and day wheeling me from one room to an-
other in the hope of giving me some relief.
I would sit for hours at a time in front of
the fireplace hoping for daybreak. The
pain was so intense I was almost crazy
in fact, I would lose my reason for hours
at a time. One day a friend of mine dropped
in to see me. No more had she glanced at
my foot than she exclaimed; ‘Mrs. Finnegan,
why in the world don’t you try the Cuti-
cura Remedies!’ Being disgusted with the
doctors and their medicines, and not being
able to sleep at all, I decided to give the
Cuticura Soap and Cuticura Ointment a
trial. After using them three days that
night I slept as sound as a silver dollar
for eight long hours. I awoke in the morning
with but very little pain, in fact, I thought
I was in heaven. After using the Cuticura
Remedies for three months I was perfectly
restored to health, thanks to the Cuticura
Soap and Ointment. I will be sixty-four
years of age my next birthday, hale and
hearty at present.” (Signed) Mrs. Julia Finne-
gan, 2234 Hebert St., St. Louis. Mo., U. S. A.,
March 7,1911.
Sold everywhere. Send to R. Towns & Co.,
Dept. 20K, Sydney, N. S. W., for liberal
sample of Cuticura Ointment, post-free.

The Inky Way.
One of the most nerve-racking jobs on earth is
that of the literary critic whose knowledge of litera-
ture is limited. There is one of him on the World’s
News, and he runs a column headed: —
“Will you tell me if I can write poetry?”
The other day a leg-puller submitted to him Adam
Lindsay Gordon’s “To My Sister.” The critic gave
the. poem plain Sheol. After about two sticks of
acrid comment he quoted the well-known lines: —
“Oh, time glides ever quickly by!
Destroying- all that’s dear.
On earth there’s little worth a sigh,
And nothing worth a tear.
What fears have I? What hope in life?
What joys can I command ?
A few short years of toil and strife,
In a strange and distant land.”
: and wound up this way:—
“Why you should call this an ‘Englishman’s’
lament it is -not easy to discover. We do not believe
the typical Englishman comes here because he must,
or that being here he gives way to despondency as
you do. Still less do we believe that he takes to
putting his opinions into such an apology for poetry.
.Your effort is a hotch-potch of borrowed ideas, not
at all well put together, and if you are wise you will
not try to relieve your ‘few short years of toil and
strife’ with verse-making.”
it is language that a respectable paper would hesitate
to use concerning a ballad by George Reid.
* * * *
“T.H.”: How these bards and bardesses do fancy
themselves! Here’s Ella Wheeler Wilcox advertising
a new fifteen-shilling edition of her “Poems of
Pleasure.and Passion.” Three-quarters of a pound!
Does the lady think she is selling herself? Or does
she fancy we are all heirs of Benjamin Guggenheim ?
“Bleeds” Fitehett’s stock has had a bad slump.
In the June Cornhill he made much ado about a
“mystery” of Waterloo—a scaffold or platform from
which Napoleon is supposed to have surveyed the
progress of the battle. Fitchett seemed to think he
was. discoverer and patentee of this “mystery,” and
loftily inquired whether any other writer on Water-
loo had. mentioned it. Whereupo-n various London
papers instantly fell upon him, and remarked acidly
chat there were very few that hadn’t. Sir Walter
Scott visited Waterloo two months after the battle,
and as long ago as that explained that the alleged
scaffold “was built or erected some months befbre,
for the purpose of trigonometrical survey of the
country, by the King of the Netherlands.”
The English Labor party’s first daily newspaper
will appear on London streets on October 8, under
the name of The Daily Citizen. Frank Dilnot,
who was for long Parliamentary Special for the
Daily Mail, will edit, and A. G. AVright, for many
..years publisher of The Times, will manage the new
concern.
Britain also has its trade union amongst press-
men, and the old prejudice against it is rapidly being
scared away, mostly by the wolf at the door. For
nstance, the National Union of Journalists, estab-
I'shed at Manchester six years ago, has a member-
ship of 2312 this year, and it is calculated that
V per cent, of the lot receive less than £l6O a year.
"he roll has increased by about 500 in two years.
“Scribe”: This is the brief story of the reporter
who has just sold his share in the paper he made,
and pocketed £150,000 for it. His name is Jones —
Kennedy Jones, better known to the London press
gang as K.J. He was a reporter when rather more
than 20 years ago he went from Birmingham to a
small job on the Morning, a London halfnonnv dailv.
Soon he was news editor. Next he was chief reporter
on O’Connor’s evening paper, the Sun. The Even-
ing News, another paper, was rapidly descending into
bankruptcy. Jones took it up with Harmsworth’s
capital, and promptly turned a big loss into a profit
of £40,000 a year. That made him solid with
Harmsworth,. who put up the money when K.J. in-
vented the Daily Mail ; and it was for his share in
this paper that the young man has just collected the
nice, dry bit mentioned above.
“Mr. Oeky”: After the slobber which is usually
to be found in the daily press anent the first-night
nerformance of a plav, whether good or bad, it is
like a glass of beer in a desert to come across the
following in a recent issue of a Victorian provincial
“ ‘NOBODY’S DAUGHTER’
was produced at His Majesty’s Theatre last night.
AVe do not like the play.”
That was all. As far as I could see there was no-
thing much wrong with the play, but I admire the
exceptional straightness of this notice, and think
it might well be borne in mind by alleged critics
on some bigger papers.
The police authorities of Victoria are
such a humorless lot that lately, having
accepted as a recruit a man who had not
long since served a sentence for assault and
robbery, and trained him up to the stage at
which he was lit to go on duty, they in-
continently fired him out because he was
recognised by a casual inspector. That officer
spotted him reporting for duty at a suburban
police station, and took him aside and spoke
quietly with him, so that he departed peace-
ably and without fuss. But he afterwards
repented; for a day or two later he turned
up again and demanded his uniform and
baton so that he might go forth and keep
the peace. This time his dismissal was less
courteous than peremptory; which was a pity
in some, ways, because he might have been
an acquisition to the force. An organisation
that can’t recognise convicted thieves when
it already has their photos isn’t likely to
arrest others whom it may not know from
a bar of soap, whereas the novice, having in-
ternal knowledge of Pentridge, would recog-
nise ex-inmates at a glance. Of course, he
might not arrest them, but it is absurd to
expect everything of a policeman.
THE PRIVILEGED PAUPER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There is no privileged class in England or Aus-
tralia, except the pauper class.— Argus.
How foolish, how fantastic are
The notions that the wealthy man,
When lolling in a motor-car,
Is privileged above his clan.
Harmswoi'th with full experience knows
The poor are privileged alone,
Who need not wear the best of clo’s,
Nor sport a single precious stone
To lure the wretch to murder prone.
The pauper’s privilege is great;
He has no social duties, so
If Royalty receives in state,
How blest is he —he need not go !
If Croesus, say, should give a feast
To last till 10 p.m. to-day,
The pauper need not be a beast,
And eat like mad in that affray—
The happy wretch can stay away !
When Duchesses give crowded balls,
The pauper’s free to sit outside.
The opera, afternoons, and calls
His precious time do not divide.
The starveling can enjoy a bone —
He’s always hungry, mostly dry—
And if of life he’s wear}' grown,
There’s all outside in which to die,
A privilege no cash would buy!
VICTORIA.

THE NEW FEATURES OF THIS REGISTER.
Nine separate totals in pounds, shillings and
pence of different kinds of transactions and of the sales of different
assistants, in addition to grand total of all cash sales.
Amount of each cash sale made by each assistant is
automatically added into a separate total. Every time a sale is made
the amount is added to the total previously shown for that assistant.
Every penny received for cash sales is recorded twice by
this register—on the adding wheels of the assistant making the sale
and on the “secret” or grand total adder, A printed record giving full
particulars is made at the lime time.
Amount in pounds, shillings and pence received in payment of
accounts is shown on another set of adding wheels. This obviates the
necessity of going through dockets or, cash book to find how much money
has been received on account—the total is ready at all times.
Total value of all goods sold on credit is recorded separately. A
glance will show the amount of credit business for the day or to date
from any stated time. This record enables the shopkeeper to see at once
what his total turnover has been.
Every penny paid out is added to previous total amount of
disbursements.
Think of the advantages of having all these separate totals and of
being able to obtain such valuable information by merely turning a key
and glancing at the records.

ARE YOU
DEAF?
If you are deaf, or going deaf, you will be glad
to hear of a simple and scientific device which will
restore your hearing, no matter how old you are.
There is no mystery or secrecy about it; we describe
it fully and explain just how it acts. .
You use spectacles as sight magnifiers, and in the
same way you may use this sound magnifier as an
aid to weak hearing. But, unlike spectacles, the
Wilson Common-sense Ear-Drums are invisible and
comfortable. You can’t tell you are wearing them.
No one else can, either, because they are not seen.
They rest the ear nerves by taking the strain on
them—the strain of trying to hear dim sounds.
What a blessed relief to have done with that
tiring strain! „ ~
Being made of the purest and softest Para rubber
they cannot be felt, even when the head rests on a
pillow. For the same reason, a blow on the side ot
the head will not hurt the ear. In fact, they pro-
tect the ear, the tender inner parts that are so
senstive, from cold winds, dust, sudden and piercing
sounds. . . .....
So that you may clearly understand why these
facts are true, and why the Sound Magnifier restores
the hearing, we give an illustration of one as it lies
in the ear. You will notice that the Sound Magni-
V
flex- is shaped
like a trumpet,
and so con-
veys even the
faintest sounds
right to the
natural ear-
drum. Being
of the softest
rubber, it can-
not hurt the
ear. It fits the
orifice of the
ear with preci-
sion and com-
fort. The di-
vision that
runs across the
mouth is to al-
low the remo-
val and inser-
tion of the
Ear-Drum, and
to brace it.
The shape
of the Wilson
Ear-Drum has
been chosen
after years of
experience a s
the best adapt-
ed to carry the faintest sound
natural ear-drum.
right
down
to
the
To persons who are nearly deaf, it is not only
trying to have people shout at them, but it hurts
the ear-drum. When age comes the ear-drum some-
times gets thickened, or loses its tightness, and
shouting does not act properly on it. It only
makes a confused and tiring noise. But the
Sound Magnifiers concentrate the sound in the best
spot for perfect hearing.
Of course, if you have lost the hearing-nerve
completely, or were bom deaf, we cannot pretend
to cure you. Only a miracle could do that, in spite
of some of the absurd claims made to-day. But if
.you can hear the faintest sound, do not despair.
You will never regret trying the Wilson Ear-Drums.
We have letters to show you from deaf people in
Australia and New Zealand, who have been cured,
and you can be cured, too. You may wear the Ear-
Drums day and night, and take them out whenever
you wish. Children can wear them (we have spe-
cial sizes for children from six to twelve). Even
a child can insert and remove them without the
least danger.
The price is £1 (the fee for a doctor’s single
visit). This includes a pair of drums (they
are only sold in pairs), a fine nickel remover and
also an inserter. After the first pair is bought you
may purchase a single drum at any time fos Bs.,
but the pair you get at first will last quite

RUGBY UNION FOOTBALL COMPETITION.
NEWTOWN RUGBY UNION FOOTBALL TEAM
Champions and Unbeaten, 1910 and 1911.
The Rugby Union Season, ended Saturday, the 17th September, 1910, Newtown having gone
through unbeaten. They are accordingly unconquered premiers, an honor which has not fallen
to any other club since the district system was introduced ten years ago.
It will, therefore, be interesting to the general public, as it was gratifying to the
proprietors of REXONA, to read the following testimonial, given unanimously by every
member of the team. The letter speaks for itself, and shows in what high esteem this premier
team holds the new Rapid Healer REXONA. They write as follows
The Manager, Rexona, Sydney. 1910 LETTER.
Dear Sir, —The whole of our football team are so pleased with the benefit we have derived
from the use of REXONA, the Rapid Healer, that we have unanimously decided to give you a
testimonial regarding same. We have used it for numerous purposes in our training, and
after our matches, both as an embrocation and as a healer for the various cuts and sores
incidental to football.
This has been a most successful year with us, and we certainly feel to a considerable extent
that we owe our success to the great benefit which we have derived from the use of REXONA,
the Rapid Healer.
A number of the boys have also used it for Chilblains, Sore Feet, and Chapped Hands, and
in every instance have found it to be an excellent remedy.
With best wishes for REXONA, We are, very truly yours,
(Sgd .) ERNEST E. BOOTH,
Also signed by the other Members of the Team. Capt., Newtown R.U. Football Team.
1911 LETTER.
Dear Sir, —During the last year we have again been training with your famous REXONA,
and again have we come out victorious in the Rugby Union Football Competition. REXONA
is certainly a grand Ointment, and we think more of it than ever, and it is our intention to
continue to train with this Ointment for the coming year, and hope to again win the pre-
miership. ( Signed ) S. TI. SLATER.
Football players everywhere will find REXONA of thp greatest use in the training-room.
It is invaluable as a rub-down for strained and tired muscles, and will rapidly heal all the
many cuts and bruises acquired in the football field. If you have not already tried REXONA,
send us your name and address, and we will send by return post a sample pot. gratis.
Rexona is sold everywhere in triangular pots at 1/6, or in large pots, holding nearly four
times the mmntity, at 3/-, or will be sent post free, upon receipt of the price, by the
REXONA PROPRIETARY COMPANY, 15 O’Connell Street, Sydney.

The S.S. Merry-go-Round.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The W. T. Appleton is a dredge, and not built for
long voyages, but she was constructed at Rotterdam
for the Melbourne Harbor Trust, and consequently
had to cross the ocean... There were periods of ex-
citement, and many changes in the crew.... “This
packet of ours was like a half-tide rock... She would
not steer to it —I mean every time a sea hit us she
would turn her back to it. . .The vessel rolled her
rails under ; in fact, there were times when the boats
dipped and the sidelights went under...We bumped
into the sea like one wall hitting another.” — Few ex-
tracts from evening, paper, concerning our latest
floating importation.
f INHERE is a savage land far, far away
from old Rotterdam’s workshops and
sedges;
It is twelve thousand miles round the watery
globe, and it’s fond of importing its
dredges.
There the savages sit by the shallows, and
write to the centres of civilisation,
For the punts and the buckets that scoop
up the sand from the doors of eacn
wretched out-station ;
There the headman skims round in a dug-
out canoe, while the war-drums are
throbbing a chorus,
And the lone gospel-bringer just gobbles
quinine, and gets next to a plaster that’s
porous;
There the arroAV that’s poisoned flies out of
the bush when the shadows of even are
falling,
And the sad exiled trader looks over the sea,
and remarks that “the Homeland is
calling.”
There the witch-doctor dances and curses
the tribe, while the holy man’s converts
are hymning,
While the slow alligators slip into the stream
and wait round for a dog to go swim-
ming.
Then the forest primeval looms up in the
dusk, and its shadows are,black on the
river.
“Oh! thou Land of Great Darkness!” the
holy man sobs, and retires to his hut
with a shiver.
Then the dangerous night like a great curtain
falls on the jungle, and all its wild
natives,
They who howl by the shores, of Port Phillip
and buy the good work of the Dutch
operatives.
What must they think in the Rotterdam
yards of the lone island labelled “Aus-
tralia”?
Do they think it an outpost that sleeps up
a creek when not holding a black
saturnalia?
Does the mariner dream when he’s wallowing
south of a tangle of huts or a city?
Does he think of black Venuses crowned
with red blooms? If he does, it’s a
shame and a pity.
For if we have induced him to leave his
Dutch home by our lunatic craze fox-
importing,
And to risk making orphans and widows as
Avell, it is time that the churches Were
snorting.
But the first blast of rage from the people
should come xvho aspire to stand up with
the nations,
For it isn’t much use talking big if some
fools, say a dredge marks our small
limitations. -
But good-luck to the submai-ine Merry-Go-
Round and the various people who
“sailed” her,
To the stokers who juggled with water and
coal, and the various people who bailed
her.
But we trust she’s the last of the foreign-
made scoops to splash out in this
blighted direction,
For there is an old legend this land believes
in and has a fair share of Protection.
n.s.w.
E. FRANCIS.
Dan’s Wedding.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
OLD Geoffrey Millar had passed over
four years ago, and left behind him a
large, profitable, going farm, in a
well-watered district, Gippsiana way, in
three equal shares, to his wife and two sons
—so long as they all remained unmarried
and lived together on the share-and-share-
alike principle. But the first one that married
was to lose all interest in the property, and
it was then to be divided fairly between the
remaining two.
It cost him a lot of trouble, but when he
had worked it out at that, he thought he
had hit on a good plan for keeping the
precious property he’d struggled, labc red
and sweated over intact, and he died con-
tentedly leaving his wife and the boys to
work it out.
“Go on, now, you don’t mean it,” *said
Mrs. Millar, a fair, placid, little woman,
giggling.
“I do, every word. I’ll say it again—
you’re the handsomest Avoman around,”
and then old Ben Potter subsided heavily
int~ a chair and wiped his face.
“Isn’t he a flatterer?” she laughed to Ned
Adams, who came in with the kettle filled.
Both admirers were about 60, but Ned,
being thin and wiry, could show his adora-
tion in works.
“Umph !” he said shortly at the sight of
Ben. “I’ll be off.”
“Law, no! You’ll stay and have a cup of
tea.. The boys’ll be in soon —and I just want
a bit of wood,” she hinted sweetly. His face
cleared, and he went to get some.
“This is the most comfortable kitchen
around for miles,” remarked Ben ponder-
ously.
“Well, now T , your kitchen isn’t so bad,” she
laughed.
“But it’s empty, Mrs. Millar, it’s
empty”
She went on laughing and setting the
table, while the two men glared, each .won-
dering why on earth the other didn’t clear
out They were both small farmers in the
neighborhood, and it was well known that
they were after widow Millar, in spite of her
husband’s will.
Presently the boys came in. Dan first,
tall, broad-shouldered and good-looking. his
face smooth, except for a little dark down
over his lip. He was the younger, and his
mother’s darling. lie caught her by the
waist, and whirled her around the room until
she was breathless and panting.
“Ah! Mother, caught again! And where’s
my tea?”
“Dan,” she said with dignity. “Remem-
ber I’m your mother.”
“Which is it to be, Mum?” he whispered.
“Remember, I’ll be best man”
“Get away now,” she said, as red as fire.
“Tea’s ready.”
John came in as the teapot was filled. He
was tall, too, but fair, straight and proper —
a model, sensible young man in every way.
He looked grave when he saw the visitors,
and gazed down his nose as he ate stolidly,
or else read an improving book that lay
beside his plate.
“Dan,” he said, as he rose from the table,
“we’ll manage the milking. You’d better
ride to Burke’s and see about that hay. It’ll
be moonlight.”
Dan flushed delightedly, and mumbled
“All right!” But his mother looked worried,
and a hard glitter came into her eyes.
The party scattered soon after that; Old
Ben and Ned, who had to attend to their
milking, went part of the way with Dan.
Mrs. Millar was left to clear up, but she
was put out and cleaned up with a lot ol
unnecessary clatter.
After a while, John came in to go to bed.
“Mother,” he said, coldly, “do you think
it’s wise, having those chaps around here so
much?”
“Look here, John,” she said, move firmly
than she spoke most times, “you seem to
forget I’m your mother, and quite able to
take care of myself.” '
“It’s only that I want to remind you about
the will.”
“About the will,” she said scornfully. “A
lot you care about us and the will, when you
keep sending Dan on errands to Burke’s, and
know all the time Sis Allen lives on the
main road. You’re just putting temptation
in his way.”
“He’s old enough to look after himself.”
John turned away abruptly, but there was
a grim smile of satisfaction on his face.
Mrs. Millar sat thinking in the big rocker
on the verandah, her brow puckered into an
anxious frown. She waited up for Dan, and
by-and-bye he came home and flung himself
down on the verandah at her feet.
“Mother,” he said, flushed and joyous-
looking. “I’ve done it. Sis and I are to be
married in three weeks.” His mother sighed
helplessly. “What’s the use of waiting?
Bother the property! Let it go to the
deuce. You will be right, anyhow, and I
can work for what Sis and I want. Some-
oody’s got to make a beginning, and John
won’t, if we wait a thousand years, so I
will. We can’t always go living on here
like this —so wish me good luck, motherie
dear,” and with tears in her eyes she kissed
him, and hoped he’d be happy.
She was unusually thougliful all next
day and evening, and startled John by com-
ing to his bedside that night with a candle
in her hand and a big pink shawl around her
head and shoulders, to ask him which he
thought was the best man of the two —Ben
Potter or Ned Adams. He gave his vote for
Ned Adams because he could get about best,
but thought the matter was - hardly worth
disturbing him in the middle of the night
for.
“Perhaps not,” she said gravely, and he
smiled his peculiar smile as she went out
with the candle.
She crept softly into Dan’s room, but
didn’t wake him, and stood shading the light
from his eyes as she looked down lovingly
on him, and then went sadly out.
Next evening, when the boys came home,
their tea was waiting for them; but the
mother had vanished and left a note, saying
that, after all, she’d decided to take Ben
Potter, and by the time they got the note
she would be married, and she hoped they’d
forgive her, but she knew that now they
could get their shares of the money all right,
and do just what they liked in the future,
ana Dan could arrange for his wedding as
soon as he liked. She’d be home in three
weeks, and she did hope they’d all be happy.
Dan went around in a tearing rage.
“I just believe you’ve had a- hand in this,”
he said to his brother. “Can’t you see she’s
just done it for our sakes?”
“Yours, you mean;” said John. “But,
anyhow, she might have done worse—Ben
Potter’s not quite a beggar”
“And if he were, she should have half of
everything I have,” said Dan warmly.
“Well, I won’t have Ben mooning around
my place. I never could stand him,” and
he commenced to plan the division of the
property.
Two or three days went wearily away,
and then Dan began to make excursions to
Burke’s, and John smiled to himself as he
noticed.
At last, Dan announced that he didn’t see
why he should postpone his wedding any
longer. It would take place at once, and if
John agreed, they could have a kick-up in
the big kitchen the night after, and mother
would be sure to be home by then.
John agreed with alacrity. It seemed a
good thing to make sure that somebody was
married.
So it came to pass, and Dan’s wedding-
party provided gossip enough for the neigh-
borhood for the next six years.
The bride, a pretty, shy bush girl, with
dark brown hair and slim little figure, and
her troop of sisters, got everything ready,
had a big bake, and turned the place into a
perfect paradise with green stuff.
The guests came by shoals in carts, traps
and on horseback, laughing and talking, and
soon there were two accordions and a fiddle
to make them merrier.
But the mother hadn’t appeared, and Dan
was a little out of spirits about it.
“I wish Mum was here, Sis,” he said,
catching at his little wife’s hand once as she
passed near him.
“We’ll make it up to her, Dan, and she
can always live with us, whoever she’s
married, if she wants to. But Dan, I’m
glad John hasn’t got to be there, too. I
don’t like him, he makes me feel creepy—he
does, true !” She broke off laughing. “Come
on, they’ll be wondering where we are.”
Just before supper, Dan, in the midst of
a waltz, saw through the dust his mother
standing in the doorway.

“Mother!” he said, dropping his partner
abruptly. The dancing stopped, and there
was a chorus of exclamations and welcomes.
Sissy was in her arms, trying to explain
everything in a breath, and scolding her for
not coming home before.
“So you’ve come back, mother?” said
John’s clear, cold voice above the din.
The mother looked weary and tired.
“Yes, John, I’ve come back.”
“Where's your husband?” whispered
Dnn eagerly.
“My husband!” she said in the silence
that followed. “I’m not married at all,
Dan.”
John laughed scornfully, Dan looked
stunned for a minute, but Sis slipped her
hand in his and smiled up into his face.
“In too much of a hurry, weren’t you,
Dan?” said John, aggravatingly.
Dan drew his wife to him.
“I don’t think so,” he said sturdily. “We’ll
manage, somehow.”
“Well. Dan, I fully intended getting
married,” said his mother. “Only as Ben
and I were going to the church at Forest
Creek, we came upon John’s wife.”
“What!” thundered John.
“What? John’s wife!” said Dan and Sis
in a breath. “John’s wife. Oh, Johnny !”
“John’s wife,” went on the mother, firmly.
“She was in a lonely house with no help
near, and had just got a fine boy, so I
stayed and nursed her through.”
“I have no wife,” said John, going close
to his mother, “you meddling old”
Dan rushed him away. “Keep your tem-
per, old man.”
“As it happens,” said John, viciously,
“there was a sort of marriage, but it was
done under a false name, so it isn’t legal”—
“So I thought at first,” said the mother.
“But I’ve had vood advice, and that’s all
right. So now, by father’s will, as you
married first, you don’t get anything, and
it all belongs to Dan and me. I’m going
to give my share to your wife and child, and
you can own them or not, as you please.
Dan will have his share, and as for me, I
think, after all, I’ll marry Ned Adams.
Ben Potter’s a bit too slow.’
R. A. KENT.
The Last Prayer of Stumpy
Wiggins.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
STUMPY WIGGINS was the saddler of
Wilga Overflow, a dusty little village
on a western stock route. Most of
his trade was with the surrounding stations
and drovers in charge of travelling stock;
and, as Stumpy was a master of his craft,
he was independent of the residents. Con-
sequently, when a very unusual outbreak of
religion struck the Overflow, Stumpy took
no pains to disguise his contempt for the
whole business, and could afford to do so.
The leader of the “revival” was Zachariah
Angwin, a new-comer, who was a shoe-
maker, and who also ran a fancy goods
depot—for shoes last a long time around the
Overflow. * Zachariah was a Primitive
Methodist, full to the brim of religious fer-
vor ; and it was not long before he had most
of his neighbors just as strong on prayer as
he was himself.
The one fly in the ointment was the scoff-
ing of Stumpy, who mocked and derided
Zachariah and his whole following. There
was, as yet, no church at the Overflow, but
the small public hall was rented, and gradu-
ally an enthusiastic little congregation was
gathered together. A feature of the affair
was the Wednesday evening prayer meeting,
a.t which weird testimonies of blighted
lives that had been regenerated, were given.
All aspirants for full membership had to
pass through the prayer-meeting stage, and
several attempts were made to coax Stumpy
to attend “just for once.”
But Stumpy repelled all these appeals
with ignominy, and drove the enemy to a
fresh line of attack. Thenceforward, for
several weeks, prayer was offered for his
conversion. At first the allusions to Stumpy
were made in a guarded manner, and he
was described as “a son of Belial,” an
“erring wanderer,” and so forth; but one
evening the blacksmith broke the ice of con-
vention by alluding to “our undersized
brother in the flesh,” and from that time the
prayers contained full particulars.
It was some time before Stumpy got to
know that he was being publicly prayed for.
One evening, as he was working in his gar-
den, Evans, the butcher, who taught the in-
fant class at the Sunday-school, stopped to
have a word with him. After feeling his
way carefully, the butcher remarked that
they looked forward some day to “having
the pleasure of Mr. Wiggins’s company at
the prayer meetings.”
“I only hope there are no more droughts
till then,” was the discouraging reply.
“At any rate,” continued the butcher,
“we’re prayin’ for you.”
“What?” snapped Stumpy; “praying for
me?”
“Yes. We pray for you every Wednesday
night.”
“Well, of all the damned impudent”
The butcher held up his hands with a
shocked and pained expression.
“Why,” snorted Stumpy, “I’ll have you
stopped. I’ll get one of these mandamuses
or caveats and block you.”
“You can’t,” triumphantly retorted the
butcher. “Even if you could stop the public
prayers, you can’t stop the private prayers
—and the prayer of a righteous man avail-
eth much. Why,” he continued, as a bril-
liant inspiration occurred to him, “I’m
prayin’ for you this very minute.” The
prayer, however, was violently interrupted
by a clod which sent the butcher’s hat fly-
ing, and its owner beat a hasty retreat.
The new development upset Stumpy con-
siderably. He found it difficult to deal with
it effectively. At last, one Wednesday
morning, .the Overflow was electrified by
the news that Stumpy was to take part in
the prayer meeting that evening. He had
confessed the error of his ways to the
blacksmith, and, at the forge, the blessed
tidings was discussed with great fervor.
The attendance at the prayer meeting was
a record one. All the residents were there;
and b -ndary riders and station hands who
had come in to Wilga on business remained
to attend. Several drovers and tank-
sinkers, who refused to believe the miracle
until they should actually hear Stumpy’s
confession, came in, seating themselves un-
easily in the back of the hall. After the
usual preliminaries, Zachariah, who was
“class-leader,” called on Brother —he was
indeed pleased that he could use the word—
Brother Wiggins for prayer. Stumpy, who
was in the very front seat, got slowly to his
feet, and, in a clear, high-pitched voice began.
The prayer opened with an exhortation
to the Lord to put manliness, fairness and
real goodness into the hearts of all present,
and this fairly safe generalisation was
backed up with loud “Amens” from both
men and women. Then Stumpy warmed to
his work and continued: “And dower us
plentifully—for Thou knowes't we need it—
with the gift of honesty and straightforward-
ness. Banish all hypocrisy and double-
dealing and twofacedness. Let not Thy
people, while claiming to he peculiarly good,
do deeds that a blackfellow would be
ashamed of.” (Silence, save for a solitary
“Amen” from the still unsuspicious smith.)
“As Thou dost apparently require persons
to be specially named for treatment, do
Thou mercifully visit Brother Anderson,
and lead him to see the wickedness of bring-
ing home mutton and other goods from
Toola station on Saturday nights for the
use of his own household.” (Dead silence,
except for the anxious shuffling of Brother
Anderson’s feet on the floor.) “And let
Brother Atkins know that, although no
human eye sees him when he sprinkles
water .on his chaff, to make it weigh heavier,
there is One Above who is a witness of it.”
(A suppressed snigger from a drover who
had that morning bought chaff from Atkins. 1
Watch over Thy chosen vessel, Brother
Hopkins, and strengthen him against the
temptation to use second-hand, perished
iron for horse-shoes. Grant, if it be Thy
will, that the dough-scales of Brother Han-
nan shall, ere long, be rightly adjusted, that
Ihy people here may get full-weight bread.”
03 Sl l enc c> save for nervous coughs and
shuffling of feet.). “And give our sisters”
( O Lord, he’s giving them a turn!” in a
loud whisper, from the back) “give them
strength and inclination to abandon scandal-
ising and gossiping, that they may then do
better the work which cometh to their hands
m their own houses. Then,” concluded
Stumpy, lapsing into modern phraseology,
we 11 have a chance of getting peace and
quietness in this God-forsaken place.”
As .he finished, with the sweat in beads
on his forehead, Stumpy walked steadily
down the aisle of the hall and out of the
door, followed by the group of tank-sinkers
and drovers. Nobody else in the meeting
moved until the blacksmith slowly rose, and,
with crimson face, stalked out without a
word being said. The rest followed, and
slipped away quietly to their homes.
Stumpy has not prayed since.
F. R.
A Song of Considerable Gladness.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
IN the bush I sit a-dreaming ,
Of the wonder-lights of town;
Happy pavements lush and streaming
With blown feather, lace and gown ;
Thronging caf6s full of laughter,
Tramcars roaring through the night,
Mirrored jewels trailing after
Steamers laden with delight.
In the town I fill with longing
To be done with dust and row,
To exchange the pavements thronging
For green meadows and a cow -;
There to see the broad sun sinking
Low behind the twisted trees,
When the yellow day is drinking
Scented evening in her breeze.
Flies the shuttle of my fancy
To and fro across the 100m —■
In the bush the eyes of Nancy
Set the dullest ways abloom;
In the town, for all the glitter
And the dust, and talk, and guile,
It is hard to feel too bitter
Face to face with Mary’s smile.
“Home, sweet home,” they love to sing us
Till we weary of the tune
Tender moral thoughts they bring us—
Platitudes as grey as June.
Give me, give me bright December
Either in the bush or street
With some girls that I remember
And some more I want to meet.
Flies the shuttle of my fancy
To and fro across the loom—
Mary, Mabel, Jane and Nancy
Set my dullest thoughts abloom.
Give me, give me stout and oysters,
Or a crayfish full of dreams —
Dreams of girls in forest cloisters
Eating pink and frosted creams.
Let me feast on prawns of vision
When the murky hour is late,
Till the empty shells the dish on
Bring me memories of Kate,
Till across the darkened reaches
Of the wild and scented night
Mermaids of the brown surf beaches
Shoot the waves of my delight.
Or amid the wonder-roaming
Of my sleep-unfettered mind
I may see the taxis homing
When the streets are hushed and blind ;
Or, perchance, about my shoulder
I may feel my Mabel’s clasp,
And arising to enfold her
Some policeman’s tunic grasp.
Rides the moon above the valley
Where the sliprails whiten hoar,
And the evening shadows rally
Round the dim and ghostly door;
Something sweet and white a-flutter
Fills the dusk with joy again,
And a hint of cream and butter
Stirs my heart with love of Jane.
Flies the shuttle of my fancy
To and fro across the 100m —
Mabel, Mary, Kate and Nancy
Set the dullest ways a-bloom;
Give me, give me stout and oysters,
Bid the crayfish round me curl,
Till in street or forest cloister
All the world is glad with girl.
N.S.W.
